==PAGE:5==
- V -
ABSTRACT 
 
Due to the increasing development in the construction of tall buildings in Denmark, there is a rising need for 
advancing within the determination of load effects on high-rise buildings. The design of tall, slender structures 
prompts some extensive challenges for the structural engineers, due to the complex dynamic problems for low 
frequency constructions affected by wind loading. The prevalent codes and standards do not fully comprise the 
wind load effects on tall structures, why wind tunnel testing often is necessary for these complex cases. The 
challenge with wind tunnel testing is however, that the techniques are not widely understood by the structural 
designers requesting the results, why there is a need for improving the understanding within this field.  
The main focus of the present master thesis is to investigate the translation from the complex dynamic load effects 
on a 350 m high-rise building affected by severe wind loading, to the static floor-by-floor loadings interpretable for 
a structural designer. 
 
The investigation is conducted for two different configurations, a single high-rise building and a twin arrangement 
of high-rise buildings, to study the influence of nearby buildings. The wind load process for both configurations is 
obtained by ‘High-frequency surface pressure measurements at discrete points’ on a scaled model in a wind tunnel 
test.  
The thesis contains a thorough exposition of the theory behind the wind phenomena and load effects occurring at 
high-rise buildings for a full comprehension of the static and dynamic load contributions of the total wind load 
effect. In connection, a detailed description of the resulting floor-by-floor loadings is included with a deliberation 
of the applied methods. 
The floor-by-floor loadings are presented in a simple form by means of a table containing 82 point loads acting on 
each floor in three directions, including each of the two transverse forces and the rotational moment. The table is 
represented in connection with 24 appertaining load combinations, accounting for the interaction between the 
three forces.  
From the determination of the final results, it is found that the dynamic load contribution from the resonant effect 
of the structure has extensive influence, where the load distribution has its peaks at the concentrated masses due to 
the increase of the acceleration caused by vortex shedding in the cross-wind directions. The assessment of the two 
test configurations shows that due to a channelling effect, an increment in the peak forces occur for the twin 
arrangement at certain wind directions, entailing a necessity for conducting a combined examination of both cases 
when presenting the peak loadings.    
 
As the rapid expansion within computer modelling enables simulations of wind flow around buildings by means 
of Computation Fluid Dynamics (CFD), it is almost inevitable not to discuss the subject in relation to wind tunnel 
testing. CFD has proven potential within the assessment of wind load effects on buildings, why the thesis contains 
a comparison of a CFD model executed as an exact replica of the wind tunnel, to investigate the similarities between 
the two simulations. The CFD simulation is carried out as a RANS turbulence model, why only mean values are 
extractable, which cannot be compared to the peak loadings determined in the wind tunnel test. However, the final 
comparison showed remarkable similarity when comparing the streamlines of the wind phenomena with an oil 
painting model tested in the wind tunnel, whereas the mean surface pressures deviate significantly in magnitude 
from the mean pressures from the wind tunnel test, entailing that the actual mean wind loading is not obtainable 
for a RANS model.

==PAGE:7==
- VII -
PREFACE 
 
This master thesis has been composed during the spring of 2015 at Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical 
University of Denmark. The thesis is conducted in cooperation with FORCE Technology, department of Hydro and 
Aerodynamics, as collaboration institute for experimental work.  
The thesis focuses on the determination of the static and dynamic response of a 350 m high-rise building under 
severe wind loading. The time-dependent wind load process is simulated and recorded in the boundary-layer wind 
tunnel at FORCE Technology. It is the intention that the thesis shall convey a comprehensive outline of the wind-
induced structural loads, useful and applicable for a structural designer. 
Two main aspects are investigated in order to compare the unfavourable and beneficial effects of two different test 
configurations:  
 
Test configuration 1: Single high-rise building 
 
Test configuration 2: Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings  
The authors have in this connection each focused in one of the above main aspects, where test configuration 1 
belongs to Camilla Greth Jørgensen, and test configuration 2 belongs to Marie Thordal-Christensen. The main 
theory and calculation routines have been conducted in collaboration between the two authors in order to retain a 
cohesive and comprehensive analysis of the given subject. The thesis is equivalent to 32.5 ETCS points corresponding 
to a work period of 5.5 months. 
 
The motivation and interest within the wind-engineering field already gained ground among the authors during 
the DTU course ‘Seismic and Wind Engineering’ in 2013, where a rising interest in the wind impacts on high-rise 
buildings continued throughout the master education in civil engineering with the choice of related courses in the 
matter. The course ‘Computational Fluid Dynamics for Buildings’ enhanced the interest for optimisation of wind 
tunnel testing, which on balance with former knowledge formed the basis of the main focuses. The special course 
‘Introduction to Wind Tunnel Testing in Civil Engineering’ has been the last launch pad for priming the authors 
for the present thesis.

- IX -
ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS 
 
During the project period, the authors owe gratitude to several individuals who has contributed to the achievements 
and completion of the present thesis.  
Firstly, the authors will start by thanking their supervisor, H. Holger H. Koss, for his invaluable knowledge, 
intellectual curiosity and inputs throughout the project.  
Furthermore, the authors are grateful that FORCE technology has put their testing facilities at disposal, where it 
moreover should be mentioned that the experimental work in the wind tunnel would not have been possible to 
conduct, without technical assistance and guidance of several exceptionally helpful individuals at FORCE. 
Lastly, the authors would like to thank Rambøll Copenhagen for putting their computer resources at disposal for 
several CFD simulations, where a special and grateful thanks shall go to Jens Christian Bennetsen, for his helpful 
assistance and for making his time for varies discussions and simulations to the authors’ advantage.

==PAGE:19==
- 3 -
INTRODUCTION
The rapid expansion of high-rise buildings around the world has raised interest in the optimisation and
development of more prevalent techniques for determination of the wind-induced load effects for tall, slender
structures. The development and implementing of high-rise buildings are naturally more prominent in the
imposing metropolises, where the expansion still has not quite gained its foothold in Denmark yet. However, due
to the still growing urbanisation, the growth of inhabitants and businesses needs to be accommodated, why there
is a rising need for constructing upwards. Therefore, the development of tall buildings has slowly expanded on
the market, where recent initiatives as the development of ‘Carlsberg byen’ can be mentioned. This initiative
contains the construction of the 100 m ‘Bohrs Tower”, already in progress, and the further formation of eight
towers more to come.
The design of tall slender buildings does however prompt some extensive challenges for the civil engineers due to
the increased wind loading and the complex dynamic problems for low frequency constructions. These wind
load effects are not fully encompassed by the prevalent codes and standards, why wind tunnel testing has
become a broad used technique to specify these load effects. The challenge with using wind tunnel testing is that
the techniques are not widely understood by the designers using the results, why there is a rising need for
improving the understanding within this field. Therefore, the scope of the present thesis will be to increase the
knowledge of the wind tunnel testing process, providing an in-depth analysis of the wind load effects on a high-
rise building, useful for a structural designer.
The main structure of a high-rise building must be designed to safely withstand the extreme wind effects the
building will be subjected to during its expected lifetime, why the importance within determining the load effect
that encompass all wind phenomena occurring for tall, slender structures, is essential. The wind phenomena
encountered when reviewing high-rise buildings are the effect of vortex shedding, inducing building motion due
to crosswind excitations and aerodynamic instability, which both attribute to increased loading and occupant
discomfort. Furthermore, the aerodynamic interaction between adjacent buildings is almost an indispensable
factor as well, as the surrounding nature for the present building might vary significantly over the years due to
the rapid development within the city centres, giving rise to complexity in the flow variation. Therefor the in-
depth analysis of the wind load effects is investigated for both a single high-rise building and a twin
arrangement of high-rise buildings with a distance of one building width, comparing the unfavourable or
beneficial effects of the two different configurations.
The investigation of the two configurations is done by analysis of a time-dependent wind load process, simulated
and recorded in the boundary-layer wind tunnel at FORCE Technology. The wind load process is obtained by
‘High-frequency surface pressure measurements at discrete points’, which is a known wind tunnel testing
technique that provides direct information about the pressure distribution on all surfaces of the test model.
The primary model under investigation is a scaled model of a square-sectioned high-rise building of 350 m’s
height and with a footprint of approximately 59 x 59 m. The geometrical scale is 1/575, which implies that the
pressure model used for the measurements is a plexiglas model with dimensions of 102 x 102 x 610 mm,
instrumented with 216 pressure taps distributed at all surfaces to record the wind loading at any instant in time.
The practical basis of the testing technique is an analysis of the time-dependent wind load process, which can be
used to determine the wind-induced mean and dynamic loads occurring under any given wind conditions, where
these loads are combined with the full-scale dynamic properties to determine the wind-induced responses of the
structure. These responses are translated into usable data, which directly can be delivered to a structural designer.
==PAGE:20==
Introduction
- 4 -
The Council of Tall Buildings and Urban Habitat (CTBUH) has developed a concise guide for wind tunnel testing
of high-rise building for the non-specialist, discussing the main areas of interest when conducting a test. This
guide has been used as an inspirational source of what to provide to the structural designer, where the main
points include: wind climate models, wind velocity and turbulence profiles, aerodynamic data, wind-induced
loads and responses, and loads from different return periods.
However, as the pressure distribution is available on the surfaces of each model from the two test configurations,
it is interesting and almost essential to validate the results with in-depth processing of the distribution compared
with knowledge from theory and codes, why this comparison is used to determine the level of accuracy of the
test measurement.
Furthermore, when dealing with wind tunnel testing, it is almost inevitable not to cover the subject of
Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD), as the rapid expansion within computer modelling “threatens” to
outperform the physical testing of a scaled model with the ‘numerical wind tunnel’. The numerical computer
simulation is however not fully developed yet when dealing with the assessment of wind-induced structural
loads, but quite advanced in the assessment of ground level wind speeds and interpretation of streamlines.
Therefore, a CFD simulation is conducted as an exact replica of the investigated wind tunnel and test
configurations, to see the similarities of the pressure distribution on the surfaces. In addition, an oil painting test
is performed in the wind tunnel, creating streamlines on the surfaces, enabling the study of the different wind
phenomena, which can be directly compared to the streamlines in the CFD model.
The main focus of the experimental work is to outline the resulting load effects consisting of base forces and
floor-by-floor loadings used for structural design. However, the determination of a trustworthy output requires
theoretical knowledge, useful test results, computational skills and verification of the data, which all is outlined
in the present thesis, divided into the 6 chapters of following content:

Chapter 1 deals with the design of the full-scale structure, accounting for the structural properties and
analysis including determination of the dynamic properties.

Chapter 2 contains all theoretical knowledge of wind phenomena required for an analysis of the flow
conditions arising from the wind tunnel test. Furthermore an investigation of the prevalent codes and
recommendations are determined for assessment of wind profile and load effects.

Chapter 3 is a practical exposition of the conduction of the wind tunnel tests, containing both
background and perspective for all tests, model design and technical procedure and setup with a full
description of the equipment and the adjustment procedure of the measured data.

Chapter 4 comprises the theoretical knowledge used for processing the wind tunnel data obtained in
Chapter 3 into the wind-induced responses of the structure, including a validation of analysis in the
time domain versus the frequency domain.

Chapter 5 encloses all results, where the wind phenomena are compared to theory described in Chapter
2, and the analysis of the wind tunnel data is conducted on the basis of the theory described in Chapter
5. Furthermore the chapter contains a full exposition of all calculation routines, enabling the reader to
reproduce the same results. The analysis of the results is separated for each of the two configurations,
being the single high-rise building and the twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings.

Chapter 6 is a separated section including theory, specification of setup and results from a full replica of
the wind tunnel test configuration in a Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) simulation. The CFD
model is used as comparison to the flow phenomena obtained by the oil painting model and as
evaluation of the comparability between wind tunnel tests and CFD simulations.
==PAGE:21==
- 5 -
Chapter 1
1 DESIGN OF HIGH-RISE BUILDING
The present chapter is divided into three main sections, where the first section contents a brief introduction of
design methods of high-rise buildings. In the second section the investigated high-rise building is introduced,
where the design and structural properties of the high-rise building are described. The third section contents a
thorough exposition of a modal analysis for both an analytical model and a discretised finite element modelling
(FEM) model, where the section is completed in a result section of the modal properties of the high-rise building.
1.1 Description of high-rise buildings
The design of tall buildings has developed quite significantly during the last centuries, and even more rapidly in
the past decades, where the term Supertall and Megatall structures have been introduced. The construction
methods for a tall building are not a simple matter, and the growing urge for developing construction methods
to be able to construct even taller structures has led to complex systems inducing more structural stiffness and
strength. In Figure 1.1, the design of the interior system possible at different height levels is illustrated, where it is
evident that the complexity rises with the increase of story numbers.
A broadly used interior system for the construction of new tall structures is the Outrigger system, as this
structural system significantly increases the stiffness of the structure and has a potential to be applicable for
high-rise buildings of 150 stories or more, as portrayed rightmost at the figure.
Figure 1.1: Interior structures (Ali & Moon, 2007)
The Outrigger structure typically consists of a stabilizing central core and super-columns located in the façade
near the outermost corners, where the two stabilizing elements are connected by means of outriggers designed as
rigid horizontal structures to ensure interaction between the core and columns, improving the overturning
stiffness and strength of the building. As seen on Figure 1.2, the outriggers will act as stiff arms interacting with
the columns when the building core tilts, which induces a force couple in the columns and thereby reducing the
moment acting on the core with up to 40% compared to a ‘free cantilever’ structure. (Choi, Ho, Joseph, & Mathias,
2012)
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 6 -
Figure 1.2: Core-supported outrigger system (Choi, Ho, Joseph, & Mathias, 2012)
According to CTBUH (Council of Tall Buildings and Urban Habitat) up to 73% of the tall buildings studied in the
2000s adopted a core + outrigger structural system. This is due to the fact of both the very high increased
stiffness, but also because it is a very efficient and cost-effective way to reduce building accelerations. (Tall
Buildings in Numbers, 2010)
Due to the above interpreted advantages of the core + outrigger system, it seems feasible to adopt the system to
the structural design of the studied high-rise building. The structural system of the construction is of great
importance for the determination of the dynamic quantities, why it is essential to consider a somewhat realistic
system. Due to lack of in-depth knowledge of the structural features when constructing a high-rise building,
varies sources have been used for estimations and comparison of the designed structure. It should however be
noted, that the scope of the thesis does not contain a full analysis of the structural system, why the design is
merely a proposal for a reasonable feasible construction of a high-rise building.
Inspiration in the current model design has been found from Beijing Supertall (RWDI, 2010) in a simplified
matter. Furthermore, the structural properties have been adjusted to correspond with practical knowledge of
high-rise constructions according to various codes and papers.
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 7 -
1.2 Structural Properties
Figure 1.3 a) portrays a typical floor plan of the 350 m high-rise building. Only the stabilizing vertical members
are accounted for, why the horizontal members are disregarded and only included in the load of the structure.
The elevation of the high-rise building is seen in Figure 1.3 b).
The high-rise building consists of a total number of 82 stories, including a 12 m high entrance hall at the ground
floor and four outriggers distributed throughout the height. The representative stories are of 4 m’s height and
the outriggers are located at a 12th, 37th, 62nd and 81st story. The outriggers have a varying floor height ranging
from 6.7 m up to 9.1 m at the top floor. Each outrigger is assumed constructed as one coherent rigid storey made
by braced steel frames, which connects the super-columns to the central concrete core.
The floor plan consists of 24 concrete/composite columns optimized throughout the height of the high-rise for
vertical load-bearing, 4 super-columns, a central stabilizing core and a glass façade. The super-columns are
distributed symmetrical around the central core at the outermost corners to maximize the force-couple and
thereby the benefit of the compound system.
Figure 1.3: a) Typical floor plan of the 350 m high-rise model, b) Design of the high-rise with the outrigger system
The outriggers connect the super-columns and central core, enabling the stabilizing members to be considered as
a compound cross section, why the overall stiffness of the structure is evaluated for the compound cross section,
instead of only including the cross section of the concrete core. It is clearly seen why a core + outrigger system is
desirable, as the moment of inertia is almost four doubled when adding the stiffness contribution from the
super-columns. However, since the super-columns and concrete core only can be considered fully rigid in the
outrigger construction, a flexibility in the span in between must be accounted for, why the total moment of
inertia is reduced with a certain percentage. The reducing percentage is chosen as 30%, so the eigenfrequency of
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 8 -
the total structure corresponds to a reasonable value compared to other Supertall structures constructed by same
technique and overall material, such as Beijing Supertall (RWDI, 2010).
The geometry and structural properties for the stabilizing parts are shown in Table 1.1. The values are used later
on for the modal analysis and are merely rough estimates. References are made to Appendix 1.1 and 1.2 for
determination of geometry and structural properties.

1.3 Modal analysis
Dynamic loading is depended on the assessed structure and covers either man-made vibrations due to
pedestrians, machinery, etc. or nature induced vibrations due to wind loading or earthquake excitations. When
assessing high-rise buildings, the dynamic loading due to nature is the most severe and common as this can
cause vibration in the entire structure, why the structural dynamic properties and behaviour of the structure as a
whole is important and a modal analysis essential.
The system considered for the high-rise building is linear-elastic, which can be substantiated by the fact that the
criteria for determining the capacity of the structure is related to a serviceability issue, rather than a safety issue.
This is the difference between determining the structural capacity for the possibility of an earthquake compared
to a wind-induced load case. In the event of an earthquake, the structure is merely designed to withstand the
load, why the structural members are allowed to reach a state of plasticity. The structural capacity for wind-
induced load is related to the vibration of the structure caused by the peak accelerations at the top of the
building, where the allowable peak accelerations are defined based on the threshold value for human comfort.
This means that the structural members never will reach a state of plasticity, as the threshold value simply is too
low compared to the actual load capacity of the structure.
When considering a modal analysis for the high-rise building, different approaches can be used. The most
comprehensive way to analyse the dynamic properties, would be to sectionalize the structure in total consistence
with the interior design of the actual building, but as this would entail nearly an infinite number of degrees of
freedom that requires unreasonable computational demand if constructed in a FEM-program, a simplified
method is desirable. Two overall methods are available, an analytical interpretation of the system and a FEM-
analysis for a discretized system, respectively.
The analytical way to find the dynamic properties for the high-rise building is to look at the structure as a
continuous system, where the model has an infinite number of modes and can vibrate with infinitely high
frequencies. However, to make the system interpretable, the high-rise building is narrowed down to a simplified
homogenous beam model, clamped in one end. This simplification has its advantages as the formulas can be
derived analytically, but does not do justice to how the actual structural system of the building works, why it is
desirable to discretize to an approximation of the real system, where the structural members that contributes to
the overall structural behaviour are taken into account.
However, the method of how to derive the dynamic properties for either a continuous system or discrete system,
originate from the same theoretical knowledge, why a representation of the Euler-Bernoulli beam theory in
relation to the equation of motion is used as the origin to the analysis of both systems.
In total three different models have been used for investigation of the dynamic properties; an analytical beam
model, a simplified stick FEM-model where the core + outrigger system is used as a compound system, and an
advanced FEM-model where the core + outrigger system is used as a separated system. In Figure 1.4 the three
models are shown.
The analytical model is used as a simplified model to interpret the dynamic properties, where the fundamental
frequency is meant to be in consistence with the more comprehensive FEM-model to enforce that the discrete
system is coupled correctly. The stick FEM-model is used for investigation of the torsional frequency of the
system, whereas the advanced FEM-model is used for the full analysis of the high-rise building.
The analytical beam model, represented in a), is studied as a one-dimensional compound system with uniformly
distributed mass and stiffness. The stick FEM-model, represented in b), is a discrete system with compound cross
section and is only used to investigate the torsional forces, resulting in a system with nine degrees of freedom.
The last model represented in c), is studied as an advanced frame FEM-model to make the core + outrigger
system of the high-rise building more realistic in relation to how it actually works. The frame FEM-model is
fitted with three degrees of freedom in each node. The super-columns are implemented as trusses and the
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 10 -
remaining parts as beam elements to realise the properties of the core + outrigger system, where the rigid
outriggers merely are transferring axial load to the super-columns and moment to the core.
Figure 1.4: a) One-dimensional system, b) Stick FEM-model, c) FEM-model of core + outrigger system
The dynamic properties when considering both a continuous and a discrete system are found by assessing the
equation of transverse motion, which governs a solution from which an eigenvalue problem occurs. Solving this
eigenvalue problem leads to the dynamic properties describing each mode, being the mode shape and angular
frequency.
The equation of motion can be obtained by considering the forces necessary to produce transverse vibration
ݓ(ݔ, ݐ) of a beam subjected to an external dynamic force ݂(ݔ, ݐ), hence considering a static equilibrium of the
forces acting on an infinitesimal beam element.

the bending moment, where ݀ݔis assumed very small, hence

If the equation is isolated so that the left side only depends on t and the right side only depends on ݔ, it is
possible to get two ordinary differential equations that relates to either ݐ or ݔ. To be valid for all values of ݔ and ݐ
the two expressions must be a constant, which is denoted ߱ଶ, this leads to:
The equation governing the time function
ݍሷ(ݐ) + ߱ଶݍ(ݐ) = 0
( 1.7 )
The equation governing the spatial function
[ܧܫ(ݔ) ߶(ݔ)ᇱᇱ]ᇱᇱെ߱ଶ݉(ݔ) ߶(ݔ) = 0
( 1.8 )
The time function is solved by applying initial conditions, whereas the spatial function is solved by applying
boundary conditions. As the high-rise building is considered uniform in terms of mass ݉ and stiffness ܧܫ, the
function of ݔ, when considering ݉(ݔ) and ܧܫ(ݔ), is constant, which leads to a simpler expression for the spatial
function.
ܧܫ߶(ݔ)ᇱᇱᇱᇱെ߱ଶ݉߶(ݔ) = 0
( 1.9 )

߶(ݔ)ԢԢԢԢ െߚସ߶(ݔ) = 0
( 1.11 )

1.3.2 Discrete system
In the analysis of the discrete model, the dynamic properties are derived by solving the equation of motion
( 1.2 ), described by means of matrices.
The absolute discretized equation of motion of a dynamic system is given as:
ࡹ࢝ሷ(ݐ) + ࡯࢝ሶ(ݐ) + ࡷ࢝(ݐ) = ࢌ(ݐ)
( 1.16 )
Where M is the mass matrix, C is the damping matrix, K is the stiffness matrix,݂(ݐ) is the external force
dependent on time and ݓ(ݐ) is the displacement vector dependent on time. The first term in ( 1.16 ) is
representing the inertia force, the second term is representing the viscous damping of the system and the third
term is the stiffness contribution of elastic forces.
However, as described in Section 1.3, the natural frequency and mode shapes of a dynamic system is always
analysed for free vibration, where the equation of motion is reduced to a homogeneous equation, with no
external load, and no contribution of damping, as given in ( 1.17 )
ࡹ࢝ሷ(ݐ) + ࡷ࢝(ݐ) = 0
( 1.17 )
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 14 -
A solution to the equation of motion is assumed as a harmonic equation with a constant shape, given as:
࢝(ݐ) = ࢶ݁௜࣓௧
( 1.18 )
Where ߔis the mode shapes and ࣓ is the angular frequencies of the system. The solution to the second term is a
known complex solution.
By substituting the harmonic solution ݓ(ݐ) into the equation of motion, the generalized eigenvalue problem is
found as:
ࢶ(ࡷെ࣓ଶࡹ) = 0
( 1.19 )
To satisfy the generalized eigenvalue problem, one of the terms in the equation has to be equal to zero. Though, a
nontrivial solution of ࢶ is only obtained if the matrix is singular, given that ࣓ଶis an eigenvalue of the problem
with a diagonal matrix. This means that the determinant of the system matrix is zero, why the characteristic
equation is derived as:
݀݁ݐ|ࡷെ࣓ଶࡹ| = 0
( 1.20 )
The eigenvalue problem is solved by equation ( 1.20 ), or with the build-in Matlab function, where the solution
will be j numbers of natural frequencies with j numbers of corresponding mode shapes. The total number of
solutions is dependent on the size of the stiffness and mass matrices.
The modal mass and modal stiffness are obtained by orthogonality conditions and are given as:
݉௝= ࣘ࢐

The natural frequencies of the system are also determined by the modal mass and modal stiffness with
Rayleigh’s quotient. The equation is a scalar and is equivalent with a single degree of freedom system.

1.3.2.1
Modal representation
The equation of motion can be expressed as modal representation, where the equation is decoupled into modes,
where each of the uncoupled systems is treated as a modal response problem and can be compared to a single
degree of freedom system. By introducing the modal components in each mode, which are used to obtain the full
response of the system, the modal equation of motion can be derived.
The displacement response of the global system is expressed by the mode shape matrix ࢶand the modal
components ࢘(ݐ) and is given as:

1.3.2.2
Damping
The damping properties of a structure is very difficult to define and are associated with a lot of uncertainties, as
the damping collaboration between the different elements in the structure only can be determined entirely
accurate by experiment on the final structure. Therefor the damping ratios, used for analysis of a linear system
with classical damping, are often based on available data from prior experiments and expert opinions.
Furthermore it can often be necessary to calculate with a set of different values to account for different scenarios.
In the following, recommended values from Dynamics of structures (Krenk, 2013) are used for determining
Rayleigh damping as the procedure for constructing a classical damping matrix.
Damping of a system is described as the energy dissipation, and is represented as the damping matrix C in the
equation of motion, given in ( 1.16 ). The damping can both be determined as a modal damping, where the
damping ratio is unchanged for each mode, or by stiffness and mass proportional damping, which is changed
with the frequencies of the modes. The Rayleigh damping is a combination of the stiffness- and mass damping
and is frequently used as it less conservative compared to the modal damping.
The Rayleigh damping matrix is given as:
࡯= ߙ௖ࡹ+ ߚ௖ࡷ
( 1.29 )
Where ߙ௖ and ߚ௖are damping parameters.
By remembering the modal damping ࡯כ matrix in ( 1.25 ) is a diagonal matrix, which contains the modal
damping for each mode, the damping ratios in the decomposed systems can be found, since each decoupled
system is reflecting a single degree of freedom system in each mode. The uncoupled modal damping in mode j is
given as:
ܿ௝= ߙ௖݉௝+ ߚ௖݇௝
( 1.30 )
The angular frequency ߱௝ and damping ratio ߦ௝ given ( 1.28 ) are substituted into the uncoupled damping matrix
to define the damping ratio:
2ߦ௝= ߙ௖
߱௝
+ ߚ௖߱௝
( 1.31 )
It is seen the damping ratios are changing in relation to the frequency of the mode. The equation shows the mass
proportional damping term is inversely proportional to the angular frequency and the stiffness proportional
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 16 -
damping term is proportional to the angular frequency, resulting in a damping ratio dominated by the mass for
the low frequencies, and by the stiffness for the high frequencies.
The angular frequency and the damping ratio is passing through minimum values, which is defined by the
damping parameters ߙ௖ and ߚ௖:
ߦ௠௜௡= ඥߙ௖ߚ௖
( 1.32 )
߱௠௜௡= ඨߙ௖
ߚ௖
( 1.33 )
A good estimation of the minimum damping ratio and minimum frequency ratio is ߦ௠௜௡= 0.01 and
߱௠௜௡= ξ߱ଵ߱ଶ. (Krenk, 2013).
1.3.2.3
Finite element modelling
In the following a short exposition of how the matrices described in the prior section are used and derived for the
discrete system.
Local FEM matrices
The local coordinate system of an element is an essential factor in the assembling of the local matrices, as it is
important to identify the direction of all degrees of freedom to couple the system correctly. The forces and
deformations of a beam element are seen in Figure 1.7. When dealing with a truss element, the rotational
deformation and rotation is neglected, since a truss only can consume axial forces.
Figure 1.7: Definition of forces and deformations of an element
The local stiffness and local mass matrices of an element are derived from the theorem of virtual work, where
the external work is equal to the internal work. By defining the deformations as the virtual work, the forces of
the system are found. In the following the unit displacements and rotations are denoted ݑ and ߠ, where the
accelerations are denoted ݑሷ and ߠሷ.

Global FEM matrices
By combining the local matrices for each element, the global matrices of a system are obtained. The global
matrices are found by the direct method, where each node in the local element matrix is applied to the position
of the nodes in the global system in the global matrix. It is highly important to change the coordinates of the
local matrices to the global coordinate of the global system, when the local matrices are combined.
1.3.3 Modal properties
In the following, an elucidation of the modal properties is made, accounting for both the analytical and
discretized system. As prior described, the analytical system is merely a simplified beam model, where the actual
coupling between the super-columns, designed only for axial load, and the core, designed only for moment, is not
taking into account. However, as the fundamental mode of the high-rise model is the swaying mode, only the
core as a compound system with the super columns will have an actual impact on the frequency of the first
mode. Therefor the analytical model can be used to validate for the advanced frame FEM-model to substantiate
that the members are coupled correctly, as the fundamental mode should be reasonable identical for both
systems.
1.3.3.1
Analytical model
The analytical model is the easiest and fastest way to account for the modal properties of the high-rise building.
The analytical model, seen in Figure 1.4 a), is used to tune the mass- and stiffness properties so the frequencies of
the structural modes are in reasonable consistence with existing theoretical knowledge of comparable high-rise
buildings. The overall structural properties are then used to interpret further on the actual modal properties by
the FEM-model.
The modal properties for the analytical model are derived for a homogenous beam, with uniformly distributed
mass, clamped at the base. The eigenfrequencies of the system for the first four mode shapes are seen in Figure 1.9
with the appertaining frequencies, and the determinations are found in Appendix 1.1.
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 20 -
Figure 1.9: The first four modes of the analytical model
Concluding remarks on the frequencies and mode shapes are done in comparison with the FEM model in the
ensuing section.
1.3.3.2
FEM model of the high-rise building with core-outrigger system
The finite element model of the core-outrigger system is conducted to give a more genuine solution of the core-
outrigger system, with sectionalised values for the core, outriggers and super columns. Since the high-rise
building is symmetric, the frame FEM model is used to define the transverse modes of the system in both the x-
and y-direction. The frame FEM-model consists of 28 nodes and 33 elements, as seen in Figure 1.4 c).
The stiffness- and mass matrices of each element are created by combining the local transverse and axial
matrices, to generate local matrices with six degrees of freedom. The beam elements are containing values in all
six degrees of freedom, where the truss elements only are containing values in the axial and transverse degrees of
freedom, leaving two rows and columns of zeros in the local matrices.
The modal analysis of the FEM-model is executed in Appendix 1.3 by defining the local stiffness- and mass
matrices for the system in Appendix 1.4-1.7, followed by collecting the local matrices to a global stiffness matrix
and a global mass matrix, where each node of the local matrices are inserted into the global node system. The
global coordinate system is defined as: [՛
՚
ր], and the local coordinate systems of the elements are
corrected to fit the global coordinate system. The resulting global stiffness matrix ࡷand mass matrix ࡹare
containing 75 degrees of freedom, where the degrees of freedom are seen in Figure 1.4 c), which entails 75 modes
determined in the modal analysis of free vibration. The resulting four first modes are plotted by Appendix 1.8
and seen in Figure 1.10 with the appertaining eigenfrequencies.
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 1 – Design of high-rise building
- 21 -
Figure 1.10: The first four modes of the core outrigger FEM model
From the results, it is seen, that the fundamental mode of the structure is in consistence with the fundamental
mode of the analytical system with a frequency on 0.14 Hz, which indicate that the system is coupled correctly
in the global matrices. However, the following modes have slightly higher frequencies for the analytical model
compared to the FEM model, which gives an indication of, that the influence from the super columns are
occurring at the subsequent modes. As the analytical system is a simplified model of the high-rise building
analysed with a compound cross section, it entails that the super columns and core are fully connected by the
outriggers at all heights. Although a reduction of the full moment of inertia is implemented to account for the
flexibility between the four outriggers, it is still a quite far-fetched solution to how the actual system works, why
the FEM-model provides results, which are more reliable. Furthermore, it is important to design for the modes
with the lowest possible frequencies, why the frequencies from the analytical model gives misleading results
from the second mode and onwards.
1.3.3.3
FEM-model of the high-rise building, stick model
The stick FEM model, shown in Figure 1.4 b), is used to obtain the torsional modes of the high-rise building. The
model consists of nine elements and nine rotational degrees of freedom. The FEM-model is executed in Appendix
1.9 by the same method as the FEM-model of core-outrigger system, where the local stiffness- and mass matrices
for each elements is determined in Appendix 1.10 and 1.11, whereupon the global matrices are determined by
adding each node from the local matrices to the global matrices in Appendix 1.12. The resulting global stiffness
matrix ࡷ࢚and mass matrix ࡹ࢚are containing 9 degrees of freedom, which entail nine modes are obtained in the
modal analysis of free vibration. The first four frequencies for the torsional modes are given in Table 1.3.

1.3.3.4
Rayleigh damping
The Rayleigh damping are determined in Appendix 1.3 and illustrated in Figure 1.11 for the frame FEM-model in
the x- and y-direction. The figure is showing the mass proportional damping and stiffness proportional damping
separately, and in correlation as the Rayleigh damping. It is easily seen the mass proportional damping induces
high damping ratios at the low frequencies, and the stiffness proportional damping is generating high damping
ratios at the high frequencies.
From the Rayleigh damping curve, marked by red, the damping ratios for each mode are found by plotting for
the appertaining frequencies, marked by stars, as seen on Figure 1.11 and given in Table 1.4. The contribution
from the mass and stiffness proportional damping is shown by ߦ௠ and ߦ௞, where it is seen the mass proportional
damping is dominating for the lower frequencies, and the stiffness proportional damping is dominating for the
higher frequencies. Furthermore it is seen, that the damping ratios for the first two frequencies for the Rayleigh
damping are equal, which is a result of the estimation of the minimum ratio of the frequency, given in ( 1.33 ).
The torsional Rayleigh damping is likewise determined for each mode in Appendix 1.9, dependent on the
torsional frequencies, which are shown in Table 1.5.

Chapter 2
2 WIND LOADING ON STRUCTURES
Wind is a natural phenomenon that is created by the changes in the atmospheric pressures and temperatures in
the atmosphere. The motions of the air are creating stochastic wind processes, where the region close the surface
of the earth is affected of friction from the surface, called the atmospheric boundary layer. Structures are affected
of this atmospheric boundary layer, which induces several wind phenomena around the structures and pressures
on its surfaces, which are interesting for engineers when structures are designed.
In this chapter an introduction is given to determine the wind velocity and the forces it is inducing, by means of
theories, earlier experiments, codes and recommendations. Furthermore the calculation routines are compared to
investigate the deviations, and to give an understanding of how the wind loading is acting on high-rise buildings.
At last a short introduction of which fundamental parameters that are important when performing a wind
tunnel test will be given.
2.1 Theory of wind phenomena
2.1.1 Atmospheric boundary layer
Since the atmospheric boundary is affected by friction, the height of the layer is changing with the roughness of
the surface, entailing a smaller height of the boundary layer at smooth sea terrain and higher at high-density
housing. The height where the friction of the boundary layer does not longer affect the wind speed substantially,
is referred to as the gradient height, and can extend up to around 1 km (Holmes, 2004). Above the gradient
height, the wind speed is assumed constant which is denoted as the geostrophic or gradient wind speed. Due to
friction, strong turbulence is created near the earth’s surface, being prevailing at the bottom of the boundary
layer and decreasing with height.
The outline of an atmospheric boundary layer is shown in Figure 2.1, where the vortices/eddies and gusts, created
by the wind, are creating a stochastic wind process.
Figure 2.1: Outline of atmospheric boundary layer
The mean of the stochastic wind process is referred to as the mean wind speed or mean velocity profile ܷഥ(ݖ), and
can be expressed as a logarithmic function. The fluctuation of the wind process is measured as the standard
deviation which is used to determine the turbulence intensity ܫ௨(ݖ), expressed as a measure of the gusts and
peaks. The size of the eddies is expressed as the integral length scale ܮ௨௫, and is a measure of the mean velocity of
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 24 -
an eddy over time. The lowest part of the mean wind profile is affected of strong turbulence and do not follow a
logarithmic curve until a certain height, which is introduced as the roughness length z0 and is a measure of the
roughness of the surface.
In Europe the basic wind velocity is a measure of 10 minutes mean wind speed, measured at relatively open areas
at 10 m height, regardless of wind direction or time of year, with a mean return period of 50 years. The basic
wind velocity is used to determine the resulting pressures by means of codes, or to scale experimental data
measured in wind tunnel tests.
Wind engineers have derived equations to reflect the atmospheric boundary layer profile’s slope. The mean wind
speed profile is expressed in two ways, in terms of the Logarithmic law and the Power law (Holmes, 2004). The
Logarithmic law is the most accurate since it is based on theoretical and mathematical expressions for
homogenous terrains. The Power law is not grounded on a theoretical basis, but is easier solved than the
Logarithmic Law due to mathematical difficulties, thus the Power law is often used to express the wind profile.
Both laws are based on the roughness length. In Table 2.1 four terrain categories are given by (Holmes, 2004),
where each terrain category are extended over a range of roughness lengths, which corresponds to the roughness
of the earth’s surface.
The mean wind speed profile, expressed by the Logarithmic law, is given as:
ܷഥ(ݖ) = ݑכ

ݑכ is the friction velocity, which is not a physical velocity, but dependent of the shear stresses of the earth’s
surfaces and the density of the air.
It is convenient to see the normalized wind profile to study the gradient of the profile. The unitless wind profile
is found by ( 2.2 )

The turbulence intensity is a measure of the fluctuation of the atmospheric boundary layer, thus it is expressed
by statistical parameters of the wind profile:
ܫ௨(ݖ) = ߪ௨(ݖ)

By experiments it is found that the standard deviation near the ground is close to 2.5ݑכ, from where the
turbulence intensity is given by (Holmes, 2004) as:
ܫ௨(ݖ) =
1
݈݊ቀݖ

( 2.7 )
A turbulent flow consists of an infinity amount of vortices with different sizes. It is expected that the vortices are
travelling in the wind direction, thus the size of one vortex is measured with respect to its local coordinate
system (x, y and z) and its flow components (u, v and w). The size of the vortices is measured by the periods of
the vortices, which are transformed into frequencies and are represented in a spectral density function. A spectral
density reflects the energy of the vortices, where the low frequencies are reflected as the bigger vortices, and the
high frequencies as the small vortices.
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 26 -
The size of the vortices in the wind direction is represented as the integral length scale ܮ௨௫, as seen in Figure 2.1,
and is given as the mean wind speed of an eddy multiplied its period.

parameter:

The mean wind speed profile and the turbulence intensity, determined by the Logarithmic law and Power law,
and the turbulence intensity, is illustrated in Figure 2.3 for intermediate roughness lengths of the four terrain
categories found in Table 2.1. It is seen the Logarithmic and Power law are following each other closely for low

Figure 2.3: Mean wind speed profile and turbulence intensity determined by (Holmes, 2004)
The mean velocity profile and turbulence intensity is described in several codes and standards, where the
procedures of Eurocode and Architectural Institute of Japan (AIJ) are processed in the present chapter. The
determination of the wind profile in the European standard is based on the Logarithmic law and roughness
lengths. It extends up to 200 m and has a certain limitation when determining wind loads on high-rise buildings.
The wind profile determined by AIJ extends up to 650 m, making it more suitable for investigation of wind loads
on high-rise buildings. It is based on the Power law, where exponential parameters and gradient heights are given
for the terrain categories.
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 27 -
2.1.2 Wind loading on structures
The wind flow around an obstacle is dependent on the shape of the obstacle, where the shapes are categorised in
three groups; streamlined bodies, quasi-streamlined bodies and bluff bodies. The streamlined bodies are
characterised for attached flows, where the separation point is occurring at the wake of the body. The quasi-
streamlined bodies are characterised as bodies where the flow is attached until the widest part of the cross-
section, where separation bubbles are created at the wake. Bluff bodies are characterised as bodies that disturb the
flow, meaning the separation point is placed before the maximum part of the cross section and large separation
bubbles are created at the wake. The outlines of horizontal and vertical streamlines around a surface-mounted
bluff body are shown in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: a) Outline of vertical streamlines around a bluff body, b) Outline of horizontal streamlines around a bluff body
The separation point of the wind flow is seen at the front of the obstacle at both the vertical and horizontal
outline. The vortices are created at the wake of the obstacle, where the magnitudes of the vortices are dependent
on the Reynolds number.
Beranek has studied the wind flow around cubes by means of wind tunnel tests (Beranek, 1984). The different
phenomena of streamlines in a turbulent flow around a typical high-rise building are shown in Figure 2.5, where
the numbers are referring to a specific phenomenon, seen in the text description.
The airflow above the building top is given as number 1, where the airflow around the building sides are given as
number 2 in the front and number 9 at the wake. The stagnation point at the windward wall on the building is
seen as number 14, where the horizontally and vertically streamlines are separated, shown as number 3 and 4.
The downwash of the building facade is indicated as number 5, which starts at the separation point and enters
into the horseshoe-vortex at the front bottom of the building, which is shown as number 6. From the horseshoe-
vortex the vortices speed up around the corners, seen as phenomenon number 8, and followed into small fast
vortices in a line backward as number 16. The recirculation of vortices at the wake of the building is seen as
number 13.
At the ground in front of the building, number 7 is seen as the stagnation point, where the streamlines are
separated. The stagnation point at the ground of the back of the building is seen as number 11, where the
reversed airflow (number 10) and airflow at the back of the building (number 12) are separated.
Number 15 is not visible in the figure, but is defined as the upward air stream at the wake of the building.

Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures

The streamlines are creating pressure forces and friction forces at the surfaces of an obstacle. The pressure forces
are acting as pressure and suction normal to the surfaces, where the friction forces are acting parallel to the
surfaces. The pressure distribution at the surfaces, induced by the wind phenomena represented in Figure 2.5, is
separated into zones, which are seen in Figure 2.7.
Figure 2.7: Flow field around a floor-mounted cube exposed to turbulent boundary-layer flow, Cook, 1990.
(Koss, Wind Engineering - Part 8: Simulation)
When the wind flow around twin arrangements of buildings are studied, the most common phenomena which
are observed are shielding and tunnelling effects. The shielding effects occur when the principal building is
standing in the shelter of the twin building, where only a small pressure occurs at the principal windward façade.
The tunnelling effects occurs when the two buildings are parallel to each other, where a large amplification of the
wind effects on the side facades are observed, as the wind is led through the spacing of the buildings, where the
magnitude of the amplification is increased with the decrease in spacing of the buildings. (Yu, Xie, Zhu, & Gu,
2015)
The wind flow around two staggered buildings are shown in Figure 2.8, where the wind flow is led through the
spacing of the buildings, where the rearmost building becomes unsheltered and an amplification of the pressure
are created at the windward wall, which are inducing suction at the front buildings leeward façade.
Further wind phenomena around twin arrangement of buildings are difficult to explain in general, as the
phenomena are dependent on the specific design of the arrangement, why several studies of specific cases have
been executed.
Figure 2.8: Flow field around two staggered buildings (Bennett)
Several studies of the pressure changes at the surfaces, and how the pressure distribution is expected to shape on
a high-rise building, have been executed through the years by many researchers by means of different wind
tunnel tests. In the following the main points of the studies of high-rise buildings conducted by Holmes and
ESDU are collected, together with a study of twin arrangements performed by Yu, Xie, Zhu and Gu, to give an
understanding of the different phenomena of streamlines and pressures the wind flow is creating. The studies are
used to compare with the averaged values of pressure coefficients and pressure distributions from Eurocode.
The study, Wind Loading on Structures by John D. Holmes, is based on knowledge from wind tunnel studies and
full-scale experiments from a wide range of researchers (Holmes, 2004). The Engineering Science Data Unit
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 30 -
(ESDU) is a technical engineering knowledge base that provides guidelines for design methods within a wide
range of engineering disciplines. The content is based on validation from committees consisting of leading
experts from industry and academia. (ESDU, About Us: ESDU, 2015).
The study of the twin arrangements of high-rise buildings is provided by (Yu, Xie, Zhu, & Gu, 2015), where an
interference building of different widths and heights are investigated for pressure distribution on the surfaces
and the interference effects.
In the following Holmes is used to explain the pressure distribution on the different surfaces in relation to a flow
from a rough boundary layer, both with mean, peak and minimum values, whereas the study of ESDU is used to
describe the pressure distribution for a uniform flow. Furthermore ESDU is used to clarify how the pressure
coefficients and drag coefficients change in relation to the angle of attack.
The local pressures on a face of a model can be used directly for design of cladding on a wall, but is at the same
time very interesting to explore in relation to the stagnation point of the pressure and separation of the flow. The
stagnation point of the windward face is typically at 70-80% of the total height for a high-rise building, below
which a strong downwash occurs. At the sidewalls high local pressures ensues after separation and reattachment
of the flow, whereas negative pressures of lower magnitude and less fluctuation follow at the rear side.
2.1.2.1
Holmes
The surface pressure on a body is expressed as a unitless pressure coefficient ܥ୮, where distinction between the
mean, the root-mean-square fluctuation, maximum and minimum values is made. The different pressure
coefficients are expressed by ( 2.9 ) in the abovementioned sequence.

refers to the specified pressure coefficient.
The mean pressure distribution from a turbulent boundary layer is marked in Figure 2.9 on a tall prism with
square cross section, comparable to a high-rise model. The aspect ratio of the model is 8, which mean that the
maximum pressure occurs at 85% of the total height as a result of the vertical wind profile on the windward face.
Figure 2.9: Mean pressure coefficients on a tall prism in turbulent boundary layer flow, Baines, 1963, (Holmes, 2004)
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 31 -
The local peak pressures do however have significantly relevance as well, why comparison of the variation in
mean, maximum and minimum is interesting. A wind tunnel test was made for a model representing a building
of 85 m height affected by a boundary layer flow corresponding to suburban terrain. The magnitude of the
mean- and peak pressure coefficients is visually shown in Table 2.3. It is observed that the mean coefficients do
not strongly diverge from the mean values from the tall prism in Figure 2.9, why both studies are considered.
Table 2.3: Mean, minimum and maximum pressure coefficients.

From the figures it is seen that the stagnation points differ in height when assessing the mean and peak values
on the windward wall, placed at around 80% height for the mean value, and increasing and decreasing with the
peak maximum and minimum values, respectively. Furthermore, the distribution of the minimum values on
the sidewall makes it apparent how the flow is separating on the front wall creating vortices that induce negative
pressures on the side- and leeward walls. The biggest negative pressures arise at the bottom corners of the
sidewalls, where separation occurs. As the leeward wall is sheltered, the airflow will be slower, hence inducing less
negative pressure compared to the sidewall parallel to the flow direction.

When the angle of attack from the wind is changed, the surface pressure distribution changes on the different

face A, so that face C has a front face as well, and face D has a rear face similarly with face B. The basic values on
the graph cannot be transferred as overall values as such, but the development in magnitude of the coefficient in
relation to the angle of attack when turned 45 degrees, is very interesting. It is seen that pressure value on face C,
ܥ௣஼, changes from negative pressure to positive pressure when the inclination reaches above 25 degrees, and
subsequently attains the magnitude of ܥ௣஺ when 45 degrees are reached. Likewise it is seen that the magnitude of
negative pressure on face B is slightly smaller than for face D at 0 degrees, as a result of B being the sheltered rear
face, but attains the same value as face D already at around 25 degrees.

2.1.2.3
Interference effects between twin arrangements
The interference effects on the wind pressure distribution between two high-rise buildings are studied in (Yu,
Xie, Zhu, & Gu, 2015) for six different widths and heights of the interference model, in a wind tunnel with a
boundary layer corresponding to a suburban terrain. The values are extracted for a distance/width ratio of 1.5-2.5,
and an interference height/principal height ratio of 0.8-1, comparable to the twin arrangement of the high-rise
buildings.
The mean and peak pressure coefficients are studied in relation to an interference factor, which are describing
the difference in pressure the interference building induces at the principal building, given as:
ܫܨ=
݄ܶ݁݉݁ܽ݊(݌݁ܽ݇)݌ݎ݁ݏݏݑݎ݁ܿ݋݂݂݁݅ܿ݅݁݊ݐݓ݅ݐ݄݅݊ݐ݁ݎ݂݁ݎܾ݅݊݃ݑ݈݅݀݅݊݃
݄ܶ݁݉݁ܽ݊(݌݁ܽ݇)݌ݎ݁ݏݏݑݎ݁ܿ݋݂݂݁݅ܿ݅݁݊ݐݓ݅ݐ݄݋ݑݐ݅݊ݐ݁ݎ݂݁ݎܾ݅݊݃ݑ݈݅݀݅݊݃
( 2.12 )
The interference factor implies that with magnitudes above 1, amplifications of the pressures at the principal
building are present, whereas magnitudes below 1 are beneficial, as it decreases the pressure on the principal
building.
In Figure 2.13 the pressure distribution on the principal building is illustrated, when the interference building is
placed in front of the principal building with a wind direction for tandem study. The interference
height/principal height ratio is equal to 0.8, which entails that a remarkable amplification of the pressures is
observed at the topmost of the principal building. Due to the wind direction, the effect of shielding on the
principal building is investigated. Generally the study shows, that shielding effects are significant when the
distance/width ratio is smaller than 3, where Figure 2.13 is illustrated for a distance/width ratio of 1.5.

It is seen that the pressure coefficients at both the windward wall and side walls at b) are significantly smaller

exposed areas compared to the isolated building for the peak coefficients and between 25-40% for the mean
coefficient illustrated in c). Furthermore it is evident that the interference effects mostly occur at the outer mid
part of the surface.

2.2 Codes and recommendations
In Section 2.1 the wind phenomena of the atmospheric boundary layer and wind flow around high-rise buildings
are represented in terms of theoretical and experimental backgrounds. These theories substantiate the codes and
recommendations for determination of wind loading on structures for structural design. It is stated in the
recommendations and codes, that if structures are considered complex it is recommended to execute wind tunnel
tests. However the calculation routines in codes and recommendations are often still performed to compare the
results, and especially the mean wind speed in nature, since it can only be determined by earlier measurements
or codes.
It is natural to use the Eurocode calculation routines to determine the mean wind speed at the top of the high-
rise buildings, since it is expected the high-rise buildings should be erected in Denmark. However the
atmospheric boundary layer profile determined by Eurocode has some limitations, since the maximum height of
the boundary layer of 200 m and the studied high-rise buildings are 350 m high. Thus a comparison to the
Architectural Institute of Japan calculation routines is performed as well, since the height of the atmospheric
boundary layer is changing with the terrain categories.
Eurocode is the European Standard, where National Annexes are added for each nation. Eurocode part 1-4 is
design guidance to wind actions for structural design of buildings. The calculation routines in Section 2.2.1 are
defined in (Eurocode, 2005).
The Architectural Institute of Japan (AIJ) is a non-profit academic association for architects and building
engineers. It is not a governmental organization but serve as publishers of research and studies to heighten the
architectural ability. AIJ has not published any code, but merely recommendations, which however are widely
used in the design of tall buildings both domestically and offshore, why it is deemed feasible to use the
“Recommendations for Loads on Buildings” (AIJ, 1996). Chapter 6 from AIJ studies the wind load for the design
of buildings that respond elastically in strong winds, and the wind load for the design of structural frames and
for the design of components/cladding of buildings.
2.2.1 Eurocode
2.2.1.1
Atmospheric boundary layer profile
The mean wind velocity determined by Eurocode is found as:

normally chosen to a magnitude of 1.

2.2.1.2
Wind forces
The wind pressures on structures are determined by external and internal wind pressures, together with friction
forces. The resulting wind forces on structures are determined by resulting wind pressures, expressed in Eurocode
as:
ܨ௪= ܿ௦ܿௗܿ௙ݍ௣(ݖ௘) ܣ௥௘௙
( 2.20 )
Where ܿ௦ܿௗ is the structural factor, taking the vibrations of the structure into account, ܿ௙ is the force coefficient,

recommended to the full height of the structure, and ܣ௥௘௙ is the reference area of the structure.

2.2.1.3
External pressure coefficients
Generally the pressure coefficients determined by Eurocode are differentiated between local pressure coefficients
cpe1 and overall pressure coefficients cpe10, for areas respectively of 1 m2 and 10 m2 or more. The local pressure
coefficients are used to determine the external loads for small elements, which is exposed to wind gusts, where
the overall pressure coefficients are used to determine the stabilizing system.
The pressure coefficients are indicated for a number of different building shapes in Eurocode, where only the
rectangular shape with a height/depth ratio higher than five and a flat roof is stated in the following.
The vertical windward wall of a rectangular shape is divided into multiple parts, when the ratio between the
height and width of the building becomes larger than two, where the peak velocity pressure is found by the
reference heights. The lower and upper parts are extended to a height equal to the width, where the area in
between is divided into strips.
The determination of external pressure coefficients for a rectangular shape is found in (Eurocode, 2005) for the
vertical walls in EC Table 7.1 and for the flat roof in EC Table 7.2. The pressure coefficients for the zones are
shown in Table 2.5.

dynamic factor ܿௗ and is given as:

which are determined by Annex B or C in (Eurocode, 2005).

2.2.2 Architectural Institute of Japan
The recommendations AIJ are used to compare to the boundary layer profile determined from Eurocode in
Section 2.2.1.1. The derivation of the boundary layer from AIJ is determined from the formulas in the following.
The velocity pressure represents the rise in pressure from the atmospheric static pressure to the stagnation point
on the windward face of the building. The velocity pressure is used when assessing the static load on the building
and is expressed by:
ݍு= 1
2 ߩܷு
ଶ
( 2.30 )
Where ߩis the density of the air, with a recommended value of 1.25 kg/m3, and ܷுis the design wind speed
depended on the wind direction, which is calculated for each wind direction by:
ܷு= ܷ଴ܭ஽ܧு݇௥ௐ
( 2.31 )
Here ܷ଴ is the basic wind speed, ܭୈ the wind directionality factor, ܧୌ the wind speed profile factor at reference
height and ݇୰୛ the return period conversion factor.
The basic wind speed in AIJ corresponds to the 100-year-recurrence 10-minute wind speed over a flat, open
terrain at an elevation of 10 m. This is in coherence with Eurocode as described in Section 2.2.1.1, except that
Eurocode consider the basic wind speed for a return period of 50 years. To be able to compare the different codes,
the same basic wind speed referring to Denmark is used, why the value of 24 m/s from Section 2.2.1.1 can be
determined for a return period of 100 years by using equation ( 2.17 ). However, as the return period conversion
factor ݇୰୛ is included in the basic wind speed for AIJ in equation ( 2.31 ), the value of 24 m/s is merely used as ܷ଴
and the return period conversion factor neglected.
The wind directional factor ܭୈ reflects the directional characteristic and is depended on the geographical location
and topographic features. This value is in coherence with ܿdir in regards to Eurocode in Section 2.2.1.1 and is often
set to a magnitude of 1. The wind speed profile factor is found by ܧு= ܧ௥ܧ௚ which consist of the exposure factor
and the topography factor.

2.3 Results and comparison
2.3.1 Atmospheric boundary layer
The determination of the atmospheric boundary layer has been clarified for the two theoretical procedures, the
Logarithmic law and the Power law, where Eurocode is based on the Logarithmic law, and the AIJ
recommendations is based on the Power law.
The four atmospheric boundary layer determinations are all based on the determination of a terrain category,
where the categories are similar, since the Logarithmic Law, the Power Law and Eurocode are based on roughness
lengths.
The high-rise buildings are expected situated in a suburban area in Denmark. The choice of terrain category is
further elaborated in Chapter 3, which explains about the roughness plates used in the wind tunnel, and Chapter
5 where the analysis and resulting wind profile are presented.
The corresponding terrain categories for the four determinations are collected and compared in Table 2.7 and
Figure 2.17. For calculation see Appendix 2.1.

2.3.2 Wind loading on structures
The pressures the wind flow is inducing to the high-rise buildings are represented for the experimental
backgrounds of ESDU and Holmes, and for the codes in terms of Eurocode. The pressure distributions
represented by Holmes are for a turbulent flow, and the ones shown in ESDU are for a smooth flow. The two
experimental backgrounds show the same position and magnitude of the stagnation point at the windward wall.
However, the main difference of ESDU and Holmes is seen in the pressure distribution due to the differences of a
smooth and turbulent flow. The pressure distribution at the windward wall represented in ESDU for smooth
flow is formed as a half circle from the ground and around the stagnation point, where the theories represented
by Holmes for a turbulent flow are formed as ellipses around the stagnation point towards the ground, see Figure
2.11 and Figure 2.9.
The pressure distributions determined by Eurocode are estimations based on experiments. The local pressure
coefficients cpe1 and the global pressures coefficients cpe10 are comparable to mean pressure coefficients for either
local or global loading. The pressure coefficients given in Eurocode are overestimations, when they are compared
with the pressure distributions represented by Holmes, except for the windward wall, where some of pressure
coefficients are lower. All in all, Eurocode should provide overestimations, as a “code” is necessitated to be on the
safe side.
The resulting drag force, overturning moment and peak acceleration in the along-wind direction, determined by
Eurocode, are listed in Table 2.10 and found in Appendix 2.2. The resulting drag force and bending moment are
forces determined only by estimations of Eurocode.

2.4 Wind tunnel testing
Wind tunnel tests have been performed and developed over the past 200 years by some of the most famous wind
engineers among others Eiffel, Göttinger and Prandtl. Wind tunnel testing is a technic to simulate the wind flow
and pressures around and on structures, where the test model is in full-scale or scaled-model. Since the geometry
is scaled, when using scaled models, it is necessary to scale the other parameters too, which is conducted by
introducing the scaling laws, described in Section 2.4.1.
When wind tunnel tests are performed, it is fundamental to build up a turbulent atmospheric boundary layer,
which reflects the one created by nature at the desired terrain category to obtain reliable test results. The wind
profile, the turbulence intensity profile and the integral length scale are used to investigate the accuracy of the
measurements, when they are compared to the profiles created by nature.
It is worth to remember, that when tests are scaled to smaller models, it is approximations of the natural
phenomena that are created, and the test results should be carefully considered if they are reliable. Furthermore
when the signals of the wind tunnel tests are recorded, it should be considered if the electronic equipment is
measuring correctly and with a high enough frequency to record all fluctuations.
In Figure 2.19 a comparison of wind loading from simulation in a wind tunnel and codes is shown. The test
results from a simulation are shown at the left graph, where the blue line indicates the wind speed in the storm
event. Combining the mean wind speed with the aerodynamic admittance and the pressure coefficients, the
pressure coefficients are illustrated with the red line, which also is a reflection of the final wind loading. The peak
load pressure from the simulation should fit the values from the codes.
Figure 2.19: Comparison of simulation of nature and codes (Koss, Wind Engineering - Part 11: Codes & Standards)
In the right graph the same storm event is shown, but here the peak wind speed is shown as the dotted blue line.
Combining the peak wind speed with the aerodynamic admittance and the pressure coefficients from the code,
the load pressure are found and shown with the dotted red line. Due to the fact that the pressure coefficient from
the codes is a mean value, the load will not vary. The load pressures from the two graphs should be the same, and
in the “perfect world” the dotted line between the two graphs should fit together.
When structures become too complex for determination of wind loading by means of codes, it is recommended
always to perform a wind tunnel test. In Eurocode no rules are given for when a wind tunnel tests is necessary to
perform, but only a recommendation of using a wind tunnel test as a supplement to the calculations. In AIJ it is
often stated that a wind tunnel test could be performed to determine pressure coefficients, however it is mostly
when dealing with vortex induced vibrations, or high-rise buildings with small distance to neighbouring
buildings it is imposed.
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 48 -
2.4.1 Scaling
2.4.1.1
Scaling laws
In a simulation it is desired to have the same physical properties for the model as for the full-scale to get results
as close to reality as possible. But since the air in a wind tunnel is the same fluid as at a full-scale, it will be
impossible to obtain full physical similarity, and therefore a partial similarity is used.
The flow around a body for the model and full-scale should have physical similarity, since the ratio between the
forces acting on the body should be the same. This is considered by fluid mechanical similarity with the Reynold
number and Froude number. Reynolds number is a measure of the viscous force around a body, and Froudes
number a measure of the gravity force of a body. The Reynold number and Froude number is given below:
ܴ݁= ݑതܮ
ߥ௔௜௥
( 2.34 )
ܨݎ=
ݑത
ඥ݃ܮ
( 2.35 )
Where ݑത is the mean wind speed, ܮ is a geometric dimension, ߥ௔௜௥is the viscosity of air and ݃ is the gravitational
force.
The Reynold number and Froude number for the model and the full-scale should always be the same, and the
scaling parameters are derived from these. Since the viscosity of air and the gravitational force in the wind
tunnel and at a full-scale buildings do not change, the geometric scales are derived from the two expressions:
ߣ௚௘௢.ோ௘=
ܮ௠௢ௗ௘௟
ܮ௙௨௟௟ି௦௖௔௟௘
= ݑത௙௨௟௟ି௦௖௔௟௘
ݑത௠௢ௗ௘௟
( 2.36 )
ߣ௚௘௢.ி௥=
ܮ௠௢ௗ௘௟
ܮ௙௨௟௟ି௦௖௔௟௘
= ቆݑത௠௢ௗ௘௟
ݑത௙௨௟௟ି௦௖௔௟௘
ቇ
ଶ
( 2.37 )
The Reynold scaling laws are used for investigation of pressure and forces acting on a body, where the Froude
scaling laws are used for bodies where the stiffness is provided by gravitational forces.
The Reynold or Froude scaling is called strict similarity, since the boundaries for the scaling laws are based on
the geometric scale and the equivalence of the specific flow state parameters. The scaling laws for strict similarity
are dependent on the ratio of the geometric scale, and from these other quantities can be determined. The scaled
velocity in the wind tunnel is found from the geometric scale and the full-scale as:
ݑത௠௢ௗ௘௟.ோ௘= ݑത௙௨௟௟ି௦௖௔௟௘
ߣ௚௘௢.ோ௘
( 2.38 )
ݑത௠௢ௗ௘௟.ி௥= ݑത௙௨௟௟ି௦௖௔௟௘ටߣ௚௘௢.ி௥
( 2.39 )
Here it is seen the mean velocity of the full-scale, scaled with the Reynold number, leads to very high velocities
for the model, where the mean velocity for the model scaled by Froudes number, leads to very low velocities.
2.4.1.2
Free scaling
Since it is difficult to test with extremely high velocities to achieve Reynold similarity, a known scaling system
called relaxed similarity or free scaling can be used for sharp-edged bluff bodies.
At a sharp-edged bluff body the separation point of vortices are known to take place at the edges, thus a force
similarity is known. From a study of force ratios performed by Castro and Robins in (Koss, Introduction to Wind
Tunnel Testing in Civil Engineering), it was found that the force ratios was stabilized at lower magnitudes of
Reynold number than presumed with strict similarity scaling laws. To ensure stabile force ratios of the body by
performing a Reynold test, the scaling laws of relaxed similarity can be used. The independency of the Reynold
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 2 – Wind loading on structures
- 49 -
number for a sharp-edged bluff body in a turbulent flow with a turbulence intensity between 0.5 % and 15 %, was
found at Reynold numbers above 10000. The wind velocity in the wind tunnel where the Reynold number is
equal to 10000 is defined as the onset, and is the minimum velocity the simulation can run at.
Free scaling is depends on three factors; sampling speed, blockage and sensibility. The sampling speed is
dependent on the equipment used to execute the simulation, where a boundary is introduced by the maximum
sampling speed. The blockage is dependent on the dimensions of the model, why a large model results in a large
geometry scale, and thereby a lower wind velocity can be used in the simulation to achieve similarity. At last the
sensibility of the wind tunnel is considered, since the simulation has to run at a certain wind velocity to achieve
a fully turbulent flow, and furthermore the wind tunnel has a limit of maximum wind velocity it can run at.
From the fundamental scales of length, mass and time, the scales for other quantities are derived by the units of
the scales. The geometric scale is based on the unit of length, and the time scale is established by the velocity
scale, since it is a measure of length per second, depended on the geometric scale. The mass scale is established by
the assumption that the density in the model and full-scale is equal to each other.
An overview of the relaxed scales is listed in Table 2.11.
Table 2.11: Overview of relaxed scales

ܴ݁௢௡௦௘௧= 10000 ߥ௔௜௥

To determine the range of the velocity scale, the reference mean velocity of the full-scale model at the top of the
building, found in Section 2.3.1, is used. The mean wind velocity is found by means of the Power Law to 42.53
