Chapter 3
3 WIND TUNNEL IN PRACTICE AND PRE-
PROCESSING
In the present chapter all the practical work in the wind tunnel test is represented. The chapter contents three
main sections of background and perspective, model design and technical procedure and setup.
In the first section the wind tunnel and boundary layer is described, together with a short introduction to the
background knowledge of the preliminary tests, the surface pressure measurements and the oil paint tests. In the
second section the applied models of the tests are represented. In the last section an exposition of the technical
procedures of the tests is given, together with a thorough explanation of the pressure system applied for the
surface pressure model. The section is completed with a description of the adjustment procedures of the measured
data.
3.1 Background and perspective
3.1.1 Wind tunnel
To be able to conduct the different test measurements, the boundary-layer wind tunnel (WT2) at FORCE
Technology has been put at disposal. The WT2 is an open recirculation wind tunnel (Eiffel type) with closed test
section. The WT2 is suitable for investigation of wind effects on structures, as it is possible to generate
appropriate atmospheric boundary layers corresponding to real nature because of its long test section.
In Figure 3.1 an outline of an Eiffel type wind tunnel is seen, where the construction and facilities more or less
are consistent with WT2 at FORCE technology. The wind tunnel has four main parts; the inlet, the flow
processing unit, the test section and the exhaust. At the inlet the pre-processing of the flow happens, where a
honeycomb helps smoothening the flow. The working section with spires and roughness plates is referred to as
the flow-processing unit, as this section generates the turbulence in the flow for the appropriate boundary layer.
The test section is, as the name indicates, where the actual test is performed with the measuring pressure model.
The exhaust is where the flow is generated by a fan. The maximum wind speed in WT2 is 24 m/s, but it is only
obtained with a smooth laminar flow without any boundary layer roughness plates. The wind speed is adjusted
by rotating the angle of the fan blades, which is done either manually or automatically. At the automatic mode,
a certain wind speed is keyed in at a display, and the blades automatically rotate until the prescribed wind speed
is obtained at the inlet. In the manual mode, the blade angle is rotated manually until the desired wind speed is
obtained by looking at a manometer, which is connected to a pitot tube at either the inlet or in the test section.
Figure 3.1:  Eiffel type wind tunnel (Koss, Wind Tunnel Testing lectures - Part 5b)
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 52 -
The WT2 has a total length of 20.4 m, where the flow-processing unit is 14.8 m and the test section 5.6 m, where a
turntable is mounted covering a diameter of 2.2 m. The width of the wind tunnel is 2.6 m and has a height of 1.8
m.
The wind tunnel is constructed of smooth wooden plates and plexiglas to secure that the walls and ceiling will
affect the wind flow as little as possible.
3.1.2 Preliminary test
3.1.2.1
Atmospheric boundary layer
To be able to translate the wind tunnel results to full-scale results, the atmospheric boundary layer profile is
important, as this has to reflect an appropriate terrain corresponding to the nature. To create an atmospheric
boundary layer profile in the wind tunnel, random roughness blocks are mounted to the floor to build up a
turbulent flow that reflects the desired terrain determined Chapter 2 in Table 2.7 and Figure 2.17.
WT2 at FORCE Technology has particular roughness plates that reflect a city-profile. These roughness plates
have been tested several times by FORCE Technology to verify that the wind profile has a correct curve of a city
roughness terrain. However, as the wind profile generated by the boundary layer from the roughness blocks is
somewhat dependent on the model scale used, an analysis of the wind profile has to be conducted.
The wind flow in the wind tunnel is changed from a laminar flow at the inlet, to a turbulent flow at the test
section, with a curved atmospheric boundary layer profile and a turbulence intensity profile that has the highest
value at the bottom. In Figure 3.2 the principle of wind flow in a wind tunnel is shown. The turbulent
atmospheric boundary layer profile is build up through the spires and above the roughness blocks in the flow-
processing unit, the velocity profile and turbulence intensity are at their highest at line 1 and are decreased over
the test section, since the bottom of the test section is smooth and without roughness blocks. This phenomenon is
illustrated in the three profiles shown between line 1 and 2.
Figure 3.2: Principle of wind flow in a wind tunnel (Koss, Wind Engineering - Part 8: Simulation)
The turbulent atmospheric boundary layer flow should reflect the desired terrain category at the centre of the
test section where the model is tested.
The wind velocity along various heights of the wind tunnel is measured as time series, and a mean wind velocity
profile and a turbulence intensity profile are determined. It is convenient to measure more time series close to the
floor, since it is expected the mean wind velocity will have a higher inclination near the bottom, and the
turbulence intensity profile will be at its highest at the bottom. These profiles are compared to the theoretical
profiles determined in Chapter 2, to verify they reflect nature.
3.1.2.2
Integral length scale
It is interesting to know the size of the vortices in a turbulent flow, since it gives an understanding of the flow
process and the fluctuations that affects the model. The integral length scale in the wind tunnel should reflect
the turbulent length scale in the nature. From an integral length scale test in the wind tunnel, the scale of the
turbulence between the wind tunnel and the true size of vortices in the nature is determined.
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 53 -
Since the size of the vortices reflects the fluctuations, it is interesting to asses these fluctuations at the building
position to see how the building is affected, thus the test is executed at the top of the high-rise building and at
60% of the height of the high-rise building. In general, a long time series with a high frequency gives the best
quality of a density spectrum, since all small fluctuations are recorded and the scatter and noise are reduced.
Furthermore the measurements of the air flow recorded at both heights of the model are used to find two
velocity factors, used for determining the exact wind flow at the top of building and at 60% height for any given
reference wind measured from the pitot tube placed at the side of the wind tunnel at 50 cm’s height.
3.1.3 Surface pressure measurement at discrete points
3.1.3.1
Surface pressure measurement
To interpret on how a turbulent wind flow affects a high-rise building, the pressure acting on each surface is
essential to record. It is important to know how the distribution is expected to shape, so the measuring pressure
points on each face can be placed correctly. Looking at theory from both Holmes and ESDU, prior described in
Chapter 2, the interesting areas are located at the of the top faces and at the corners when assessing a sharped
edges high-rise model, why the density of the pressure taps are concentrated in these areas. The wind flow
recorded at each tap is used for determination of the pressure acting on the façade, where the dynamic forces
induced by the fluctuations and thereby peak pressures are of main interest.
It is furthermore interesting to see how the flow is affected if a neighbouring building were to be constructed
next to the high-rise, why the test also is conducted with a twin model to serve as blockage for the wind.
3.1.3.2
Reynolds test
The Reynolds test is conducted to determine in which wind velocity range the turbulence is presupposed as fully
established. When the turbulence is fully established the load coefficient will be constant within a certain range
of velocities, called the similarity range. The dimensionless load coefficient is found from normalising the
measured load on the pressure model with the reference pressure force from the flow, as seen in Section 2.1.2.2.
By exposing the pressure model for different wind speeds and graphically comparing the load coefficients for
each speed, the curve will plateau at an apparent value which reveals the lower limit for the velocity range, hence
the wind speed that minimum can be used in the pressure test.
3.1.4 Oil painting
The streamlines around an obstacle can be detected by visualization tests, such as erosion tests, lazer tests and oil
painting tests. The tests are used to give an understanding of the wind flow around an obstacle, and to study the
different phenomena of the flow the streamlines are creating. The visualization tests are compared to studies of
the streamlines, explained in Chapter 2 and especially Figure 2.5 and Figure 2.7.
With an oil painting test, a model is covered with oil paint in a wind tunnel, and the streamlines mean directions
are seen at the surfaces of the building and the ground. It is convenient to make a movie of the oil painting test,
since the most affected areas are detected immediately after the wind tunnel is turned on. When the oil paint has
dried, the streamlines can be seen as fine lines at the surfaces. The stagnation points in front, in the wake and on
the front side of the building, explained in Figure 2.5, are often detected on the oil paint model, together with the
horseshoe vortex and board jet streams around the building. Furthermore the vortices at the sides and wake of
the building are often noticeable as well.
Since the oil painting test only is a visualization test, the magnitude of the pressures is seen in the extent of
driven oil paint on the surfaces, which is compared to the streamlines visible in the CFD simulations.
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 54 -
3.2 Model design
3.2.1 Atmospheric boundary layer
The atmospheric boundary layer in WT2 at FORCE Technology is created with particular roughness plates,
which is conducted to the purpose of creating a city-profile. The flow-processing unit of the wind tunnel is 14.8 m
long and contains teeth and spires at the inlet and four sizes of blocks. The walls and ceiling are created of
smooth wooden plates and plexiglas to secure a small friction in the flow. The floor is covered with smooth
wooden plates, carpet and artificial grass to create a turbulent flow.
In Figure 3.3 a) WT2 is seen with the city-profile roughness elements from the test section, where the turntable is
in the foreground. In Figure 3.3 b) WT2 is seen with the city-profile roughness elements from the centre of the
flow-processing unit, where spires and teeth are seen in the background. The sizes of the roughness elements are
listed in Table 3.1 with an approximated amount of elements.
Figure 3.3: a) WT2 seen from the test section, b) WT2 seen from the centre of the flow-processing unit

Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 55 -
The time series are measured with a cobra probe, which is an instrument that can measure a dynamic velocity in
three directions with a frequency up to or even higher than 2000 Hz. The cobra probe is measuring the pressures
at the nose and has a reference pressure port at the body. It has a built in transducer in the body, which generates
the data to instantaneously velocity vectors and a static pressure (Cobra Probe, 2015). The output is a data file
with information about number of samples, the sample frequency, date and time of measurement. The
measurements are listed in the output file as time series of the three velocity vectors u, v and w in the unit m/s,
and a static pressure in the unit Pa.
The reference wind speed in the wind tunnel is measured with pitot tubes. A pitot tube measures the relative
pressure difference in the unit mm-water-column between the positive side at the nose and the negative side at
the side of the tube. The pitot-tube is connected to a Furness, which is changing the signal from a pressure
difference into volt, to be able to record the digital signal as a time series. Each Furness has a different calibration
factor to convert the signal from volt to mm-water-column or Pa.
The density of air changes in relation to the atmospheric pressure and the temperature of the air. The
temperature inside the wind tunnel is measured with a thermometer, which logs the temperature for each run.
3.2.2 Surface pressure model
For the investigation of wind loads on high-rise buildings, the primary model used is a plexiglas model, to which
the pressure acquisition system is mounted.
The plexiglas model is prior constructed by the authors of the Master Thesis “Comparison of Methods to
Determine Wind Loading on High-rise Structures and their Dynamic Response” (Barfoed & Hauberg, 2010). The
model consists of four 5 mm plexiglas plates in the dimensions of 102 mm x 610 mm and a top, which forms the
high-rise model by the means of glue and screws. To be able to measure the surface pressure, the model is
provided with 216 holes with steel tubes at discrete points distributed on each surface and on the top. The pattern
of the pressure points are placed accordingly to the expected pressure distribution on the high rise as described in
Section 3.1.3, where denser points are concentrated around the corners and at the areas of interest, as seen on
Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: The location of pressure points and size of reference areas on each model surface and roof. All measurements in mm.
The location of each pressure points is meticulously noted down in an excel sheet and given a specific number, as
the analysis demands full attention to where each pressure point is located and to which scanner it is connected
to. The excel sheet can be found in Appendix 3.1.
The bottom of the model is constructed of hard plastic material with two oblong holes in each side in order to
allow cables and tubes to penetrate and connect to the acquired hardware.
The second model for the test of the twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings is referred to as “the
interference model”, as its main purpose merely is to serve as blockage for the pressure model, thus no
installation of pressure taps is necessary. To serve as a blockage for the wind, the model is constructed of solid
foam material in the same dimensions as the pressure model. For pictures of the two models, reference is made to
Section 3.3.2.
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 56 -
3.2.3 Oil painting model
The best result of an oil painting test is depended on the quality of the model. Smooth surfaces result in the oil
paint moving freely along the surfaces, and sharp edges results in a better separation of the vortices induced by
the turbulent wind flow.
Each high-rise building is created of six chipboards. To secure no bumps at the surfaces, the corners are cut with
a 45 degrees angle and attached with glue. The surfaces are covered with mortar and painted with white spray
paint to create smooth surfaces. The oil painting test is executed on both the single high-rise building and the
twin arrangement, resulting in two full test-models. One high-rise model is mounted on a 1.2 x 1.2 m white
chipboard plate with a smooth surface, and two high-rise models are mounted on another white chipboard plate
with a smooth surface, where the two models in the twin arrangement are placed with a distance of 100 mm,
corresponding to the width of a single high-rise building.
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 57 -
3.3 Technical procedure and setup
3.3.1 Preliminary tests
3.3.1.1
Atmospheric boundary layer
The wind velocity is measured at the centre of the turntable with a cobra probe, which is mounted on a robot
arm that can move in three directions. The measurements are conducted at 29 different heights for duration of
one minute and with a sample frequency of 1250 Hz. The heights are listed in Table 3.2 and the test setup is seen
in Figure 3.5, illustrating the robot arm containing the cobra probe and pitot-tube. The heights are each
measured manually with a ruler.

The reference wind speed is measured with pitot-tubes at the inlet of the wind tunnel, at the side of the test

as mean-, standard deviation-, minimum- and maximum wind velocities for each of the 29 measurements. The
temperature is logged likewise the wind velocity.

The integral length scale is measured at the centre of the turntable with a cobra probe, in the same manner as for

to the height of the high-rise building and at 60 % of the height. All time series are measured in three minutes
and an overview of the seven measurements is given in Table 3.3.

The reference wind speed and temperature is logged as mean-, standard deviation-, minimum- and maximum

3.3.2 Surface pressure measurement at discrete points
The pressure acquisition system is a complex system where all elements play an important role in obtaining the
desired results. The main components in the system are the elements used for the translation of pressure signal at
the pressure taps into readable data. The basics of the total system consist of the high-rise model with pressure
taps, sensors, referred to as scanners, to record the pressure signals, a connecter box to transfer the signals from
the scanners to a computer device and the control-program. In the following the features of the main elements in
the data acquisition chain are elaborated after which the setup of the test is explained.
3.3.2.1
Data acquisition
When conducting a physical test as wind tunnel testing, the main challenges lies within converting the values
from the original analogue signal into digital values, which can be interpreted by a computer and used for
processing and analysis. The process of gaining interpretable results is referred to as the acquisition chain, where
the main phases or elements consist of analogue data acquisition, a digitizing unit and a computer processing
unit.
Normally the chain consists of several sub elements within each main phase, but as the scanners used in the
wind tunnel test (etched silicon diaphragm type ESP-32HD) are capable of converting the original pressure signal
to a digital signal in one step, the chain is reduced to fewer phases. Figure 3.6 shows the main elements of the
system when the model is setup for calibration, where the PtPSI unit is connected to the computer processing
unit with installed PtPSI software outside the picture.
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 59 -
Figure 3.6: Hardware setup for data acquisition and calibration
The scanners collect the data in multiplex, why the signal from each terminal is collected in one digital signal,
which is transferred to the PtPSI unit. The multiplex signal is reconstructed in the PtPSI unit, where the signals
from each scanner are separated so the signal from each terminal on each scanner becomes interpretable.
Sensor
The sensors in the data acquisition chain consist of seven different scanners that serve as transducers, which
converts the pressure signal into a measurable electric signal. The scanners each have 32 terminals, which are
connected to the 216 pressure taps on the pressure model. Furthermore, the scanners have five extra terminals as
illustrated in Figure 3.7. The scanners have two different modes, a “run mode” and a “calibration mode”, where
the output from each channel differs from the two modes. In run mode, the output signal represents the
differential pressure between the designated terminal and the reference pressure from the R-ref terminal. In
calibration mode, the output is the differential pressure between the taps connected in parallel to a predefined
calibration pressure at the CAL terminal and the reference pressure from the C-ref terminal. Terminal C1 and C2
are used to switch between the two modes by applying a high pressure that makes a slide inside the scanner
move from run mode to calibration mode.
Figure 3.7: outline of the scanners (Geyer, Technical Procedure TP 187.1.210 - Pressure Tests, 2009)
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 60 -
The scanners produce a multiplex signal, as prior described, where the signal from each terminal collectively is
translated to one digital output. The output is transferred trough the cable socket seen at the right side of the
scanner on Figure 3.7, where a cable from each scanner is connected to the PtPSI unit.
PtPSI unit
The PtPSI unit are the connecter between the sensor-signal and the PtPSI software on the computer, where the
digital output of the numeric values is manipulated. To be able to interpret the different signals from each
terminal, the multiplex signal is reconstructed by the PtPSI unit so each signal gets separated.
The PtPSI unit also serve as the “main station” for the calibration system, when calibration is conducted, as it
furthermore links the Furness and compressor to the calibration system.
PtPSI software
The PtPSI software is used for setup of the data sampling and for processing the digital values from the scanner
output. The output signal from the scanners is in the unit volt, but as the PtPSI software is setup with a
calibration file which contains a conversion constant for each terminal, the output file provide the values in [Pa],
which can be used for further analysis. Before sampling the data, the sampling rate and sampling time is
specified in the software as a maximum of 625 Hz and 50 s. The sampling rate has to be sufficiently high to avoid
aliasing, as the conversion from a continuous signal to a discrete is vulnerable to be misinterpreted, when the
digitalized image of the continuous signal has to be reconstructed.
Furthermore the software is used for changing between calibration and run mode.
The PtPSI software stores all files in GDF format, why the files are not readable unless a GDF-reader is installed
on the computer device. To be able to process the output files the GDF files are all converted to ASCII format by a
Matlab routine provided by FORCE Technology.
3.3.2.2
Calibration
To make sure the output from each scanner is consistent with what is expected, the system needs to be
calibrated, as electronic components naturally drifts over time. To calibrate the system, four terminals on each
scanner are used – C1 and C2 are used for switching to calibration mode and C-ref refers to the static pressure at
calibration which is used to find the differential pressure of the series of pre-defined pressures applied to the CAL
terminal, starting from -250 Pa to 250 Pa. The calibration routine is setup in the PtPSI software, where the
pressure range and a pressure step of 100 Pa are specified. In Figure 3.6 the hardware setup for calibration is seen,
where the Furness, which functions as a manometer, is used for checking the pressure range and the ‘calibration
system’ for changing the pressure by means of an installed compressor.
The output from the calibration is logged in the PtPSI software and archived in a calibration file with three
calibration constants for all 32 terminals on each scanner. To achieve an acceptable calibration file, the constants
have to be within a certain range. The 1st calibration constant is a known error and should range between +/- 10,
the 2nd constant should range between +/- 1000 and the 3rd should be in the range of 400-900. The values are based
on experience by FORCE Technology. The calibration file is saved and incorporated in the PTPSI program when
logging the output from the scanners during run mode.
To demonstrate that the calibration is successfully carried out, a calibration test is conducted. The scanners are
switched to run-mode, and a pre-defined pressure (-190 Pa and 250 Pa) is applied to each R-ref terminal by means
of a manometer and pressure system. The file is logged in the PtPSI program after which a GDF-reader makes it
possible to check if the pressure on all terminals corresponds in magnitude but opposite value to the applied
pressure with reasonable tolerance. Furthermore a zero run is recorded both prior to and after the calibration test
to correct for any interference with the signal, when the files are analysed.

Test execution for Reynolds test
The test is conducted by exposing the single high-rise pressure model to an increasing range of wind speeds at an
angle of 0 degrees. In Table 3.4 an overview of the wind speeds are seen, where the values correspond to the
reference height for the pitot-tube at 500 mm. The test protocols can be found in Appendix 3.2.
Table 3.4:  directory of wind speeds used in the Reynolds test
Wind speed
[m/s]
1.47
8.61
2.37
10.74
2.94
12.40
4.98
14.12
6.92
16.07
Test execution for surface pressure measurement
Both the pressure test for the single high-rise building and twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings are
conducted in the exact same manner, only varying the mounting of the interference model, why the test
program is only specified once.
The test is carried out by exposing the pressure model to a wind speed of approximately 15 m/s at 500 mm
height, which is the reference height for the pitot-tube, for 360 degrees in intervals of 10 degrees. The wind angle
from which the models are exposed is illustrated in Figure 3.10 a) for the single high-rise, and in b) for the twin
arrangement. To have enough sampling data and to overcome any damaged test measurements, each test series is
conducted three times. Furthermore a zero run is sampled every seventh run, leaving six runs in between to be
corrected for the most recent drift and noise in the scanners. Each run is listed in a test protocol, where run
number, angle of wind exposure, reference speed and temperature is noted. The test protocols are found in
Appendix 3.2.
Figure 3.10: Angle of wind direction a) Single high-rise building,  b) Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 63 -
3.3.3 Oil painting test
The oil paint, which is used for the oil painting test, is a suitable mixture of linseed oil, diesel oil and black colour
dust. The linseed oil gives the oil paint thickness and viscosity, where the diesel oil makes it more fluid and
thereby less viscous. The black colour dust makes the streamlines visible on the white-painted models.
The oil painting test is executed for both the single high-rise building and for the twin arrangement. The full
models are mounted to the floor in the wind tunnel with screws. The model is painted with the black oil paint at
the building and ground, and the wind tunnel is turned on full speed immediately after. The streamlines at the
models are detected after a few minutes, and the desired results are obtained when the oil paint is dry, which
requires the wind tunnel to run around an hour.
In Figure 3.11 a) the single high-rise building is seen without oil paint, and in Figure 3.11 b) the twin arrangement
of high-rise buildings is seen, where the oil paint is applied before the wind tunnel is turned on.
Figure 3.11: a) Test setup of single high-rise building without oil paint, b) Test setup of twin arrangement with oil paint before testing
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 64 -
3.3.4 Time series adjustment procedure
After the data from the surface pressure measurements are converted to readable ASCII files in Matlab, the time
series are adjusted for the zero offsets and disturbing noise of the scanners. It is experienced that the scanners are
extremely sensitive and can be very unstable. Some of the scanners measure a high mean value and standard
deviation for the zero offsets, entailing that noise is affecting the scanners. Furthermore some of the peaks in the
time series are obscurely high, which are considered to be a result of the scanners sensibility, hence these peaks
are corrected in the time series. At last certain pressures are creating resonance in the tubes, where a correction of
the time series is conducted to accommodate this issue.
A set of Matlab scripts from FORCE Technology are modified and collected in one script, found in Appendix 3.3,
to fit the four tests of surface pressure measurements. It is specified in the beginning of the Appendix, which tests
time series to be adjusted, where 1 refers to the calibration test, 2 refers to the Reynold test, 3 refers to the surface
pressure measurement of the single high-rise building and 4 refers to the surface pressure measurement of the
twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings.
3.3.4.1
Correction factor between Furness and PtPSI system
The reference pressure in the wind tunnel is measured by the pitot-tube at the side of the wind tunnel, where
both the analogue channel from the Furness and the digital signal from channel 32 on the scanners are
connected, as described in Section 3.3.2.3.
To secure the reference pressure is measured correctly, a correction factor for each scanner can be found between
the Furness and channel 32 at the seven scanners in the PtPSI system. The most reliable measuring instrument to
record the reference pressure in the pitot-tube is the Furness compared to the drifting scanners, thus the
correction factors are applied to the seven scanners.
However, after analysis of the obtained data, it became evident, that the analogue channel was not recorded in
the time series for the surface pressure measurements, why the correction factor is not relevant for the
adjustments of time series.
3.3.4.2
Calibration test
From the calibration test a correction factor can be applied if un-matching results are obtained. A reference
pressure of 250 Pa and -190 Pa is applied to the pressure system in the test, to check if the pressures in the
scanners are measured correctly. After the zero offset is subtracted, the applied pressure at the system should be
similar to the measured pressure at the scanners.
It is found in Appendix 3.3 that the calibration test is very close to the applied pressure, as the deviation of the
applied pressure and the measured pressure is around 0.11-1.16 %. Since the deviation is quite small, is it
considered that it will not have a significant effect of the final results, thus no correction factor is applied.
3.3.4.3
Zero drift correction
By nature, the air in a room will be moving and create disturbing noise, therefore a zero offset is always
subtracted from the measured data. Furthermore it is experienced that the pressure system at FORCE
Technology has a zero drift, which is dependent on time, why a zero measurement is executed every seventh run
to accommodate this problem. It is varying from scanner to scanner if the zero drift is increasing or decreasing in
magnitude, thus a correction factor for each scanner is found. The zero drift is corrected in the Matlab routine in
Appendix 3.4, where the zero drift between two zero runs are calculated and a linear interpolation of the zero
drifts is applied to the six intermediate runs.
Since the reference pressure at the pitot-tube is not logged as mentioned in Section 3.3.4.1, the reference pressure is
measured as time series at channel 32 at the seven scanners. The reference pressure is found from the mean value
of the seven mean values of the seven time series at channel 32. The six runs in between two zero runs should
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 3 – Wind tunnel in practice and pre-processing
- 65 -
have the same reference pressure, since the wind speed of the wind tunnel was not changed over the runs. It is
found the six reference pressures between two zero runs deviates up to 5 %, which is considered to be reasonable.
3.3.4.4
Correction of peaks
Due to the sensibility of the scanners some of the peaks are obscurely high. A peak coefficient is calculated to
examine the magnitude of the peaks, which is found from the mean value and standard deviation of each time
series of each pressure taps, as seen below:
ܿ௣௘௔௞= ݔ௜െݔҧ
ߪ௫
( 3.1 )
Where ݔ௜ is the time series, ݔҧ is the mean value and ߪ௫ is the standard deviation. It is known from experience
that the peak coefficient should have a maximum value of +/-5.
The peak coefficient is calculated for the times series for each run for each tap. If the peak coefficient has a higher
value than +/-5, the peak is reduced by the mean of the two values before and after, leading to five values in total.
The principle of the correction of the peaks is shown in Figure 3.12 for pressure tap 19 for an angle of attack of 20
degrees. The full time series is seen in Figure 3.12 a), and a zoom in on the peaks is seen in Figure 3.12 b). In both
figures the original time series is seen with the corrected time series and the peak coefficient. It is seen that the
peaks, which are exceeding a peak coefficient of +/-5, is corrected to a lower value. Furthermore it is seen the
“obscure” peaks often still exist as peaks in the corrected time series, just with a more reasonable magnitude.
Figure 3.12: a) Principle of correction of peaks in time series, b) Zoom in of correction of peaks
When the time series are corrected, it is observed that the negative peaks (suction) are fairly higher than the
positive peaks (pressure). The suctions of a building are most often of higher magnitudes than the pressures,
which can be the explanation of the negative peaks.
Questions can be raised by implementing a correction of the obtained data as done for the peaks, as this is
somewhat manipulating with the measured data. But as the scanners provided are old and very sensitive, it is
considered acceptable to use this correction to get more reliable results.
3.3.4.5
Pressure signal distortion
When the plastic tubes between the pressure taps and the scanners are affected at certain frequencies, resonance
will occur and the tubes will work as harmonic resonators or flow dampers. To accommodate the issue of
pressure signal distortion, the Tijdeman-Bergh (Geyer, Technical Procedure TP 187.1.210 - Pressure Tests, 2009)
transfer function is calculated and used for the multiple tube system with the Matlab script in Appendix 3.5. By
using the transfer function, the noise, generated due to resonance, is corrected in the time series.
The parameters to determine the transfer function are given in Table 3.5 It is assumed from experience at FORCE
Technology, that the transfer function can be found for an average value of the temperature, thus an average
temperature for the surface pressure measurement for both the high-rise and the twin arrangement are found.
==PAGE:82==

3.3.4.6
Pressure signal correction
Since the transfer function is operating in the frequency domain, the fast fourier transformation function is used
to transform the time series into the frequency domain, so that the time series can be corrected from pressure
signal distortion, whereupon the inverse fast fourier transformation function is used to reconstruct the time
series into the time domain. In Appendix 3.3 equation ( 3.2 ) is used to correct the time series.
݂Ԣ(ݐ) = ܫܨܨܶቈܨܨܶ[݂(ݐ)]

consisting of complex numbers, and ݂Ԣ(ݐ) is the corrected time series in the time domain. (Geyer, Technical
Procedure TP 187.1.210 - Pressure Tests, 2009).
3.3.4.7
Damaged channels correction
When the first assessments of the pressure coefficients where performed in Section 5.3.2, it became evident that
some of the channels are damaged, as the measured magnitudes are completely different from the assumed
pressures and the measured pressures at the nearby channels.
In Figure 3.14 the mean pressure coefficients of the four surfaces for a wind direction of 90 and 180 degrees are
seen, where the windward wall for 90 degrees is wall C and for 180 degrees wall B. When studying the two
windward walls it is clearly seen that a suction area, indicated with blue, occurs at the area where all theories
==PAGE:83==

- 67 -
indicates the largest pressure and stagnation point would occur. Furthermore at wall D and B for 90 degrees, and
wall C and D for 180 degrees, it is observed that some channels are divergent from the remaining wall pressures,
where these are seen as red, indicating that the pressure is approximately 0.
Since all the divergent pressures are occurring at the upper region of the high-rise model, it became evident that
18 channels at scanner 3 are measuring incorrect data.
Wind direction ߠ= 90°
Wind direction ߠ= 180°

To accommodate this issue, the time series of these 18 channels are corrected, by taking the mean of each time
step in the time series of the nearby tap above and below the damaged channel. The corrections are found in

different surfaces is presented for both the single high-rise and for the twin arrangement.

Chapter 4
4 METHODS OF STRUCTURAL RESPONSE
The turbulent nature of the wind induces dynamic forces acting on the structure, causing it to resonate when
affecting with the eigenfrequency. This phenomenon gives rise to complex dynamic problems, both regarding a
load-contribution to the sectional forces due to resonance, and motion-induced vibrations due to response of the
building. The first term relates to a safety issue, as the additional inertia force from resonance effects can lead to a
prevailingly dominant load portion causing the structural elements to fail, whereas the last term relates to a
serviceability issue, as the motion-induced vibration criteria is based on occupant comfort.
The fundamental effect of the turbulent wind is the response of the structure presented as acceleration, as the
total peak accelerations, measured in milli-g, at the uppermost occupied floors, can be compared to threshold
values for satisfying the human comfort level. Whereas the total acceleration is a result of the two components,
due to the sway motions of the building, and the component due to rotational motion.
Furthermore the acceleration response is used for determining the additional inertia force caused by resonance,
by applying Newton’s Second Law of motion, where the force is determined on the basis of the mass and
acceleration of the structure.
Fundamentally the overall objective is to use the response analysis to convert the fluctuating wind load into
static equivalent loads, which can be used when determining the structural sectional forces. These static
equivalent loads are determined by accounting for both the static, the quasi-static and the resonant load
combined in each of the two sway directions and in the torsion direction. The load case for each of the sway
motions and twisting motion will not necessarily occur at the same instant in time, which entails that each peak
of the three loads should not be treated as simultaneous loads, why a set of reduction factors is applied to account
for the peak values occurring at different time slots. These reduction factors yields a set of load combinations,
which should be applied to account for the adequate loading for the structural system, lastly providing the floor-
by-floor loadings for each floor in the building, as the name indicates.
In the following the methods of response analysis are elucidated, describing the components included in the
resulting response, with focus on the determination of peak accelerations and floor-by-floor loadings.
Furthermore a short description of the potentially harming aerodynamic forces for a tall and slender bluff body
is represented, which can be of interest when assessing a high-rise building.
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 70 -
4.1 Methods of response analysis
For a mathematical description of the process from a fluctuating wind flow to a corresponding load effect on the
structure, being the response presented as e.g. the bending moment, stress resultant or acceleration etc., a solution
strategy in both the time domain and frequency domain is possible. Representation in the different domains
provides properties and insight that are hard to detect in the other domain, why each has its own advantages.
When assessing the stochastic process of a turbulent wind, the analysis in the time domain gives full insight to
the exact occurrence of the wind phenomena in time and space. The solution strategy in the time domain will
therefor provide the exact solution as nothing is filtered out, compared to the frequency domain, where all
information concerning time is disregarded. However, a frequency domain approach is useful for analysis of the
wind phenomena occurring, where e.g. vortex shedding can be detected by assessing a power spectrum.
Furthermore an analysis in the frequency domain requires much less computational demand compared to the
time domain, where all data are processed in extensive time series. Because of the disadvantages of the
computational demand in the time domain response prediction, a solution strategy in the frequency domain is
the most widespread method of use. Therefore a comparison of the accuracy between the two methods is of
interest, where the time domain approach can be used as verification, as this always will provide the full and true
solution.
Figure 4.1 shows the process of the solution strategies for determining the peak responses in the time domain and
frequency domain, respectively.
Time domain
Frequency domain
Figure 4.1: Time and frequency domain representations (Strømmen, 2010)
The chain of events in the time domain starts by assessing the time series of the wind flow ܷ(ݐ), translated to
the structural load ܳ(ݐ), ending with the time series of the response ݎ(ݐ), found by time integration, where the
maximum values can be determined by e.g. extreme value analysis or a peak factor and standard deviation.
The frequency domain involves the step of transfer from a wind field spectral density ܵ௡(߱), through a
corresponding modal load spectrum ܵொ(߱), lastly ending with the sought response spectrum ܵ௥(߱), from where
the maximum values are found in relation to the fundamental frequency of the structure.
In general the response of the structure can be described as a combination of the static part of the wind, being the
time-average or mean of the loading, and the dynamic part, being the fluctuation of the loading deviating from
the mean. Illustrative the peak response can be defined as:
ܺ(ݐ) = ܺത+ ݔᇱ(ݐ)
( 4.1 )
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 71 -
Where ܺ(ݐ) is the load effect, ܺത the time-averaged component, and ݔᇱ(ݐ) the fluctuating component, which is
the component of interest, when assessing the dynamic impact.
The fluctuating component ݔᇱ(ݐ) accounts for the dynamic loading on the structure, caused by wind gusts with
varies frequencies. The frequencies of the wind gusts are prevailingly within a low frequency range, where
structures’ eigenfrequencies normally do not get affected, however, for structures with low natural frequencies
and damping, the fluctuating nature of wind phenomena can cause excitations of significant vibrations, due to
the resonant response of the structure. The resonant dynamic response occurs when the frequency of the wind
gusts affect with the natural frequencies of the structure. Therefore distinction should be made between the
resonant response R, and the background fluctuating response B, which all structures are affected by. The
background response represents the quasi-static response caused by gusts below the lowest natural frequency of
vibration, and is prevailingly the dominant contribution in the case of along-wind loading. The resonant
response contribution does, however, become more and more significant and lastly dominating, when the
structure becomes slender and taller entailing a lower and lower natural frequency.
Figure 4.2 shows the response spectral density of a dynamic structure under wind loading, where the
contribution from the background response is found as the most dominating, and the resonant response of the
first and second mode are seen at the hatched areas.
Figure 4.2: Response spectral density for a structure with significant resonant contributions (Holmes, 2004)
To illustrate how the contribution of the resonant response to of the background response changes concurrently
with the natural frequency of the structure, three time histories are shown in Figure 4.3. The first time history a)
is of the exciting wind force, whereas b) and c) are response time histories of a structure with high fundamental
frequency and of a structure with low fundamental frequency, respectively. It is clearly seen, that the structural
response in b) generally follows the time series of the wind force, showing that the resonant response has small
contribution to the background response because of the high fundamental frequency of the structure.
Conversely, the natural frequency and thereby the resonant response plays an important role in c), where the
deviation from the time variant of the wind force is obvious.
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 72 -
Figure 4.3: Time histories of a) wind force, b) structural response for a high natural frequency,
c) structural response for a low natural frequency (Holmes, 2004)
When calculating in the time domain, both the background component, response component and mean
component are accounted for, as it is the direct time series which is analysed. However when calculating in the
frequency domain all three contributions needs to be determined and included in the response.
In the following an exposition of the solution strategy in both the frequency domain and the time domain will
be accounted for. In the frequency domain, the “Spectral Method” with static equivalent loads will be elucidated,
whereas the “Newmark Method” will be explained in the time domain, followed by a description of extreme
value analysis.
4.1.1 Frequency domain
When assessing the sampling data, the peak wind-induced pressures and structural loads measured in the wind
tunnel, do already contain both the mean value, defined as ܺത in equation ( 4.1 ), and the quasi-static fluctuating
value of the total response, defined as the background contribution B of the fluctuating component ݔᇱ(ݐ) in
equation ( 4.1 ). However, what is unaccounted for in the measurements, is the resonant contribution to the
resonant response R, which represents the effects of the additional “inertia forces” caused by the wind-induced
resonant vibrations.
To calculate the peak response of the structure in the frequency domain, quite a number of different approaches
are at hand, e.g. the “Gust Response Factor”, the “Dynamic Response factor” or the “Peak Factor method”,
however, by the method of Wieland in 1999, the peak acceleration can be determined on the basis of static
equivalent modal loads, which is favourable when comparing to a time domain analysis. The following formulas
are derived based on the “Spectral Method” from theory presented by Wieland in (Larsen, Larose, & Livesey,
1999).
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 73 -

4.1.2 Time domain
The structural response solved in the time domain can be both carried out analytically by modal analysis or by
numerical time integration. The analytical analysis necessitate the system to be linear and decoupled by modes,
where the eigenvalue problem is solved for either the full or truncated system, and each uncoupled systems are
reflecting a single degree of freedom system. The eigenvalue problem of the full system is often sorely expansive
to solve, why it can be necessary to find alternative solution methods. The reduced or truncated system can be an
alternative, but considerations of the impact by not including all modes have to be taken into account.
A numerical solution of the eigenvalue problem by time integration is available for both linear and non-linear
systems, but does also require some approximations to be resolved. However, for linear systems, as pertinent in
this case, these approximations can be done with reasonable accuracy. Even though both methods are available
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 75 -
as the present system is linear, direct time integration is somewhat less cumbersome than an analytical analysis
with a decoupling of the modes.
Varies time integrations algorithms are available for the dynamic equilibrium equations, where Newmark and
Duhamel integration has gained most currency. Duhamel integration however requires the response to be
dissolved into modal components, whereas Newmark time integration is solved for the full system, why
Newmark’s method has been chosen for determination of the structural response.
In the following a short exposition of the Newmark method will be made, primarily based on theory from
(Krenk, 2013).
4.1.2.1
Newmark time integration
Newmark’s method represents a single-step time integration approach for the solution of the dynamic response
of structural system, where the differential equation at time ݐ is attempted satisfied after the solution at time
ݐെοݐ is found. This requires the solution of a set of linear equations at each time step.
When the structure is linear, the dynamic equilibrium, defined as the equation of motion as described in Section
1.3.2, is given by:
ࡹ࢝ሷ(ݐ) + ࡯࢝ሶ(ݐ) + ࡷ࢝(ݐ) = ࡲ(ݐ)
( 4.11 )
The state of a linear structure at any particular time is described completely by the load vector ࡲ(t) and the state
vector [࢝(t), ࢝ሶ(t)] containing the displacements and velocities at time ݐ.
The objective of the Newmark time integration procedure for the equation of motion is, as mentioned, a one-step
algorithm, in which the state vector [࢝୬ାଵ, ࢝ሶ௡ାଵ] at time ݐ௡ାଵ is calculated by the use of the state vector
[࢝୬, ࢝ሶ௡] at the previous time ݐ௡ and the load vector at both these times, ݂௡and ݂௡ାଵ. (Krenk, 2013)
The stability and accuracy of the algorithm is dependent on the choice of the degree of forward weighting of the
time step and the size of the time step οݐ= ݐ௡ାଵെݐ௡. The degree of forward weighting are described by the
parameters ߛ and ߚ which are chosen in the range of 0 < ߛ< 1 and 0 < ߚ<
ଵ
ଶ. If the parameters both are
chosen as their minimum values, it entails full backward weighting, whereas the upper values entail full forward
weighting. For an average acceleration algorithm, the choice of parameter values are set as ߛ=
ଵ
ଶ and ߚ=
ଵ
ସ to
reach unconditional stability, which is independent of the time step. The size of the time step is often determined
beforehand from the sample frequency, why it is desirable to have unconditional stability. However, if stability
or accuracy of the integration algorithm cannot be obtained properly, the time interval can be chosen
sufficiently small by splining the time series, discussed in Section 5.3.3.1, providing a more stable solution as more
values to evaluate are at disposal.
The Newmark algorithm is obtained by satisfying the dynamic equilibrium at time ݐ௡ାଵ. This is done by
substituting a set of discrete relations between displacement, velocity and acceleration in the equation of motion,
from which the acceleration vector ࢝ሷ࢔ା૚at time ݐ௡ାଵ can be calculated.
The discrete relations expressed by velocity and displacement are given as:
࢝ሶ࢔ା૚= (࢝ሶ௡+ (1 െߛ) οݐ࢝ሷ࢔) + (ߛοݐ࢝ሷ࢔ା૚)
( 4.12 )

correction step.

of extreme value analysis.
4.1.2.2
Extreme value analysis
If the numerical outcome of a physical process at any time or position in space is random and only can be
predicted by probability, the process is stochastic. Elaborated, random means that an observed set of records of
the numerical outcome cannot be precisely repeated, but will instead follow a mathematical pattern represented
by statistics.
The instantaneous wind velocity pressure can be described as random and is therefore referred to as a stochastic
process. The physical characteristics of the process are described by its statistical properties, why prediction of the
value of the pressure at a certain position and time only can be performed in a statistical manner. There is a
distinction between short term and long term statistics, where the random outcome of a short term process
represents the conditions within a certain weather condition, while the long term statistics are representatives
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 77 -
extracted from a large set of individual short term conditions. To be able to use the numerical outcome of a
process, the statistics of a short term process has be somewhat homogenous, why a time-window is introduced,
which divide the outcome in sufficiently short fragments to render reasonably constant statistical properties.
Normally this time-window has a period of T = 10 minutes. Figure 4.4 shows a stochastic process, where statics
are made for short-term conditions from a time-window with a period T. It shows that the process can be
described as mentioned in equation ( 4.1 ) by means of a constant mean ݔҧ௞, which is time invariant, and a
stationary fluctuating part ݔ௞(ݐ), dependent on the time, where it is a general assumption that the fluctuating
part will render a Gaussian probability distribution, as seen on the right in the figure.
Figure 4.4: Short term stationary random process (Strømmen, 2010)
Two levels of randomness can be described in the process. The first level is the statistics made from the short
term process in the time-window T, represented by the Gaussian distribution, whereas the second level of
randomness is the statistics made on the sample set of e.g. the mean value or max value found from statistics
from the recorded sample set in each time-window. The first level of randomness is referred to as the time
domain statistics and the second level as the ensemble statistics.
The ensemble statistics are used, when assessing the peaks of a stochastic process, known as an extreme value
estimation. The evaluation of the peaks of a process can be rendered by different extreme probability functions,
known as Rayleigh, Weibull and Gumbel distributions.
Figure 4.5 shows the principle of a time series of the wind pressure from the recorded pressure points. The time
series is divided into time-windows, referred to as subseries, of ܶ= 10݉݅݊. The stippled vertical line divides the
time series into subseries, where the total number is ܰ= 16, from which the extreme value analysis can be
made on both the minimum peaks within each subseries, marked by red, and the maximum peaks, marked by
green.
Figure 4.5: Principle of extreme value analysis of wind pressure with a time-window of T = 10 min.
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 78 -

statistical values being the mean of the distribution, the standard deviation, the dispersion and the modal value
as given below.

The objective of an extreme value analysis is to model the sampling data extremes, to allow for generalisations
about the recurrence of these events. The characteristic loads or responses are therefore found as the overload risk
in 50 years for the probability of reaching a limit state under the condition of a particular prevailing wind speed,
determined as the 78% fractile of all extremes. Thereby the minimum extreme values are determined at the 22%
fractile, and the maximums at the 78% fractile.

4.1.2.3
Static equivalent loads
As mentioned, when calculating in the time domain, all information regarding mean force, background
component and responses component is already accounted for, as the analysis is made from the direct time series
of the data. The dynamic portion of the load is determined in measure of the response, found by time integration
by the Newmark method, whereas the “static” part of the load is included in the external wind force ܨ measured
directly in the wind tunnel from the pressure model.
The forces of the high-rise building are treated individually in the three directions, resulting in shear forces in
the x- and y-direction, and torsional moments in the rotational direction, which is referred to as ɔ-direction. The
forces are found by horizontal static equilibrium which can be derived from the equation of motion ( 1.16 ), thus
the internal forces ࡲ࢏࢔(ݐ) can be found by two expressions, in terms of the stiffness, damping and mass matrices
and the responses of the system:
ࡲ࢏࢔(ݐ) = ࡷ࢝(ݐ) + ࡯࢝ሶ(ݐ)
( 4.34 )
ࡲ࢏࢔(ݐ) = ࡲ(ݐ) െࡹ࢝ሷ(ݐ)
( 4.35 )
Where ࡲ(ݐ) is the external wind load matrix described in Section 5.2.2.
The sectional forces are obtained by either ( 4.34 ) or ( 4.35 ) and describes the full picture of the loading history,
where the peak of the time series is found by extreme value analysis as described in Section 4.1.2.2, and
summarized for each location h, leading to the final denotation for the peak load used as the static equivalent
load ܨ෠௛
ᇱ.
However, as it is interesting to see how the different components of the load contributes to the overall picture, as
it is seen for the static equivalent loads found in the frequency domain in Section 4.1.1, the peak sectional forces
can also be divided into a component of the mean force, a background component and a resonant component,
seen in ( 4.36 ):
ܨ෠௛
ᇱ= |ܨത௛| + ටܨ෠஻
ଶ

( 4.36 )
Where the mean ܨത௛ is determined from the external load matrix ܨ at each location h.
As all information regarding positive/negative is neglected due to the square-rooting of the dynamic
components, as also present when calculating in the frequency domain, the absolute value of the mean force is
used to avoid misleading results.
The background component ܨ෠஻௛is found by:
ܨ෠஻௛= หܨ෠௛െܨത௛ห
( 4.37 )
Where ܨ෠௛ is the peak of the fluctuations of the load time series found as the 78% fractile by extreme value
analysis, summed at each location h, and ܨത௛ is the mean of the external load at location h. Again the absolute
values are used, as the minus/plus part will be neglected when square-rooting.
==PAGE:96==

The resonant component ߂ࡲோis determined by Newton’s Second Law of motion, acceleration multiplied with
mass, which leads to:
߂ࡲோ= ࡹ࢝ሷ෡
( 4.38 )
Where ࡹ is the mass matrix, and ࢝ሷ෡ the peak acceleration also determined as the 78% fractile by extreme value
analysis. At each location h, the values are summed, which leads to the denotation of the additional force as ߂ܨோ௛
used in equation ( 4.36 ).
The two different approaches in the time domain derived above can be used to determine the floor-by-floor
loadings, which should lead to approximately the same results. However, the most comprehensive and thereby
also most extensive method is to analyse the direct time series of the sectional forces found by the equation of
motion. The creditability of the method is reasoned by the fact, that generalisations of the mean and peak load is
not included as it is all the measured discrete values which are comprised, including both positive and negative
values, which is disregarded in the component-dividing-analysis in both the frequency and time domain.

4.2 Peak resultant response
To find the absolute peak resultant responses of the structure, the contributions from both x-, y- and ɔ-direction
has to be combined.
The responses of the high-rise building are illustrated in Figure 4.6, where it is seen the response of the ɔ-
direction is measured at the corner of the cross section of the high-rise building, since this is where the largest
torsional response occurs. The responses of the x- and y-directions are in units of [m], [m/s] and [m/s2] and for the
ɔ-direction in [rad], [rad/s] and [rad/s2] for displacement, velocity and acceleration, respectively.
Figure 4.6: Response of displacement, velocity and acceleration in x-, y and ɔ-direction
To obtain the peak resultant response of the high-rise building, the responses in the three directions are
combined through a set of combination rules, which are different, dependent on if the time domain or frequency
domain approach is applied to obtain the responses in the three directions.
The advantages of the peak resultant response determined in the time domain is that the responses are combined
as time series, why the responses in the three directions are combined directly when they occur in each time step.
The peak resultant response, determined in the frequency domain, is found by the peaks in each mode and
direction, where the occurrences of the peaks are lost in the process. Furthermore the responses in the time
domain are obtained by the full system of all modes through Newmark time integration, where only a certain
number of modes are included in the frequency domain. Though the time domain method is quite time
consuming, it gives the most reliable results.
It is known the first sway mode of the system will be dominating, thus the responses are studied at the top of the
high-rise building, since this is where the maximum response occur in the x- and y-direction.
4.2.1 Time domain
The response of the ɔ-direction will, as mentioned above, have the largest response at the corner of the cross
section. The response of the ɔ-direction is separated into an x- and y-component, whereupon the superposition
laws are used to obtain the total response of the system. The principle of the combination of the responses is
shown in Figure 4.7.
Figure 4.7: Principle of combination of the responses in three directions in time domain
The positive direction of the torsional response is defined as counter clockwise direction. The x- and y-
component are found by the following trigonometric equations:

4.2.3 Comfort criteria
The motion-induced vibration, due to the dynamic response of a building, is perceived by humans if the
acceleration of the top floor exceeds a comfortable magnitude. This entails, that a certain threshold value has to
be implemented as a serviceability limit, if the structure is inhabited by humans. However, as the sensitivity of
motion is entirely individual, threshold values are a complex matter, as distinction also should be made between
the perception of motion and the tolerance of it.
The human perception of motion-induced vibration is measured in terms of the unit milli-g, why the peak
accelerations for the 36 wind directions are changed into the unit for comparable reasons, by the following:
ݓሷ[௠௜௟௟௜ି௚] =
ݓሷቂ௠
௦మቃ
݃
1000
( 4.55 )
Where the acceleration ݓሷ in the unit [m/s2] is divided by the ground acceleration ݃, to obtain the acceleration in
the unit milli-g.
As mentioned, sensitivity to motion is greatly dependent on individual tolerance, why a wide field of studies
have been conducted to determine the range of threshold values. The studies can generally be categorised under
three different types: field experiments and surveys of building occupants conducted in wind-excited tall
buildings; motion simulator and shake table experiments testing human test subjects; and field experiments
conducted in artificially excited buildings. (Kwok, Burton, & Hitchcock, 2009). From varies experiments,
similarity in the perception range has been implemented by different researchers, whereas W.H. Melbourne’s
comfort criteria for horizontal accelerations are widely used as a guidance, seen in Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Definition acceleration for comfort criteria (Koss, Wind Engineering - Part 7: Vibration Comfort and Control)

6

20 <
Furthermore there is a difference in comparing the threshold values for different return period of the wind
event, as a 5-year return period has a lower acceptable limit than for a return period of 20 years. This entails that
the threshold value decreases with the return period of the wind event, which correlation is derived by W.H.
Melbourne, and given as: (Koss, Wind Engineering - Part 7: Vibration Comfort and Control)
ݓሷ෡ൣ௠/௦మ൧= ඥ2 ݈݊(݂ଵܶ)ቆ0.68 + ݈݊൫ܴ௣൯
5

݂݋ݎ݂> 1ܪݖ
( 4.56 )
Where ݂ଵ is the fundamental frequency of the system, ܶ is the observation time equal to 10 minutes, and ܴ௣ is
the return period of the wind event.
In Figure 4.8 the threshold values are illustrated as dependent on the return periods of the wind events, where it
is evident that the peak horizontal acceleration criteria becomes lower for a lower the return period.

If the peak accelerations, determined from the wind tunnel test, indicate that the threshold value for human
comfort generally is not accommodated, mitigation measures might be necessary in order to minimize the
occupant perception of discomfort due to motion of the structure. The mitigation measures can either be
modifications of the structural system, the aerodynamic shape of the building as the design of the façade, or
installation of vibration control devices etc.., where pros and cons for each initiative has to be weighed in relation
to expenses, time and inconveniences.

Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response

4.3 Floor-by-floor loading
The fundamental objective of making a dynamic analysis of the wind loading on a structure, is to find the
important variables needed for the structural engineer, being the load effects consisting of the base overturning
moment, base shear and base torsional moment. Furthermore, the corresponding force- and torque distributions
with height are essential, in order to determine the sectional forces in the individual structural members. The
force- and torque distributions with height are therefore found as the floor-by-floor loading, which quantify the
maximum point load valid for each floor in the building in the x-, y- and ߮-direction.
The floor-by-floor loads originate from the static equivalent loads, which are found in the degrees of freedom,
where the external wind forces are acting. From the static equivalent loads, the weighted load distributions with
height for each direction are found, enabling the load to be determined floor by floor.
However, as the static equivalent loads are determined in each direction for 360 degrees, and the floor-by-floor
loadings only are interesting as the maximum load contribution, the loads have to be simplified to one single
maximum load per floor in each direction. The peak loads are determined from the circular-plots to ensure that
the maximum for each direction option is accounted for. The peak for each direction are determined by assessing
the base overturning moments and base torsional moments, as the forces inducing the maximum base moments
are the ones which should be designed for, when looking at the overall stability of the building.
This entails, that for determining the peak floor-by-floor loads in the x-direction, the peak of the base moment
ܯ௬ is evaluated, for the y-direction it is the peak of ܯ௫ and for torsion it is the peak of the base moment of ܯ௭.
As earlier mentioned, the maximum load case for each of the sway motions and twisting motion will not
necessarily occur at the same instant in time, which entails that each peak of the three loads should not be
treated as simultaneous loads. This yield at set of reduction factors, which can be used as load combinations
ensuring that the most critical occurrence of force-interactions is taking into account. The load combinations are
determined in a number of threes, where the specific direction from where the peak occurs at the base moments
for ܯ௫, ܯ௬and ܯ௭are used as a reference to find the specific percentage for each of ܨ௫, ܨ௬and ܯ௭. Say for
instance, that ܯ௬ has the peak at a direction of 0 degrees, entailing that ܨ௫ will be 100% at this direction, where
the values from ܨ௬ and ܯ௭ will have a different percentage at this specific direction, making a load combination
for maximum of ܨ௫. The same will be in evidence from the two other directions, entailing three overall load
combinations.
To account for the interaction from both maximum and minimum values of the three load cases, eight
combinations for min and max between the three loads are present, entailing 3ݔ8 = 24 load combinations in
total. In Table 4.3 the eight load combinations are illustrated, which can be used on the maximum for the specific
direction for each of the three loads ܨ௫, ܨ௬and ܯ௭.
Table 4.3: The eight possible load combinations for one specific direction

ܯ௭
ܨ௫.௠௔௫
ܨ௬.௠௔௫
ܯ௭.௠௔௫
ܨ௫.௠௔௫
ܨ௬.௠௔௫
ܯ௭.௠௜௡
ܨ௫.௠௔௫
ܨ௬.௠௜௡
ܯ௭.௠௔௫
ܨ௫.௠௔௫
ܨ௬.௠௜௡
ܯ௭.௠௜௡
ܨ௫.௠௜௡
ܨ௬.௠௔௫
ܯ௭.௠௔௫
ܨ௫.௠௜௡
ܨ௬.௠௔௫
