ܯ௭.௠௜௡
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 87 -
When determining the structural load effects as floor-by-floor loading with an appertaining set of load
combinations as explained in the abovementioned, it is done without regard for the meteorological data. This
method is called the “Non-directional Method” according to CTBUH (Irwin, Denoon, & Scott, 2013). Simply
described, the method assumes that the wind speed from a T-year return period occurs from all directions, which
is a somewhat conservative approach. The choice of the conservative calculation approach is due to lack of
available meteorological data because of missing knowledge of geographical or directional location. However, if
meteorological data is available and the location is known, it is advisable to add an additional set of reduction
factors to the load combinations to decrease construction cost, by adding the probability of a storm event or the
accurate wind speed to the direction in question.
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 4 – Methods of structural response
- 88 -
4.4 Aerodynamic forces
4.4.1 Vortex shedding
Wind buffeting at bluff bodies or high-rise buildings induces wind forces and moments in three directions. At
low wind velocities the wind at the structure will induce motions in the along-wind direction. For higher wind
velocities there will be motions in the along wind direction as well, but in addition the transverse wind will
induce motions in the across-wind direction of the structure. These motions are excited by a known
phenomenon called vortex shedding, where the vortices at the wake of the building becomes periodic and shed
from side to side of the building. The phenomenon of vortex shedding is illustrated in Figure 4.9.
Vortex shedding is not critical to the structure unless it matches the fundamental frequency of the structure,
whereby a lock-in effect is created, further discussed in the following section.
Figure 4.9: Phenomenon of vortex shedding (Taranath, 2005)
The frequency of the vortices being shed is found by expression of Strouhals number:
ܵݐ= ݂௦ܮ௪௜ௗ௧௛
ܷഥ௛
( 4.57 )
Where ݂௦ is the frequency of the vortex shedding, ܮ௪௜ௗ௧௛ is the width of the high-rise building and ܷഥ௛ is the
mean wind velocity at the top of the building. Strouhals number for a square cross section is found by Figure E.1
in (Eurocode, 2005) to ܵݐ= 0.12. The wind velocity at the top of the high-rise building in full-scale is 42.53 m/s,
which results in a vortex shedding frequency of ݂௦= 0.087ܪݖ. In a power spectrum of the crosswind loading on
the high-rise building, the vortex shedding frequency should easily be recognized as a peak if the phenomenon
occurs.
4.4.2 Lock-in
If the vortex shedding frequency becomes similar to the eigenfrequency of the structure, a resonance condition is
created, which can induce critical responses of the structure. This phenomenon is called lock-in, as the vortex
shedding has “locked-in” the eigenfrequency of the structure, and the vortices are controlled by this. When the
lock-in effect is created, a small increment in wind velocity will not affect the damaging lock-in phenomenon,
since the lock-in effect occur within 10% of the frequency. (Taranath, 2005). When the wind velocity is increased
significantly, the frequency of the vortex shedding will turn back to be controlled by the wind speed.
The critical mean wind velocity where lock-in occurs, is determined by the expression of Strouhals number in
( 4.57 ). By matching the frequency of the vortex shedding to the eigenfrequency of the structure, the critical
mean wind velocity for lock-in to occur at the high-rise building is found to 68.42 m/s. The critical mean wind
velocity is significantly higher than the wind velocity of 42.53 m/s at the top of the high-rise building in full-
scale, why lock-in is not taking into account in the investigation of the wind loading.
==PAGE:105==
- 89 -
Chapter 5
5 ANALYSIS OF WIND TUNNEL DATA
In the present chapter all measured data from the wind tunnel test are analysed. The chapter is divided into six
sections, where the first section is a verification of the wind tunnel data, which is conducted by the preliminary
tests to evaluate the quality of the boundary layer in wind tunnel. In the second and third section a thorough
description of the calculation routines is stated, where the methods described in Chapter 4 are implemented in
the analysis of the high-rise building. In the fourth and fifth section the analyses of the single high-rise building
and the twin arrangement of the high-rise building is given, including a thorough analysis of the drag force
coefficients, the mean average surface pressure coefficients, the pressure coefficients of the surfaces, the peak
accelerations, and lastly the floor-by-floor loadings with appertaining load combinations, where the theory in
Chapter 2 are compared where possible. In the last section a comparison between the two test configurations is
performed to summarize the differences of the two cases, completed with a combination proposal between the
two test configurations.
The three methods for determining the responses, described in Chapter 4, are thoroughly analysed for the single
high-rise building, where the advantages and disadvantages are presented, thus it seemed reasonable to only
analyse the twin arrangement of high-rise buildings for the most credible method. Furthermore, the mode
contribution is only discussed in the analysis of the single high-rise building, since the results for the twin
arrangement are identical. The estimations of the resulting drag force and overturning moment by Eurocode are
only compared to the single high-rise building, since no nearby buildings are taking into account in the
Eurocode estimations.
In the analysis of the twin arrangement emphasis is primarily put on the interference effects, which the
interference building is causing the principal building, where the differences between the single high-rise and
twin arrangement lastly are concluded.
5.1 Verification of wind tunnel data
5.1.1 Atmospheric boundary layer
The quality of the simulated boundary layer in the wind tunnel is evaluated, by both the normalized mean
velocity profile and the profile of the turbulence intensity, and then verified by the integral length scale
described in Section 5.1.2.
The normalized mean wind profile is determined by the mean wind velocity in the measured heights, given in
Table 3.2, divided by the reference wind velocity measured at the cobra probe in the centre of the wind tunnel,
which are located in a height of 0.5 m. The turbulence intensity profile is found by ( 2.6 ). The wind profile and
the turbulence intensity profile from the airflow measurement are plotted with the theoretical profiles discussed
in Section 2.3. The wind profile is shifted until best fit is found for the theoretical curves.
In Figure 5.1 the wind tunnel profile (WT) for both the normalised wind profile and turbulence intensity are seen
in comparison with the profiles from EC, AIJ, the Logarithmic law and the Power law. The wind tunnel profile is
initially plotted in Appendix 5.1 with all theoretical wind profiles for each existing terrain categories, to
determine which category had the best fit for each code and recommendation. The most evident match was
found for category II in EC, described as areas with low vegetation; category II in AIJ, described as open areas
with few obstructions as grassland or agricultural field; and lastly as category III, described as suburban terrain,
in the logarithmic and power law represented by Holmes.
As described in Section 3.1.2.1, the boundary layer plates in the wind tunnel, provided by FORCE technology,
should reflect a city-profile. However, the test measurements of the wind profile, shows that the boundary layer
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 90 -
corresponds to a rather smoother terrain compared to a city roughness. This is possibly due to the fact, that the
maximum model scale recommended by FORCE is 1:400 (Geyer, Technical Procedure TP 187.1.210 - Pressure
Tests, 2009). The model scale reflects how the wind profile is designed, as a small scale results in a more curved
graph compared to a larger geometrical scale, which induces a steeper curve, and as the model scale used is 1:575
for a full scale building of 350 m’s height, a city-profile is hard to obtain with the roughness blocks provided.
Figure 5.1: Comparison of WT wind profile and turbulence intensity
 to different terrain categories from codes and recommendations
From Figure 5.1 it is clearly seen that the curvature of the WT wind profile, marked by black, matches quite
flawlessly with especially the Power law, marked by dark blue, when reaching a height above 75m. The lower
part of the profile does conversely not fit any of the profiles, which indicates that the roughness of the boundary
layer in the wind tunnel does not reduce the wind speed at the base to the predicted extend. Furthermore, the
turbulence intensity from the wind tunnel does not match any of the theoretical profiles. Normally the slope of
the turbulence intensity is quite conspicuous at the base, due to the fact that the flow is reduced and the surface
roughness generates high turbulence, leaving a profound turbulence intensity at the very base. However, as the
standard deviations from the measured wind velocities do not distinctly vary along the height of the wind
tunnel, and in addition possess the largest values at the mid, the slope of the turbulence intensity does not follow
any theoretical profile. Both the wind profile and the turbulence intensity indicate that the constructed
boundary layer does not flagrantly represent a theoretical terrain type, which can give rise to uncertainties in the
accuracy of the expected results.
The profile for the normalised wind speed matches the curve of the Power law when assessing the wind at higher
points, which has interest when considering the high-rise building. When reference is made to Table 2.8 in
Section 2.3.1, it can be concluded from each code and recommendation, that the mean wind velocities at a
reference height corresponding to the top of the building are similar with small deviations, where the Power law
and Logarithmic law are slightly higher than the mean wind velocities determined by Eurocode and AIJ.
As mentioned is the Power law the best fit to the WT wind profile, together with the fact, that choosing the
highest magnitude of the four determined mean wind velocities will be on the safe side, the choice of reference
mean wind velocity at the top of the high-rise results in a magnitude of 42.53 m/s.
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 91 -
5.1.2 Integral length scale
The scale of turbulence is referring to the geometrical scale of the size of the vortices, generated from the flow-
processing unit in the wind tunnel, in relation to the size of vortices in the flow in nature from the
corresponding terrain category, determined in Section 5.1.1.
The size of average vortices in the wind tunnel is referred to as the integral length scale ܮ௨௫, whereas Eurocode
refers to it as the turbulent length scale for natural winds ܮ(ݖ), both mentioned in Chapter 2. These two
parameters are compared to each other, verifying that the size factor between the geometric scale of the model
ɉ௚௘௢ and the turbulence in the flow is reasonable, based on Cook’s recommendations.
The integral length scale is determined by means of the power spectrum of the recorded measurements. The
power spectrum images how the energy is dissipated in the flow at different frequencies, which is explained in
terms of the vortices dissipating energy when dissolving into smaller vortices. The value of the integral length
scale is found by plotting the spectral density function of the turbulence expressed in terms of ܮ௨௫ as a shape
parameter, found by ( 2.8 ) in Chapter 2. The right side of the equation is used as the fitting curve in the
calculated spectrum, where the constants for either Kaimal, Von Kármán or Fichtl-McVehil are used as best fit,
where the parameters are given in Table 2.2. The value of ܮ௨௫ is varied until the calculated error, which
represents the difference between the actual spectrum to the fitted curves, are as insignificant as possible, and the
peak of the curve fits with the peak of the spectrum.
The error factors are found by equation ( 5.1 ).
݁ݎݎ݋ݎ= ට෍หܵ௞௔௜/௞௔௥/௙௜௖െܵ௨(݂)ห
ଶ
( 5.1 )
Where ܵ௨(݂) is the actual spectrum recorded as a time series in the wind tunnel, and ܵ௞௔௜/௞௔௥/௙௜௖ is the
theoretical spectrums determined by ( 2.8 ). The power spectrum of the velocity fluctuations is found in
Appendix 5.2.
Figure 5.2 shows the wind spectrum for data from 1st run of 600 Hz at the top position of the high-rise building.
The spectrum is merely plotted with the three “shape functions” with minimum error to clarify how the
different functions describes the shape. The error of the different functions has a negligible difference, why only
one of the functions is chosen for further comparison. As Eurocode is based on values for more natural winds,
which is most comparable with Kaimal’s parameters, the value of ܮ௨௫ is chosen in relation to Kaimal.
Figure 5.2: Wind spectrum with shape functions
The value of ܮ௨௫is found for Kaimal’s parameters at two different heights, namely the top of the building and at
60% of the height. These values can then be compared to the turbulent length scale ܮ(ݖ), found by ( 2.28 ) in
Section 2.2.1.6. The ratio between ܮ௨௫and ܮ(ݖ) represents the geometric length scale ɉ௅ of the simulated flow,
==PAGE:108==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 92 -
which should match the geometric scale of the model to validate that the design loads can be used as an estimate
of the real wind loads. However, the ratio rarely matches the geometric scale of the model, but can be considered
sufficiently accurate if the values do not deviate with a factor more than three for global forces and moments
and two for local pressures on façade and roof cladding elements, according to Cook. (Koss, Wind Engineering -
Part 8: Simulation).
To verify the determined value of ܮ௨௫, the power spectrum of all seven data samplings are assessed, where the
sampling frequency of the measurements are listed in Table 3.3 in Section 3.3.1.2. However, the measurements of
both ID3 and ID4 did not return credible spectrums, which could indicate that the sampling frequency limit of
the Cobra Probe is exceeded around 2500 Hz (Cobra Probe, 2015). The most reliable results with lowest error
margin are found at a sampling frequency of 1250 Hz for both the full height of the model and at 60% of the
height. The wind spectrums plotted with the approximated shape function of Kaimal’s parameters for both
heights are seen in Figure 5.3.
Figure 5.3: a) Wind spectrum at top of building, b) Wind spectrum at 60% of the height of the building
The turbulent length scale ܮ(ݖ) is determined by ( 2.28 ) in Chapter 2 at the given heights. These values are
divided by the integral length scale ܮ௨௫, found from each of the wind spectra, which provide the geometrical
length scale ɉ௅, which can be compared to the geometrical scale ɉ௚௘௢. In Table 5.1 it is seen, that the factor
between the two scales, both at top and 60% of the building height, is less than the limit of three, which is the
acceptable deviation factor according to Cook. This means that the integral length scale is “verified” and the
model set up is considered acceptable for the choice of model scale and terrain category.
Table 5.1: Geometric scale factor

5.1.3 Reynolds test
The Reynolds test is, as described in Section 3.1.3.2, conducted to determine in which wind velocity range the
turbulence is presupposed as fully established. The test provides the experimental minimum value of the velocity
limit the wind tunnel can be run at, which further can be compared to the theoretical limit, known as the
Reynolds onset value, determined as 1.47 m/s in Section 2.4.1.3. The test is conducted at teen different wind speeds
listed in Table 3.4. The wind speeds are recorded at the pitot-tube at the side of the wind tunnel at a reference
height of 0.5 m and is multiplied with the velocity factor for the top of the building ݔ௧௢௣ in Table 5.2.
To assess the minimum velocity limit at the top of the building, the drag force coefficients at the different
velocities are determined by ( 2.10 ) in Section 2.1.2.2, where the drag force ܨௗ is a summation of all pressure taps
in the wind direction for each run. By plotting in Appendix 5.3 the calculated drag force coefficient dependent on
the wind velocity with an angle of attack of 0 degrees, the lower limit of the similarity range is found to be above
5 m/s for ܥி௬ when the curve plateaus, as evident in Figure 5.4. This corresponds to a value of 5.35 m/s for the top
of the building. Below this velocity the drag coefficients are not stable at the same level, which indicate that the
wind has not created a full turbulent flow that will behave as predicted.
Figure 5.4: Drag force coefficients for determination of Reynolds test
When determining the minimum velocity scale mentioned in Section 2.4.1.3, it becomes evident that the
theoretical minimum value from the Reynold onset is exceeded by the experimental Reynold test, why the
minimum velocity scale experimentally is found to ݑ௧௢௣.௠௜௡.௠௢ௗ௘௟= 5.35݉/ݏ.

Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data

5.2 Pre-analysis of wind tunnel data
5.2.1 Pressure of full-scale high-rise building
After the data has been pre-processed though the calculation routines described in Section 3.3.4 the data is ready
to be analysed. The data is loaded and treated individually for each of the runs in the wind tunnel tests, meaning
three measurements per 10 degrees, resulting in a total number of 108 runs.
The corrected data from Section 3.3.4 for each of the tests, of the single high-rise building and the twin
arrangement of high-rise buildings, consist of a 108 reference pressures and 108 matrices with time histories for
216 pressure taps, which each has a length of 31102 time steps. The analyses of the two test setups are performed
in the same way, why the following are only explained for one of the tests. The data processing is found in
Appendix 5.3.
To be able to determine the wind forces acting on the surface of the full-scale high-rise building, the pressure
coefficients are used. The pressure coefficients are unitless parameters, which has the same magnitude at the
surfaces of the full-scale high-rise building and the scaled model. The pressure coefficients ܥ௣ are found from the
pressures measured at the scaled high-rise model ܲ௠௢ௗ௘௟ as:
ܥ௣= ܲ௠௢ௗ௘௟

model.

Since the high-rise building is symmetric, the FEM-model of core + outrigger system is used in both the x- and y-
direction, and the stick FEM model is used for the ɔ-direction. The resulting wind loading forces ࡲ࢞.ࢎ(ݐ), ࡲ࢟.ࢎ(ݐ)
and ࡹࢠ.ࢎ(ݐ) are applied to the two finite element models as seen in Figure 5.6.
Figure 5.6: External wind loading forces ࡲ࢞.ࢎ, ࡲ࢟.ࢎ and ࡹࢠ.ࢎ on finite element models
The external wind loading forces ࡲ࢞.ࢎ(ݐ) and ࡲ࢟.ࢎ(ݐ) are assembled in a 75 x 31102 matrix for both the x- and y-
direction, referred to as ࡲ࢞(ݐ) and ࡲ࢟(ݐ), and a 9 x 31102 matrix for the external torsional moment in the ɔ-
direction, referred to as ࡹࢠ(ݐ), to fit the necessary size of the external force in the modal analysis. Since the
external wind loading force are applied to the core in the frame FEM model, the assembled wind load matrices
ࡲ࢞(ݐ) and ࡲ࢟(ݐ) are zeros with only time histories of the horizontal degrees of freedom at node 2-9, as seen in
Figure 5.6 and Appendix 5.5 and 5.7. The assembled load matrix ࡹࢠ(ݐ) of the stick FEM model, consist of the
nine resulting torsional moment time histories ࡹࢠ.ࢎ(ݐ) in the nine torsional degrees of freedom.
5.3 Calculation routines
As mentioned in Section 5.2.1 each of the tests of the high-rise buildings are performed three times for 360
degrees in steps of 10 degrees. The angle of wind direction is shown in Figure 3.10 in Section 3.3.2.3, where it is
seen that the interference model is placed in front of the surface pressure model for an angle of wind direction at
0 degrees, in the twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings.
5.3.1 Force coefficients
The drag force coefficient, represented in Section 2.1.2.2, gives an overview of the forces acting on the surfaces,
where it is easily seen which of the static forces in the two directions of the building that is dominating. The drag
force coefficients are unitless, thus the drag force coefficient of the surface pressure model is equal to the high-
rise building in full-scale.
The mean drag force coefficients are determined by ( 2.10 ) in Appendix 5.3 for each of the 108 runs, with
individually reference wind velocities, whereupon the drag force coefficients are plotted in a circular plot for 360
degrees in steps of 10 degrees as wind directions.
It should be noted that since the drag force coefficients only are mean values, the fluctuations of the wind flow is
not observed, why the drag force coefficient are only useful for an overview.

5.3.2 Pressure coefficients
As mentioned in Section 5.2.1 the pressure coefficients of the surfaces are unitless, why they are equal to the
magnitude of the pressure coefficients for the surface pressure model and the high-rise building in full-scale. The
pressure coefficients are found as time series for each of the 216 pressure taps at the surface pressure model in
Appendix 5.3 by Eq. ( 5.4 ).
The surface pressure coefficients are shown visual as contour plots for interesting wind directions for mean,
minimum peak and maximum peak coefficients, and a clear overview of pressure and suction areas of the
surfaces is observed. The plots are created in Appendix 5.4.
The plots are treated individually for each of the 36 wind directions. An extreme value analysis of each pressure
tap for three runs are performed, where each of the three time histories are divided into subseries with a duration
of 10 minutes in real time, giving a total number of 48 subseries. In each of the 48 subseries the mean, standard
deviation and maximum and minimum value are found. To find the 78% fractile of the minimum and
maximum values as described in Section 4.1.2.2, the Gumbel distribution is used to perform a statistical analysis
of the 48 minimum and maximum values, by using the fractile fraction method in ( 4.32 ). After the extreme
value analysis, the mean, minimum peak and maximum peak of each of the 216 taps at the high-rise building
are obtained.
By using the excel sheet in Appendix 3.1 the position of the pressure taps at the surfaces is given, and four
matrices with tap numbers for each surface and one matrix for the top of the high-rise building are created in
Appendix 5.4. Likewise five matrices are found for the position of each tap in the x- y- and z-direction, the
appertaining area, and at last five matrices with the pressure coefficients are created.
The five surfaces are plotted with the position and pressure coefficients matrices by the built-in contour function
in Matlab for the interesting wind directions.
The interference factor between the single high-rise building and the principal building in the twin arrangement
are determined by ( 2.12 ), where the mean, maximum peak and minimum peak matrices for the two
measurements are divided with each other, to obtain contour plots of the interference factors ܫܨ஼ҧ೛, ܫܨେ෡೛ and
ܫܨେ෱೛.
At last the total mean average pressure coefficient for each of the surfaces in each of the 10 degrees steps, are
found by multiplying the mean pressure coefficients with the tributary areas and dividing with the total area of
one surface, whereupon the mean average pressure coefficients are plotted in steps of 10 degrees for 360 degrees as
wind directions in a circular plot.
5.3.3 Responses of the system
5.3.3.1
Responses through Newmark time integration in the time domain
The responses of the modal analysis in the time domain are found by Newmark time integration. To prepare the
external wind loading time histories ࡲ࢞(ݐ), ࡲ࢟(ݐ) and ࡹࢠ(ݐ), shown in Figure 5.6, for the integration algorithm,
the time step duration is considered. For stabile solutions of the responses, the minimum size of time step is
given as: (Koss, Wind Engineering - Part 5: Low-rise buildings)
߂ݐ௖௔௟௖൑ܶ௠௜௡
20
( 5.9 )
Where ܶ݉݅݊ is the period of the lowest eigenfrequency. As the lowest eigenfreqency of the high-rise building is
0.14 Hz, the period has a magnitude of ܶ݉݅݊= 7.14ݏ. This implies that the size of the time steps should have a
maximum length ofȟݐ௖௔௟௖= 0.35ݏ. The time series are measured with a frequency of 625 Hz, resulting in a
mean time step in full-scale of ߂ݐ= 0.31ݏ. This shows that the time series should have a sufficiently low time
step. However, the time step size is still subdivided into a smaller time steps, as it was observed this gave a more
stable solution. To do so the built-in Matlab function spline is applied in Appendix 5.5. The time series are splined
==PAGE:114==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 98 -
with three, meaning the time step size becomes ߂ݐ௦= 0.10ݏ, leading to a total number of 93305 time steps in the
time histories. The principle of the spline function is shown in Figure 5.7, where it is seen the function creates a
more curved crests in the time series.
Figure 5.7: Principle of spline function
The initial conditions of a time integration is highly important, as it defines the initial responses of the system,
and can induce misleading responses at the beginning of the time series. To generate correct initial conditions the
time histories ࡲ࢞(ݐ), ࡲ࢟(ݐ) and ࡹࢠ(ݐ) are mirrored and placed in front of the original time series, resulting in a
time series of 186610 time steps. The Newmark time integration is conducted on the long time series and
afterwards cut off at real time ݐ= 0ݏ, where the correct initial conditions are visible. The initial conditions for
the response time series are seen in Figure 5.8 as an outline, where it is seen that the initial conditions for the
three directions are completely different, which are impossible to create, without mirroring the time series.
Figure 5.8: Principle of initial conditions of response time series
The Newmark time integration is performed in Matlab with a Newmark function given in Appendix 5.6, which
follows the steps described in Section 4.1.2.1. The function requires the following input data: The full stiffness-,
damping- and mass matrix, the initial conditions for the displacement and velocity, the time step size and
number of time steps, and the external wind force in a load vector. The responses of the system are created in the
Newmark function, in terms of time histories of the displacement ݓ(ݐ), velocity ݓሶ(ݐ) and acceleration ݓሷ(ݐ), by
determining the prediction steps and correction steps, explained in Section 4.1.2.1.
The responses of the high-rise building are treated individually for the three directions for each of the 108 runs.
As mentioned in Section 5.2.2 the x- and y-direction are analysed with the frame FEM model, and the ɔ-
direction is analysed with the stick FEM model. The responses for each of the 108 runs result in three
displacement-, velocity- and acceleration time series. The responses of the twin arrangement of high-rise
==PAGE:115==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 99 -
buildings for 180 degrees are seen as a principle of the response time histories in Figure 5.9 and found in
Appendix 5.5. From these responses it is possible to find the peak accelerations by extreme value analysis, which
leads to the resulting floor-by-floor loadings.
Figure 5.9: Displacement, velocity and acceleration response in three directions
5.3.3.2
Responses through the spectral method in frequency domain
Unlike the time domain where the responses are found in terms of times series, the responses in the frequency
domain are obtained as peak values. The peak responses in terms of accelerations of the modal analysis in the
frequency domain are obtained by the spectral method described in Section 4.1.1.1. The responses are treated
individually in each of the x-, y- and ɔ-direction for each of the 108 runs and found in Appendix 5.7.
At first the external wind loading forces ࡲ࢞(ݐ), ࡲ࢟(ݐ) and ࡹࢠ(ݐ), described in Section 5.2.2, are transformed into
modal loads by ( 4.2 ) for each directions. From the modal loads the loading spectrum for each mode j are
obtained. The loading spectrum is obtained in Matlab by the built-in function pwelch that calculates a power
spectral density of a given time series. It is necessary to remove the mean value of the time series to calculate a
power spectral density, which is performed by using the built-in Matlab function detrend. The number of
sample points used in the calculation is defined by the filter length ݂݂݊ݐ, which should be an integer to obtain
fast results in the fast fourier transformation and not exceed the number of data points. A high number of filter
length allows the spectrum to see the low frequencies, where a low number of filter length removes the visibility
of the wave lengths. As the high-rise building has a low eigenfrequency, the filter depth is chosen to the
maximum possible number, to make the low frequencies visible in the spectrum, resulting in ݂݂݊ݐ= 30720.
Furthermore the pwelch function requires the sample frequency in full-scale, which is known as ߂ݐ= 0.31ݏ.
The geometric variance of the area underneath the spectrum is obtained by the build-in Matlab function trapz,
from where the standard deviation of the modal load ߪொೕ
כ is obtained. At last the loading spectrum for the mode
is read off at the present frequency, to obtain the spectral modal ordinate ܵொೕ
כ(݂௝).
The number of investigated modes is naturally halt as there is no spectrum to read off the spectral modal
ordinate at frequencies above approximately 1.7 Hz, corresponding to a frequency of 2߂ݐ, thus only two modes
are considered in all calculations.
The peak acceleration vector for all degrees of freedom in each mode is obtained by ( 4.7 ), and then combined
through the CQC rule in ( 4.44 ), to obtain the peak accelerations ݓሷ෡ in the x-, y- and ɔ-direction.
==PAGE:116==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 100 -
5.3.4 Peak accelerations
5.3.4.1
Time domain
The responses in the x-, y- and ɔ-direction, obtained in Section 5.3.3.1, are combined through the method
described in Section 4.2.1, where the torsional response is divided into an x- and y-component, whereupon the
total response time history for each of the 108 runs is obtained by ( 4.43 ) in Section 4.2.1 and found in Appendix
5.5.
The peak accelerations ݓሷ෡ at the top of the high-rise building for both the total acceleration time histories and the
x-, y- and ɔ-direction acceleration time histories are determined by performing four extreme value analyses.
Each of the 108 runs for the 36 wind directions are divided into subseries of 10 minutes duration, giving a total
number of 48 subseries for each wind direction of 10 degrees steps. In each of the 48 subseries the minimum and
maximum peaks are found. Through a statistical analysis with Gumbel distribution of the 48 extreme values,
the mean, 22% minimum peak and 78% maximum peak values are obtained by the fractile fraction method in (
4.32 ).
5.3.4.2
Frequency domain
The peak accelerations ݓሷ෡ in the frequency domain are obtained for the x-, y- and ɔ-direction in the 108 runs in
Section 5.3.3.2, whereupon the peak accelerations in the three directions are combined through the calculation
routines described in Section 4.2.2. The standard deviations and correlation coefficients between the modes and
directions are determined to obtain the peak acceleration in the x- and y-direction through ( 4.48 ) and ( 4.49 ),
which takes the torsional contribution into account, see Appendix 5.7.
By determining the correlation coefficient ߩ௫௬ between the x- and y-direction, the effect of correlation between
the two directions is visible. It becomes evident that the two directions are fully correlated as the correlation
coefficient is always equal to หߩ௫௬ห= 1, which is natural as the modes are identical in the x- and y-direction. This
implies that the peak accelerations in the frequency domain are always obtained by ( 4.53 ).
The peak accelerations in the frequency domain is determined for three runs in each wind direction, thus it is
assessed to choose the maximum value of the three peaks as the peak value for the directions, since the dispersion
of the peaks are relatively high.
5.3.4.3
Peak accelerations with a return period of 5, 10 and 20 years
The peak accelerations from the storm event with a return period of 50 years are scaled to a return period of 5, 10
and 20 years, to compare the peak accelerations to the threshold values of human comfort.
From the return period of 50 years the peak acceleration of the high-rise model can be found from the
acceleration scaling law in Table 2.11. From the basic wind velocities in Table 2.9 with return periods of 5, 10 and
20 years, the velocity scales are established, and from the acceleration scale the peak accelerations with return
periods of 5, 10 and 20 years are obtained.

5.3.5 Static equivalent loads
5.3.5.1
Compounding of static equivalent loads
To obtain the static equivalent loads for all three methods, the forces are determined in all 75 degrees of freedom
for the frame FEM model in the x- and y-direction. The forces in the horizontal degrees of freedom, seen in
Figure 1.4 c), are assembled and assessed in nine heights to obtain the total static equivalent loads, seen in Figure
5.10, from which the floor-by-floor loadings can be estimated.
The torsional moments in the ɔ-direction are determined by means of the stick FEM model, which only
contains nine torsional degrees of freedom, thus no selection of the degrees of freedom is necessary.
Figure 5.10: Nine static equivalent loads in nine heights of the high-rise building
5.3.5.2
Full solution in time domain
The internal forces of the high-rise building are treated individually in the three directions, resulting in
horizontal forces in the x- and y-direction, and torsional moments in the ɔ-direction. The forces and torsional
moments are obtained for the 108 runs as time series by the static equilibrium in ( 4.34 ). The internal force time
series ࡲ࢏࢔.࢞(ݐ) and ࡲ࢏࢔.࢟(ݐ)in the x- and y-direction are determined for the full matrix of all degrees of freedom,
as described in Section 5.3.5.1, to obtain the total horizontal forces ࡲ࢞.࢚࢕࢚(ݐ) and ࡲ࢟.࢚࢕࢚(ݐ) in the nine heights as
seen in Figure 5.10. The torsional moments in the ɔ-direction ࡹ࢏࢔.ࢠ(ݐ) is found in the same manner by the static
equilibrium. See Appendix 5.5.
To obtain the base drag force time histories ࡲ࢞.࢈ࢇ࢙ࢋ(ݐ) and ࡲ࢟.࢈ࢇ࢙ࢋ(ݐ) and torsional base moment ࡹࢠ.࢈ࢇ࢙ࢋ(ݐ), the
total horizontal forces and torsional moments are summarised. From the total horizontal forces in the nine
heights the overturning base moments ࡹ࢞.࢈ࢇ࢙ࢋ(ݐ) and ࡹ࢟.࢈ࢇ࢙ࢋ(ݐ) are obtained as well.
The resulting static equivalent loads ࡲ෡࢞, ࡲ෡࢟and ࡹ෡ࢠof the nine heights and five base forces are obtained by
performing an extreme value analysis in the x-, y- and ɔ-direction for each of the 36 wind directions. The
extreme value analysis of the forces are performed in the same way as for the accelerations, described in Section
5.3.4.1, where a 22%- and 78% fractile are found by the Gumbel distribution of the 48 minimum and maximum
peak values.
5.3.5.3
Component-dividing-method in time domain
From the obtained maximum and minimum peak accelerations found by extreme value analysis in Section
5.3.4.1, the resonant contribution from the inertia forces ߂ࡲோ is determined by ( 4.38 ) in the x- and y-direction
for the full matrix of the FEM model. The horizontal forces in each degree of freedom are assembled as explained
in Section 5.3.5.1 in the nine heights for each of 36 wind directions. The resonant contributions in the ɔ-direction

The mean force ܨത௛and background force ܨ෠஻௛ are obtained from the external wind loading forces ࡲ࢞.ࢎ(ݐ) and
ࡲ࢟.ࢎ(ݐ) in the x- and y-direction and from the external wind loading torsional moments ࡹࢠ.ࢎ(ݐ) in the ɔ-
direction, in each of the nine heights, described in Section 5.2.2. An extreme value analysis is performed for each
of the nine heights of the three external wind loads for three runs per wind direction, resulting in 48 maximum
and minimum peak values, from where the 22%- and 78% fractile are found by the Gumbel distribution through
( 4.32 ) for each of the 36 wind directions. The resulting maximum and minimum background forces are then

At last the static equivalent loads ࡲ෡࢞, ࡲ෡࢟and ࡹ෡ࢠ, contains the loads for the nine heights as shown in Figure 5.10,
are obtained by equation ( 4.36 ), where the maximum absolute value of the minimum and maximum forces of
the resonant and background contribution is used to obtain the resulting forces.
5.3.5.4
Component-dividing-method frequency domain
From the 108 obtained peak accelerations in the frequency domain, explained in Section 5.3.4.2, the resonant
contribution from the inertia forces ߂ࡲோin the x- and y-directions are found for the full matrix of the frame
FEM model for the first two modes by ( 4.8 ), and the torsional resonant contribution are similarly found for the
first two modes by the stick FEM model. The horizontal resonant forces, obtained in each degree of freedom in
the x- and y-direction, are assembled as explained in Section 5.3.5.1 in the nine heights for each of 36 wind
directions. The resonant forces for the two first modes are then combined through the CQC rule, similar to the
equation in ( 4.44 ).
The mean forces ܨത௛for each of the nine heights in each of the 108 runs are found from the external wind loads
ࡲ࢞.ࢎ(ݐ), ࡲ࢟.ࢎ(ݐ) and ࡹࢠ.ࢎ(ݐ), which are shown in Figure 5.6. The background forces ܨ෠஻௛for each of the nine
heights in each of the 108 runs are determined from the standard deviation and peak factor by means of ( 4.10 ).
The static equivalent loads ࡲ෡࢞, ࡲ෡࢟and ࡹ෡ࢠobtained by the spectral method in the frequency domain are found
by ( 4.9 ) in each of the nine heights for three runs in the 36 wind directions. Since the dispersion of the peaks is
relatively high, the maximum of the three peak values in each wind direction are chosen as the resulting static
equivalent load.
5.3.6 Floor-by-floor loadings
5.3.6.1
Determination of floor-by-floor loadings
The floor-by-floor loadings are determined by the static equivalent loads ࡲ෡࢞, ࡲ෡࢟and ࡹ෡ࢠ, determined by the full
solution in the time domain, described in Section 5.3.5.2. The static equivalent loads, which are containing the
loads in the 36 wind directions for the nine heights, are changed for each wind direction from point loads into
uniformly distributed loads along the height, whereupon the built-in Matlab function spline is used, to obtain a
more smooth distribution of the uniformly distributed loads.
From the uniformly distributed load in each direction, the floor-by-floor loading at the 82 stories of the high-rise
building, see Section 1.2, are found by integrating over the appertaining 82 heights, see Appendix 5.5.
5.3.6.2
Load combinations of floor-by-floor loadings
The load combination factors are found from the three maximum and minimum peak base moments of the
high-rise building in the 36 wind directions, determined in Section 5.3.5.2. By dividing the 36 maximum and
minimum peaks in the x- y- and ɔ-direction with the maximum occurrence of a maximum or minimum peak
in the three directions, the base moments are transformed into load combinations in terms of percentages from
the peak occurrence. At last the 24 load combinations can be defined by the method described in Section 4.3.
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 103 -
5.4 Single high-rise building
5.4.1 External wind load effect
5.4.1.1
Drag coefficients
The assessment of the drag force coefficients is done in comparison to ESDU and Eurocode, presented in Chapter
2. The drag force is a representation of how the overall wind force affects the building when hitting the surface
and can be described as a measure of the air resistance of the structure.
The drag coefficients are determined by ( 2.10 ) in Section 2.1.2.2 for all measurements and calculated as described
in Section 5.3.1. The coefficients are plotted in a circular plot for 360 degrees to account for how the drag force in
both directions, x and y, varies with the angle of attack. In Figure 5.11 the wind attack angle in relation to the
coordinate system is illustrated followed by the circular plot of the drag coefficients.
When the drag force is measured to a magnitude in the region of one, it entails that the approaching flow is in a
standstill where a stagnation point at the windward face can be detected. This occurrence is easily recognised at
the plateaus of the curves at approximately 1 and -1 for both ܥி௫ and ܥி௬, where the wind attacks perpendicular
to the surface of face A, C, B and D for 0, 90, 180 and 270 degrees, respectively. Furthermore it is seen, that for an
angle of attack of 0 degrees ܥி௫has no resulting value, as the wind attacks parallel to the surface of face C and D
creating no drag at all. However, as soon as the angle of attack is inclined, a resulting drag in the negative x-
direction occurs, due to uneven vortex shedding, having its maximum at about 15 degrees before changing to a
resulting drag in the positive x-direction and equalling ܥி௬at 45 degrees as expected. The symmetry of the
circular plot is an indicator of the symmetry of the building, as the shape of the curve reiterates itself for every
90 degrees, which verifies the measurements, as it is in coherence with expectations for a symmetrical building.
Figure 5.11: Drag force coefficients in x- and y-direction for the single high-rise for 360 degrees
Looking in comparison to the theory described in Chapter 2, the force coefficients on a surface mounted block
for a free stream presented by ESDU, identical tendencies in the curves for both theory and measurements are
seen. The graph in Figure 2.10 presented in Chapter 2 for ESDU is more or less equal in shape for an aspect ratio
of 5 to the drag coefficient of both ܥி௫ and ܥி௬ presented in Figure 5.11 for the first 45 degrees. The small increase
in value seen for ܥி௬ at 10 degrees is also detected at the measurements in Figure 5.11 when zooming, indicating
that all tendencies described in theory are discovered correspondingly in the wind tunnel test measurements,
verifying the wind effects measured on the model are reliable.
==PAGE:120==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 104 -
The tendencies of the curve match quite flawlessly with theory, however, the actual magnitude in the value of
the force coefficient is somewhat lower than the expected value. For a cubic form, hence an aspect ratio of around
one, theory states that the drag coefficient also should have a value around one at the windward face, with
increasing value with increasing aspect ratio. The drag force coefficient from Eurocode are given by ( 1.22 ) in
Chapter 2 with a value of 1.45 determined in Section 2.2.1.4, which is also significantly higher than the one given
from the measurements. This could be due to the fact, that the neither ESDU nor Eurocode account for the fact
of a turbulent wind, which cause the summarized drag force to decrease in value.
5.4.1.2
Pressure coefficients
In same manner as the drag coefficients, the assessment of the pressure coefficients is done in comparison with
ESDU and Eurocode, presented in Chapter 2. In the contrary to the drag coefficient, which is an illustrative
measure of the total drag of the building, the pressure coefficients accounts for the pressure distribution on each
surface. The surface distribution of the pressure coefficients is used to assess if the different wind phenomena are
occurring in concordance with theory.
The pressure coefficients are determined by ( 2.9 ) in Section 2.1.2.1 for all measurements and calculated as
described in Section 5.3.2. Four averaged mean pressure coefficients summed for all pressure points on each
surface are plotted in a circular plot for 360 degrees to see how the different surfaces interact when the model is
rotated one round, changing the angle of attack of the wind. In Figure 5.12 the circular plot of the four force
coefficients is illustrated in a), and outlined for 45 degrees in b).
Figure 5.12: a) Mean averaged pressure coefficients for each surface for 360 degrees for the single high-rise,
b) Mean averaged pressure coefficients for each surface outlined for 45 degrees for the single high-rise
The circular plot is evidently symmetrical every 45 degrees, verifying the measurements of a symmetrical
building, as evident in the plot of the drag force as well. Looking at the plot for 45 degrees, the similarity of the
shape in comparison to Figure 2.12 from ESDU in Section 2.1.2.2 is obvious. The windward face, A, has an
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 105 -
expected resulting pressure slowly decreasing with the inclination towards 45 degrees. The along-wind faces, C
and D, have an equal magnitude of negative pressure at 0 degrees, but immediately varying as the inclination is
tilted towards face C. The resulting negative pressure for face B is smaller than the two along-wind faces, which is
explicable as this is the sheltered face, but the magnitude equals the value for face D as soon the inclination of 45
degrees is reached. The same phenomenon is seen for face A and C at 45 degrees, as the wind attacks at the corner,
making both faces equally windward.
Comparing the values of the pressure coefficients, the same tendency as for the drag force is seen, as the
development in the values matches quite flawlessly with theory, whereas the overall value for the windward face
is somewhat lower than theory states, which can be explained in the same manner as for the drag force.
Moreover, the summarised pressure coefficients of a surface of 350 m height does not give a particularly
comprehensive picture, why it is far more interesting to look at the pressure distribution on the surfaces, where
both the mean, minimum and maximum values are illustrated by contour lines plotted in Matlab.
In Table 5.4 the pressure coefficients, plotted for a wind direction of 0 degrees, are seen for both the mean,
minimum and maximum values for all four walls and the top of the building. Notice should be made on the
colour bars apparent for each wall, as the range varies dependent on the pressure range and the wall assessed. The
division is made due to the varying range of pressure, so all phenomena can be illustrated clearly. Generally the
blue colour represents the lowest value of the pressure range and red the highest.
Comparing the contour lines with the wind tunnel model affected by a boundary layer flow corresponding to
suburban terrain, presented by Holmes and shown in Table 2.3 in Section 2.1.2.1, both the windward wall and the
leeward wall is very consistent in the magnitude of the pressure coefficients. Taking notice in the maximum
coefficients at the windward wall, the magnitude of the stagnation point reaches a value of almost 1.8 for both
models, and decreasing to a value of around 1.1 ~ 1.2 at the centre bottom, indicating the downwash beneath the
stagnation point. For the leeward wall the minimum values are most evident at the outer corners of the model,
reaching a value about -1.65, slightly decreasing in negative pressure towards the centre of the wall ending with a
value of -1.3, which is more or less consistent with the values for Holmes’ model.
As apparent from Holmes’ model, the stagnation points differ in height when assessing the maximum, mean
and minimum values for the windward wall. Considering the maximum coefficients in Table 5.4, the peak
pressure occurs at just above 90% of the total height, whereas the stagnation point for the mean pressures occurs
just beneath the 90%, and decreasing to about 85% for the minimum coefficients. These values are in accordance
with theory, stating a stagnation point for the mean values around 70-80% of the height, but increasing in
percentage with increase in the aspect ratio.
The phenomenon of separation zones as seen at Figure 2.7 in Section 2.1.2, is evident, when looking at especially
the top of the building for the minimum values, where a large suction area arises around the front corners when
the wind attacks the windward façade and separating the flow at the vertices.
Assessing the mean values in comparison to the wind tunnel model of a tall prism in a turbulent boundary layer
flow presented by Holmes in Figure 2.9 in Section 2.1.2.1, some of the same phenomena on the side walls are seen
in Table 5.4, as a thin line of turquoise contour at the separation angle from the windward façade indicating a
smaller negative pressure at the near vertex and then increasing in negative magnitude towards the centre of the
wall, decreasing towards the back vertex again, indicating a separation and reattachment zone. Furthermore the
peak of the minimum values on the sidewalls agrees with the minimum values for the sidewall on Holmes’
model in Table 2.3. Looking at Table 5.4 a half circle of green contour appears at the top vertex with a magnitude
of -2.4, changing to a magnitude around -2 just below, as is more or less consistent with the phenomena and
values appearing at Holmes’ model at the upper part of the side wall. These erratic suction zones apparent in the
contour on the sidewalls are caused by the varying fluctuations in the wind flow, which create vortices that shed
crosswise the wind direction on the sidewalls.

Eurocode (EC) provide general overall values for the pressure coefficient for each wall and roof, which are listed
in Table 2.5 in Section 2.2.1.3 for a rectangular building with ݄/ܾ> 5. The pressure coefficients provided by EC
are mean values, which are divided in local and global coefficients used for cladding design and overall stability,
respectively, where the local values are the ones which are comparable with the mean pressure coefficients
provided from the wind tunnel test. Zone D in EC corresponds to the windward wall in Table 5.4, where the
mean values reach a magnitude of 0.9 at the stagnation point and decreasing to a minimum value of the means
of 0.1 at the outer corners, whereas EC design for a value of 1.0 for the total windward area. The leeward wall is in
EC designed for a total value of -0.7 for Zone E, where the values from the wind tunnel varies from -0.60 ~ -0.46.
Assessing the sidewalls and the roof in EC, both faces are divided into different areas, accounting for the larger
values in negative pressure at the separation angles. For the sidewall the values at the edge closest to the
windward wall has a magnitude of -1.4 (Zone A), decreasing in negative value to -0.5 at the edge of the leeward
wall (Zone C), which is consistent with the measurements from the wind tunnel, varying from -1 to -0.4 in
roughly the same zones. Furthermore it is seen, that the roof zones in EC is very convergent with the areas
divided by the contour lines in Table 5.4 for the top, however only if assessing the minimum values instead of
the mean values. Zone I in EC, which is the zone closest to the edge of the leeward wall accounts for both negative
and positive pressure, which are consistent with the pressure coefficients for the wind tunnel test, if looking at
the maximum values, where an area corresponding to Zone I has a positive magnitude of around 0.2.
Generally the values in EC are slightly overestimated in comparison to the mean pressure coefficient determined
from the wind tunnel test. However, it should be noted, that when determining the final pressure on the high-
rise building, the comparison between the wind tunnel test results and EC is determined as shown in Figure 2.19
in Section 2.4. As illustrated on the figure, the peak pressure determined from EC accounts for the gust pressure
multiplied with the mean load coefficients, whereas the peak pressure from the wind tunnel test accounts for the
mean pressure multiplied with the peak load coefficients, which ultimately should lead to the same resulting
value. However, when assessing the peak load coefficients in Table 5.4, it becomes evident, that the range between
the peaks and the mean coefficients varies considerably because of the fluctuating negative pressure in the across
wind direction due to vortices shedding from the windward corners. This leaves peak coefficient values above
two times the mean coefficients provided by EC when assessing the sidewalls and leeward wall, entailing that the
values determined from EC is far too generalized when determining wind on a high-rise building.

When assessing the external wind load, it is interesting to see if any aerodynamic wind load effects occur, which
is done by plotting the Power Spectral Density of the wind load. This is done for a wind direction of 0 degrees in
the x- and y-direction, seen in Figure 5.13, where the two first fundamental frequencies are marked.

For the cross-wind loading ܨ௫, it is obvious that the fluctuations have a peak compared to the load in the along-
wind direction ܨ௬, which can be explained by the vortex shedding phenomenon, explained in Section 4.4.1. The
vortex shedding frequency is found from the expression of Strouhals number to 0.087 Hz, which obviously is
within the range of the peak occurring for ܨ௫. However, as vortex shedding only becomes critical when the
frequency conflicts with the fundamental frequency of the structure, the phenomenon is not further processed.
5.4.2 Response of the structure
In the preceding sections, the external wind load acting on the structure has been outlined, exhibiting which
regions that are mostly affected. However, when the assessing the wind load on a high-rise building, the effect of
the wind load, in correlation with the building dynamic properties, has high interest as well, why the responses
of the structure in relation to accelerations and floor-by-floor loadings are outlined in the following sections.
5.4.2.1
Mode contributions
The responses are, as described in Chapter 4, determined in both the frequency domain and in the time domain.
The solution strategy sought in the time domain by the Newmark method includes all modes determined in the
dynamic analysis for each direction, whereas the solution strategy in the frequency domain normally only
contains the first mode in each direction, unless more modes are dominating. However, as no distinction is made
between the different modes in the time domain, and the most risky contributions to the responses occurs when
the structure is affected by resonance, it is essential to see which modes that contributes to the resonance effect of
the building, to be able to ascertain the number of modes to include in the frequency domain.
A zoom in on the time series of the acceleration responses for outrigger 4 (ܨଵ଴in Figure 5.10), found by the
Newmark method, are shown in Figure 5.14 to investigate the wave periods of the response for the x-, y- and ɔ-
direction. Looking at the time series, it is obvious that it is the first fundamental mode that dominates in each
direction, where the “big” waves ranging around zero for the x-, y- and ɔ-direction correspond to the first
fundamental mode with a period ofܶ௫௬.ଵ= 1/݂௫௬.ଵ= 7.14ݏ in the x- and y-direction, andܶఝ.ଵ= 1/݂ఝ.ଵ=
2.22ݏin the ɔ-direction. The second mode is evident in the “small” waves that fluctuate around the crest of the
first period in all directions, with a period ofܶ௫௬.ଶ= 1/݂௫௬.ଶ= 1.59ݏ for the x- and y-direction, andܶఝ.ଶ=
1/݂ఝ.ଶ= 0.76ݏ for the ɔ-direction. This shows, that the contribution for both first and second mode for each
direction is quite prevailing, why both modes in each direction are included in the frequency domain.
Figure 5.14: Zoom in of response at outrigger 4 for the single high-rise, where the wave
length of the first two eigenfrequencies in each direction are clearly seen
==PAGE:125==

The same phenomenon can be detected in the power density spectrum of the displacement responses for a wind
angle attack of 180 degrees at outrigger 4, seen in Figure 5.15, where the energy reveals a peak around the
fundamental frequencies of the structure for the x-, y- and ɔ-direction. In the ɔ-direction the first three modes
are visible in the power spectrum, however, to avoid aliasing only frequencies up to 1.7 Hz can be included in the
frequency domain analysis, as described in Section 5.3.3.2, why only the two first modes of torsion is included in
the ɔ-direction.
As the response-analysis is performed, where distinction is made between the three directions, x, y and ɔ,
respectively, all three power spectra are illustrated with each of their fundamental modes. It should however be
noted, that the first six fundamental modes of this particular building, if looking at it as a 3-dimensional
structure, is;
First and second mode as the sway mode in each direction of x and y, ݂ଵ= fଶ= 0.14ܪݖ, the third mode as
torsional motion in ɔ-direction ݂ଷ= 0.45ܪݖ, the fourth and fifth mode as “second mode” of sway in each

Comparing the x- and y-direction in Figure 5.15, the across-wind response is revealed in the x-direction and the
along-wind response in the y-direction. Here it is seen that the resonance effect of the first mode is far more
prevailing for across-wind excitations than for along-wind excitations. This can be resolved from the value of
spectral ordinate in the x-direction which is evidently dominated by the peak in the spectrum induced by the
fundamental frequency of mode 1, if compared to the along-wind excitations in the y-direction, where the
background response reaches almost the same value of the spectral ordinate, as the peak in the spectrum induced
by mode 1.
The consideration of the mode contribution can almost directly be translated to the twin arrangement of the
high-rise buildings, why the number of included modes merely is chosen as the same as for the single high-rise
building, not further elaborated in the analysis for the twin buildings.

5.4.2.2
Peak accelerations
The responses in terms of peak accelerations are determined both in the frequency and in the time domain,
where both results are presented and compared in the following. The peak accelerations in the frequency domain
are determined by the spectral method discussed in Chapter 4 in Section 4.1.1, and calculated as described in
Section5.3.4.2. The peak accelerations in the time domain are determined by the Newmark method, discussed in
Chapter 4 in Section 4.1.2, and calculated as described in Section 5.3.4.1.
The peak accelerations are calculated in each direction (x, y, ɔ) as a separate values, and combined through the
methods described in Section 4.2.
As mentioned the frequency approach is the most widespread due to the computational effort needed when
calculating in the time domain, and since the full solution is at disposal in the time domain, it is interesting to
see the comparison between the two methods to verify if a solution strategy in the frequency domain is
sufficiently accurate.
The peak accelerations for 360 degrees for both the frequency and time domain are shown in Figure 5.16 for all
three directions to the left and combined for all directions to the right. In the frequency domain, three crosses are
marked for every 10 degrees, accounting for each of the peak accelerations determined for each of the three runs
conducted per 10 degrees.
x-, y- and ɔ-direction peak accelerations
Combined peak acceleration
Frequency domain
Time domain
Figure 5.16:  Comparison of peak accelerations at outrigger 4 in the x-, y- and ɔ-direction, and combined acceleration,
determined in the time and frequency domain for the single high-rise
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 111 -
Comparing the two methods of investigation, the curves of the accelerations are quite consistent in shape, both
separately for each x, y and ɔ-direction and for the total acceleration for all directions combined. However, when
looking at the values for the two methods, it becomes obvious that the accelerations in the frequency domain
overestimates the very peaks with quite a considerable magnitude, reaching up to almost a difference of 4 milli-
g, when looking at the peaks in the combined accelerations for a wind direction of 170 degrees. The difference is
caused by the fact, that the peaks in the frequency domain are determined as the very peak of each of the three
runs conducted for each 10 degrees. Looking at the three crosses for each run, it is evident that the results are
quite scattered around the design curve for the peaks, leaving it obvious that the final result would vary
significantly if only one run was chosen, or the mean value of the peaks for that matter, which is a big
uncertainty when calculating in the frequency domain. If more runs were conducted, a mean value of the peaks
might have given a more comparable solution with the time domain, but as only three test runs were conducted
and the difference between them so pronounced, choosing the “peak of the peaks” is on the safe side. The
prominent difference from the three runs can be explained by the fact, that the peak accelerations determined by
the frequency domain approach is singlehandedly dependent on a read-off from one single value of the ordinate
in the power spectrum, and since the power spectrum consists of a finite number of discrete points scattered in a
“curve”, the read-off can give a somewhat flimsy solution.
To verify if a solution strategy in the frequency domain is sufficiently accurate when assessing the peak
accelerations, the comparison to the results from the time domain reveals that a solution in the frequency
domain is on the safe side, as the magnitude of the peak accelerations in general are overestimations. However, if
the threshold values for human comfort should be obeyed, a solution strategy in the time domain is far more
beneficial. To compare the results for both the frequency and time domain with the threshold value for human
comfort, the peak accelerations are scaled to a return period of 5, 10 and 20 years from the storm event with a
return period of 50 years, as described in Section 5.3.4.3. The peak accelerations with the threshold values of storm
events with return periods of 5, 10, 20 and 50 years are shown in Table 5.5.

acceleration in the along-wind direction is occurring at 270 degrees for the x-direction, with a magnitude of
approximately 11 milli-g. The peak accelerations from the wind tunnel test exceeds the value from the Eurocode,
where an explanation could be the fact that Eurocode is not designed to investigate buildings with a height
above 200 m, why an exceedance is expected. Furthermore only a peak acceleration in the along-wind direction is
available in Eurocode, which gives an unclear picture of which directions should be investigated, as it is clearly
seen the cross-wind direction is the dominating one, when the peak accelerations are investigated.

5.4.2.3
Static equivalent loads
The overall objective of a wind tunnel test is, as mentioned in Chapter 4, to provide for the fluctuating nature of
the wind and response of the structure, by determining the static equivalent loads which can be used, when
determining the structural sectional forces. These equivalent loads are, as the peak accelerations, determined in
both the frequency and time domain to assess the creditability of calculating in the frequency domain and if the
methods provide the same output. Furthermore, two different methods within the time domain are assessed, a
full time series of the “static equivalent loads” that accounts for the full picture, and a component-dividing-
method, similar to the method in the frequency domain, which distinguish between the quasi-static
contribution of the wind load and the resonant contribution, as elaborated in Chapter 4.
The static equivalent loads in the frequency domain are determined by the method discussed in Chapter 4 in
Section 4.1.1.2, and calculated as described in Section 5.3.5.4. The static equivalent loads in the time domain are
determined by the methods discussed in Chapter 4 in Section 4.1.2.3, and calculated as described in Section 5.3.5.2
and Section 5.3.5.3.
The loads are calculated in each direction (x, y, ɔ) as separate values and not combined, as done for the total peak
acceleration. This is done in order to produce the floor-by-floor loadings in each direction, where the concurrency
of a wind force acting in all three directions is taking into account by a set of load combinations factors,
elaborated in Section 5.4.2.5.
Before turning the static equivalent loads into floor-by-floor loadings, an examination of the loads in general is
done to ascertain any differences of the calculation routines. However, to understand how the different
components in the wind force contributes to the overall load, a division of the three components, mean,
background and resonance, are done for the two component-dividing-methods in the frequency and the time
domain, respectively.
The contribution from the different components varies significantly dependent on which height of the building
that is being assessed, e.g. the resonant component has immense contribution at the uppermost heights whereas
the value decreases at the lower levels. Therefor a comparison between the 1st outrigger at 12th floor (ܨଶin Figure
5.10), and the 4th outrigger at 81st floor (ܨଵ଴in Figure 5.10), is assessed.
It should be noted, that the magnitude of the difference between the values of the two outriggers cannot be
directly compared, as the load in outrigger 4 spans over a considerably smaller height compared to outrigger 1,
why the division between outrigger 1 and 4 is merely to see how the contributions from each component in the
wind load changes with the height of the building.
The static equivalent loads in the x- and y-direction, determined by the spectral method in the frequency
domain (Figure 5.18), and the corresponding method in the time domain (Figure 5.19), are shown with the mean
component ܨ௫.௠௘௔௡/ܨ௬.௠௘௔௡, the resonant component ܨ௫.ோ/ܨ௬.ோ and the background component ܨ௫.஻/ܨ௬.஻
apart, to see how the contribution from each parameter varies from outrigger 1 to outrigger 4.
Correspondingly to the x- and y direction, the static equivalent load for torsional motion is determined in both
the frequency domain (Figure 5.20) and time domain (Figure 5.21), respectively, where ܯ௭.௠௘௔௡ is the mean
component, ܯ௭.ோthe resonant component, and ܯ௭.஻the background component.

Looking at the comparison between outrigger 1 and 4 both in the x- and y-direction in Figure 5.18 and Figure
5.19 it is evident that the dominant component changes throughout the height of the building. At outrigger 1,
the fluctuating part of the wind force, known as the background component ܨ௫.஻/ܨ௬.஻, has the biggest
contribution to the equivalent load, followed by the static mean component ܨ௫.௠௘௔௡/ܨ௬.௠௘௔௡, whereas the
resonant component ܨ௫.ோ/ܨ௬.ோ is ranging between ~ 1MN and 2 MN, not contributing significantly to the
overall load. The contribution of the resonant component is expected, as it is determined on the basis of the
frequency and mode shape of the decisive mode, where the fundamental sway mode has biggest impact at the
top of the building, leaving the smallest impact at the bottom, which evidently shows in the resonant part.
From the plots of outrigger 4 in the x- and y-direction for both domains, the load contribution from the
resonant effect of the building, due to the accelerations at top of the building, is palpably dominating. The
background and mean component contributes almost equally to the load, but with no significant value, leaving
ܨ௫.ோ/ܨ௬.ோ almost equivalent to the accumulated load. As expected, the resonant contribution follows the shape
of the accelerations, illustrated in Figure 5.16, having its peaks for cross-wind at every 90 degrees. At the along-
wind directions, the resonant part is still the dominating contribution of the load, but significantly smaller than
in the across-wind direction, verifying that the resonance of the building repeatedly is most profound
perpendicular to the flow.
Comparing the two calculation methods in the frequency (Figure 5.18) and the time domain (Figure 5.19) in the x-
and y-direction, it is seen that the mean components obviously are identical, but the background component is
underestimated in the frequency domain, when looking at the plots for outrigger 1. This can be explained by the
fact, that the background component, calculated in the frequency domain, only accounts for the standard
deviation multiplied with a peak factor, which not always is consistent with the peaks determined as the
maximum value of both the 22%- and 78%-fractile in the time series. When looking at the time series of the
external force for outrigger 1, the peaks of the 22%-fractile has a tendency to be slightly higher in absolute value
than the 78% fractile, as more pronounced peaks are measured for suction.
Conversely the resonant component is overestimated in the frequency domain, which can be spotted for both
outrigger 1 and outrigger 4, but most flagrant in the across-wind direction for outrigger 4, which is consistent
with the results from the peak accelerations in Figure 5.16.
As the static equivalent load for torsional motion in the ߮-direction has another unity, it is treated separately
from the x- and y-direction, where comparison between the two outriggers is seen in the frequency domain in
Figure 5.20 and in the time domain in Figure 5.21. Same tendencies as in the x- and y-direction are detected,
where the background component ܯ௭.஻ is the dominating portion of the load at outrigger 1, and the resonant
component ܯ௭.ோat outrigger 4. Conversely, the shape of ܯ௭.ோdoes not significantly change from outrigger 1 to
outrigger 4 compared to the x- and y-direction, where it was more pronounced at certain angles. Furthermore it
is seen, that the mean component ܯ௭.௠௘௔௡has almost the complete same shape ranging around zero, regardless
of which outrigger that is being assessed, and is always the smallest contributor to the load, which is explicable,
since the torque occur, when the forces vary in magnitude split from the centre, which will appear more
pronounced at the peak of the loads, which naturally arise from the background and resonant components.
Furthermore it is interesting to see which component peaks at which angle, as the peak of the background and
resonant component occur almost unambiguously at every 90 degrees, whereas the mean peaks at 10 degrees
offset in the positive direction for the maximum values and 10 degrees offset in the negative direction for the
minimum values.
In Figure 5.22 the resulting static equivalent loads accumulated for all three components (mean, background and
resonant) and the resulting force for the time series in the time domain, are all shown for both outrigger 1 and 4.
The resulting loads are illustrated in both the x- and y-direction for all three calculation methods.
Similar to Figure 5.22, Figure 5.23 shows the same information, merely with data in the ߮-direction instead.
It should be noted, that in the resulting loads, the ‘absolute value’ is taken of the mean forces in the component-
dividing-methods to account for the fact that the resonant and background components only contribute with
positive values.

==PAGE:137==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 121 -
As evident in both Figure 5.22 and Figure 5.23, only the forces calculated by the time series provides the full
solution with both positive and negative values, which is caused by the fact, that the equations used for the
component-dividing-analysis in both the frequency and time domain contain square-rooting of all dynamic
components and thereby disregard all information about positive and negative contributions. As the time series
provide the most credible solution, this method is used as verification.
Looking at the resulting forces for outrigger 1 in Figure 5.22, the shape of the curves for all three calculation
methods are very comparable, also spotting the similarity to the shape of the force coefficients illustrated in
Figure 5.11. However, the resulting forces for outrigger 1 in frequency domain are slightly underestimated
compared to both forces determined in the time domain, which most likely is due to the underestimation of the
background component as discussed after comparison of Figure 5.18 and Figure 5.19. Again, the same
phenomenon as discussed for the separated components, the frequency domain conversely overestimates the
forces at outrigger 4, which is caused by the overestimation of the resonant component.
The same tendencies for the torsional equivalent loads are apparent in Figure 5.23, where the shape of the forces
matches in all three methods for both outrigger 1 and 4, most prominent in two upper solutions though.
Concordant to the forces in the x- and y-direction, the frequency domain is an underestimation, when looking at
outrigger 1. However, when comparing the values for outrigger 4, where an overestimation normally is detected
in the frequency domain, only the peak at 90 degrees seems to have a slightly higher resultant compared to the
forces in the time domain. Generally when assessing the torsional equivalent loads, there is a slight tendency of
underestimations in the frequency domain.
To give a more clear overview of how the comparison of the peaks in the frequency and time domain looks in
relation to the actual time series of the forces, a time series of ܨ௫, ܨ௬ and ܯ௭ are plotted, where the 78% fractile
value determined by the full time series, denoted as ܨ݌݁ܽ݇.௧, and the peak static equivalent load found in the
frequency domain, denoted as ܨ௣௘௔௞.௙, both are marked on the time series of the forces, to see if they are within
the same range. The time series are seen in Figure 5.24 for a wind attack angle of 180 degrees, where the
minimum peak within each subseries is marked by red, and the maximum peak by turquoise.
It should be noted, that the plot of the time series only is shown for one single run, whereas the 78% fractile of the
time series and the peak static equivalent load in the frequency domain both are determined for all three runs,
why another time series of the three runs could look differently for the ܨ௫, ܨ௬ and ܯ௭ forces in the figure.
Looking at the plots of the time series of the three directions, each has a different conclusion on the verification
of the frequency domain. For the across-wind loading (x-direction for 180 degrees) it is evident that the two
methods are almost impeccably analogous. However, for the along-wind loading (y-direction for 180 degrees), the
frequency domain approach has its “fractile-value” at a slightly higher range than what the 78% fractile states
from the calculation of extreme value analysis in the time domain., which leads to an overestimation in the
frequency domain. Conversely, when looking in the ɔ-direction, the 78% fractile of the time series range at a
slightly higher level than the frequency domain, entailing an underestimation in the frequency domain.

Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 122 -

y-direction

Figure 5.24: Time history response for the single high-rise for wind direction of 180 degrees  ( run 56)
It should though be clearly noted again, that the peak loads in the frequency and time domain are illustrated for
one single wind direction, here 180 degrees, which cannot be used to directly conclude on the results, as a
different wind direction might induce another conclusion, especially if looking at Figure 5.22, where a decline for
ܨ௫is seen at outrigger 4 for exactly 180 degrees in the frequency domain, causing the underestimation in Figure
5.24. Therefor the concluding summery of the comparison between the three methods should be done in relation
to Figure 5.22 and Figure 5.23, where all wind directions is evident.
From assessing the calculations in the frequency domain versus the calculations in the time domain, it is obvious
that the time series provides the most credible solution, as no generalizations are included, and the full time
series is used. However, the calculations in the frequency domain are considered reasonable satisfactory if an
analysis in the time domain is not possible. For assessing the forces, which should be used by the structural
designer in the following, the forces calculated in the time domain is used to provide the results with most
accuracy.
==PAGE:139==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Single high-rise building
- 123 -
5.4.2.4
Base forces
The key data for any high-rise building will be in the form of aerodynamic base moments, base shears and base
torsions. The base forces are used to determine the critical angle of attack of the wind that should be used for the
structural design, where all three principle force directions are assessed. The principle coordinate system of
structural loads in relation to the floor plan is illustrated in Figure 5.25.
Figure 5.25: Plan of the high-rise building
with coordinate system for structural loading
The base shears, moments and torsions are calculated as explained in Section 5.3.5.2 and presented in a circular
plot for 360 degrees for the mean, maximum and minimum peaks in Figure 5.26.
It is seen, that the base bending moments in the x- and y-direction follow the shape of the base shear forces
inducing these moments, which furthermore are similar in shape, when comparing the mean forces to the drag
coefficient illustrated in Figure 5.29. From the figures the critical wind directions for both x, y and ɔ are clearly
seen, where x and y reveals its peaks at the across-wind direction, inducing the maximum and minimum at 0
and 180 degrees for ܨ௫ and ܯ௬, and maximum and minimum at 90 and 270 degrees for ܨ௬ and ܯ௫. For the
torsional moment the maximum peaks are revealed at about 10 degrees offset in the positive direction from each
90 degrees, and 10 degrees offset in the negative direction from each 90 degrees for the minimum peaks, leaving
eight critical wind directions for torsion.
