5.4.2.5
Floor-by-floor loadings
For a structural designer it is convenient to have the static equivalent loads translated into floor-by-floor
loadings distributed over height and linked to each floor, as generally used when examining the overall stability.
The floor-by-floor loadings are determined as described in Section 5.3.6.1, where the weighted load distribution of
the static equivalent loads enables the load to be determined floor by floor. Figure 5.27 shows the peak load
distribution of the forces with height; the peak force for ܨ௫ is determined for a wind direction of 180 degrees, 270
degrees for ܨ௬ and 170 degrees for ܯ௭. The figure contains the linear distribution of the static-equivalent loads for
the nine heights as the solid line, from where the load per floor is determined as the weighted load distribution
between the nine heights, shown as the dashed line, where each floor is marked by a dot.
Furthermore, it is seen that the lowest 40 m of the high-rise building is not provided with a static equivalent
load, as no pressure points are available at the lower part of the high-rise due to the model configuration in the
wind tunnel test, why an expected load distribution are added to include the lowest 40 m in the resulting floor-
by-floor loadings.
Figure 5.27: Plot of Static Equivalent Loads (SEL) divided by 9 heights, Floor-by-floor Loadings (FBFL),
and location of floor heights for the single high-rise
As evident on the plots for the x- and y-direction, the load contributions increase with height, having peaks at
every location of the outriggers, due to the massive mass inducing a bigger contribution of the dynamic forces.
The biggest load contribution is at the top of the high-rise building, which is expected due to the impact of the
resonant dynamic force. In Table 5.6 the peak floor-by-floor loadings for the single high-rise are listed for each
direction of x-, y and ߮ for a return period of 50 years.
The resulting drag force and overturning moment determined by Eurocode is found in Table 2.10 to a
magnitude of ܨ௪= 58.33ܯܰ and ܯ௪= 10228.9ܯܰ݉. When comparing the drag force from Eurocode to the
maximum occurrence of base drag forces determined from the floor-by-floor loadings in Table 5.6 as ܨ௫=
67.48ܯܰ and ܨ௬= 66.76ܯܰ, an underestimation is clearly observed. Furthermore, when assessing the
overturning moment induced by the floor-by-floor loadings found as
ܯ௫= 14796.2ܯܰ݉
and
ܯ௬= 14737.6ܯܰ݉, the underestimation from Eurocode is obviously even more pronounced, resulting in a
reduction of almost 50%. Thereby the statement that Eurocode has certain limitations when used for the design
of structures with a height above 200 m, as discussed for the peak accelerations, is further substantiated.

Figure 5.28: Distribution of forces in percentage of maximum peak for the single high-rise building

Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings

5.5 Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
5.5.1 External wind load effects
5.5.1.1
Drag coefficients
The drag force coefficients are compared with the theory of ESDU and Holmes described in Chapter 2 and the
assessment conducted for the single high-rise building in Section 5.4.1.1. The drag force coefficients are
determined by ( 2.10 ) in Section 2.1.2.2 and as described in Section 5.3.1. The force coefficients are plotted in a
circular plot of 360 degrees, to investigate how the drag force coefficients are changing due to the change of the
angle of attack from the wind. In Figure 5.29 the drag force coefficients for the twin arrangement of high-rise
buildings are shown, together with the coordinate system of the high-rise building and wind angle of attack,
where it is seen that the interference model is placed in front of the principal model for an angle of 0 degrees.
Figure 5.29: Drag force coefficients in x- and y direction for 360 degrees for twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
It is clearly seen in Figure 5.29 that both of the drag force coefficients in the x- and y-direction have a magnitude
close to zero at an angle of attack of 0 degrees, where the interference building are placed in front of the principal
model. When comparing the angle of attack of 0 degrees to the drag force coefficients of the single high-rise
building, shown in Figure 5.11, ܥி௬are significantly lower, as the interference effect of shielding is occurring,
which implies a beneficial effect to the principal building, as the wind forces are consumed in the interference
building. At 45 degrees the two high-rise buildings are staggered and the wind flow is passing through the
spacing of the two high-rise buildings. However, the shielding effect is still occurring as the magnitudes of ܥி௬
still is significantly lower at this position than for the single high-rise building. The magnitude of the ܥி௫is
similar to the single high-rise building at 45 degrees, and increases with the angle of attack up to 90 degrees,
where the magnitude has become slightly larger than for the single high-rise building. This effect is occurring as
the interference effect between the two buildings induces amplification in the pressures at the surfaces. From 90 –
270 degrees the two force coefficients are almost not affected of the interference building, as the principal high-
rise building is in the front, thus the magnitudes are similar to the single high-rise building. At 270 – 360 degrees,
interference effects are occurring, inducing the same effects as observed from 0 – 90 degrees, where only the
direction of ܥி௫is changed. Furthermore due to the interference effect between the two buildings, the small
increase in ܥி௬at an offset of +/-10 degrees from 90 and 270 degrees are not similar to the single high-rise
building and the studies in Section 2.1.2.2, as the drag force coefficient does not become positive. Similar, due to
the shielding effect, this phenomena is not occurring in ܥி௫ for an angle of attack of +/- 10 degrees from 0
degrees.

Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 130 -
At an angle of 0 degrees, the interference building is in front, and the principal building is in shelter of the
interference building, which entails low wind pressures at the windward wall and smaller suction at the
sidewalls. At an angle of 90 and 270 degrees the two buildings are parallel and a channelling effect occurs, where
the suction of the two adjacent surfaces is experiencing remarkable amplifications, compared to the isolated case
of the single high-rise building. At 180 degrees the principal building is in front of the interference building,
where the pressure distributions of the surfaces are alike the pressure distributions at the single high-rise
building, where the interference effects are only inducing a slightly lower suction at the leeward wall.
With reference to Section 2.1.2.3, the interference effects between the high-rise buildings are dependent on the
distance/width ratio, where a small ratio results in large interference effects, which decreases together with a
larger distance width/ratio. It was found that the shielding effects are of great impact at ratios below 3, and the
channelling effect are of great impact at ratios below 3.5 The distance/width ratio of the twin arrangement is
equal to 2, which results in both shielding and channelling are clearly seen at the test results.
The main focuses on the assessment of the surfaces are to study how the wind flow are acting on the surfaces of
the principal building, for a tandem arrangement and a parallel arrangement, why the two wind directions
studied are for an angle of attack of 0 and 90 degrees, respectively. The mean, minimum and maximum pressure
coefficients for the principal building in the twin arrangement are shown, for a wind angle of attack at 0 degrees
in Table 5.8 and for a wind angle of attack at 90 degrees in Table 5.10.
To compare the results of the principal building in the twin arrangement to the single high-rise building, the
interference factors ܫܨ, determined by ( 2.12 ), of the mean pressure coefficients and interesting peak pressure
coefficients are studied at the most affected surfaces. The comparison is shown for 0 degrees in Table 5.9 and for
270 degrees in Table 5.11.
The plots are found in Appendix 5.4 and produced as described in Section 5.3.2. The plots are performed in similar
manner as for the single high-rise building, where each wall has an appertaining colour bar to illustrate the
pressure distribution, where the lowest magnitudes are indicated with blue and the highest with red.
When looking at the pressure distributions of the principal building in Table 5.8 for 0 degrees, it is easily seen the
windward wall, A, are affected of the shielding effect. The pressure coefficients at the windward wall are showing
a small suction in the mean pressure coefficients and a small pressure at the maximum peak coefficients, which
are similar to the studies represented in Figure 2.13. The top of the windward wall is affected of the vortices and
high wind speeds that are created at the top of the interference building, as these hits the uppermost part of the
windward surface on the principal building and induces pressure.
The increased pressure at the uppermost part of the windward wall are also inducing larger suction at the top
corners of the sidewalls, as seen in the mean and minimum peak coefficients, which are similar to the studies in
Section 2.1.2.3 as well. However the magnitudes of the minimum peaks are quite high compared with the Figure
2.13, which are explained in terms of higher interference building induces higher interference effects at the
sidewalls (Yu, Xie, Zhu, & Gu, 2015). Besides from the top corner of the sidewalls, the pressure distribution of the
surfaces is only varying slightly with 0.2 for the mean coefficients and 0.5 for the peak coefficients, evident in the
red colour of the surfaces.
The leeward wall are affected of suction, which are slightly higher than the pressure distributions of the single
high-rise building in Table 5.4, which is also evident in the mean average pressure distributions of the walls,
shown in Figure 5.30.

5.5.1.3
Aerodynamic forces
When assessing the external wind loads, it is interesting to study if any aerodynamic forces occur, which can be
observed by plotting the Power Spectral Density of the external wind loads. This is done for a wind direction of 0
and 180 degrees in the x- and y-direction in Figure 5.31, where the two first fundamental frequencies are marked.
External load in the x-direction
External load in the y-direction
Wind direction of 0 degrees
Wind direction of 180 degrees
Figure 5.31: Power spectral density for the external wind force for 0 and 180 degrees for the principal building in the twin arrangement
The wind phenomenon vortex shedding can be detected in the cross-wind direction of a power spectral density,
as a peak will occur around the vortex shedding frequency, which is found in Section 4.4.1 to occur at
݂௦= 0.087ܪݖ. For the cross-wind loading ܨ௫ for 180 degrees, it is obvious that the fluctuations have a peak in the
range of the vortex shedding frequency, where no peak is observed for the load in the along-wind direction ܨ௬.
When assessing the cross-wind loading ܨ௫ for 0 degrees, it is obvious that the shielding effects, due to the
interference building, are removing the vortex shedding from the principal building, as no significant peak is
observed. This implies the forces in the x-direction will be remarkable reduced, due to smaller motions.
However, as vortex shedding is a natural phenomenon and only becomes critical when the frequency matches
the fundamental frequency of the structure, the phenomenon is not further processed.
==PAGE:152==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 136 -
5.5.2 Response of the structure
5.5.2.1
Peak accelerations
The peak accelerations for the principal building in the twin arrangement are studied for 360 degrees in steps of
10 degrees. The peak accelerations are determined in both the frequency domain, in terms of the spectral method,
and time domain through Newmark time integration, as described in Section 5.3.4.1 and 5.3.4.2.
When the peak accelerations of the single high-rise were assessed in Section 5.4.2.2, it became evident that the
peak accelerations determined by the spectral method resulted in overestimations with nearly 4 milli-g. However
peak accelerations of the twin arrangement of high-rise buildings are still assessed for both the frequency
domain and the time domain to observe the differences of the two methods, when dealing with interference
effects.
The peak accelerations of 360 degrees are shown in Figure 5.32 for the principal building in the twin
arrangement, where the peak accelerations for each run in the frequency domain are marked by crosses.
x-, y- and ɔ-direction peak acceleration
Combined peak acceleration
Frequency domain
Time domain
Figure 5.32:  Comparison of peak accelerations at outrigger 4 in the x-, y- and ɔ-direction and combined acceleration, determined in the
time and frequency domain for the principal building in the twin arrangement
It is seen the shape of the separated peak accelerations in the time domain are quite consistent and can almost be
mirrored at 180 degrees, as they are only slightly varying. As mentioned in Section 5.5.1.2, the shielding effects
occurring at 0 degrees, due to tandem arrangement of the two buildings, are beneficial for the principal high-rise
building, as the peak accelerations for 0 degrees are significantly lower than for 90, 180 and 270 degrees.
Furthermore the troughs at 45 and 315 degrees in the combined peak acceleration are more pronounced than for
==PAGE:153==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 137 -
the single high-rise building, which again is caused by the shielding effects still are occurring at these angles,
which also were evident when looking at the mean average pressure coefficients in Figure 5.30.
The increment in the cross-wind direction at 100 and 260 degrees are noticeable in the y-direction, as the
channelling effects are clearly seen, when comparing to the case of the single high-rise building, where the peaks
are occurring at 90 and 270 degrees. Furthermore the channelling effects are inducing an increment of 2-3 milli-g
in the critical wind directions.
The shape of the peak accelerations in the ɔ-direction is smooth compared to the single high-rise building, which
can be explained as the interference building are causing a more consistent oblique wind pressure at the surfaces.
The consistent oblique wind are inducing a consistent torsional response, compared to the single high-rise
building, where the torsional responses are more inconsistent and only occurring as a result of turbulence and
vortex shedding of the wind flow. Furthermore it is seen the torsional responses at 90 and 270 degrees are
significantly higher than for the single high-rise building, which again is explained because of the interference
building, since it is inducing a large uneven wind flow at the two sidewalls in the crosswind direction, which is
leading to larger torsional accelerations.
As expected the frequency domain are leading to overestimations of the peak accelerations, and even inexplicable
crests, where the peak acceleration in the y-direction suddenly get a peak at 210 degrees. Furthermore the
deviation of the three runs for each 10 degrees wind angle is very pronounced, which are unfavourable for the
quality of the determination method. Consequently the peak accelerations are most accurate determined in
terms of the time domain, which also became evident when assessing the single high-rise building.
The peak accelerations are scaled to a return period of 5, 10 and 20 years from the storm event with a return
period of 50 years, as described in Section 5.3.4.3. The peak accelerations and the threshold values for human
comfort for storm events with return periods of 5, 10, 20 and 50 years are shown in Table 5.12. It is the seen the
peak acceleration for the time domain is just barely below the threshold values for return period of 5, 10 and 20
years. The peak accelerations determined in the frequency domain are exceeding the threshold values for all
return periods, which were expected. As mentioned for the single high-rise building in Section 5.4.2.2, the peak
accelerations could be reduced by assessing the peak accelerations at a lower floor than at the height of the
uppermost outrigger, together with a shorter distance from the centre of rotation of the building.
Table 5.12: Total peak accelerations in the frequency and time domain at outrigger 4 for the
principal building in the twin arrangement, and the threshold value for human comfort according to Section 4.2.3

The responses of the accelerations are shown as 3D plots in Figure 5.33, where a clear overview of the resulting
motions in x- and y-direction are seen. The responses are plotted as the total response, meaning the x- and y-
components from the torsional displacement are included. It is seen for a wind angle of attack of 180 degrees that
the motions in the cross-wind direction are dominating and that large motions are occurring. The response for
wind direction of 40 degrees is significantly smaller, as it is seen the motions are not nearly as large as for a wind
direction of 180 degrees.
==PAGE:154==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 138 -
The differences the interference building is inducing are evident when comparing the acceleration responses to
the single high-rise building in Figure 5.17. For an angle of attack of 40 degrees it is seen the single high-rise
building is fluctuating more than the principal building in the twin arrangement, since the peak acceleration in
the x- and y-direction are of higher values. For an angle of attack of 180 degrees, the motions in the x-direction
are of same magnitudes as the single high-rise building, where the motions in the y- and ɔ-direction are
significantly lower than for the single high-rise building, resulting in a more ellipse shaped motion time history.

building in the twin arrangement, where the motions in the x-, y- and ɔ-direction are included
5.5.2.2
Static equivalent loads
The static equivalent loads for the principal building in the twin arrangement are found from the full time series
in the time domain and the component-dividing-analysis in both the frequency and time domain as described in
Chapter 4, and determined by the routines explained in Section 5.3.5. The static equivalent loads are assessed and
compared to the case of the single high-rise building.
The three methods of determination of the static equivalent loads where carefully assessed and compared in the
analysis of the single high-rise building in Section 5.4.2.3. It was found that the frequency domain resulted in
overestimations of the resonant contribution at outrigger 4, where the background contributions gave
underestimations at outrigger 1, together with a general underestimation of the torsional forces. Consequently
the full solution of the time series is used as verification for the two component-dividing-analyses, thus the
analysis of the static equivalent loads for the principal building in the twin arrangement are only investigated
for the time domain. However the forces are still determined in the frequency domain, where references are
made to Appendix 5.8, where plots of these are found.
To investigate the contribution from the three components to the total static equivalent loads in the time
domain, the three loads are studied separately in Figure 5.34 for the x- and y-direction and in Figure 5.35 for the
ɔ-direction, where the mean component ܨ௫.௠௘௔௡/ܨ௬.௠௘௔௡/ܯ௭.௠௘௔௡, the resonant component ܨ௫.ோ/ܨ௬.ோ/ܯ௭.ோ
and the background component ܨ௫.஻/ܨ௬.஻/ܯ௭.஻ are shown at outrigger 1 and outrigger 4, corresponding to
12th floor and 81th floor respectively. As mentioned for the single high-rise building, the magnitudes of the forces
are not directly comparable, as the span of the forces is of different heights, as seen in Figure 5.10.
It is seen in Figure 5.34 for the x- and y-direction that the mean component in outrigger 1 is almost identical
following the drag force coefficient seen in Figure 5.29, where both the x- and y-mean component are close to
zero at 0 degrees. The mean components in the x- and y-direction in outrigger 4 look in some semblance like the
drag force coefficient for the single high-rise building. This phenomenon can be explained in terms of the
uppermost part of the high-rise building is affected of a large pressure induced by the speed up above the
==PAGE:155==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 139 -
interference building, as seen in Table 5.9 at a wind angle of attack of 0 degrees, why the mean component have a
value different from zero.
Furthermore it is seen the resonance component are dominating at outrigger 4, where the background
component are dominating at outrigger 1, which is similar discovered for the single high-rise building. These
observations are consistent with the fact that the resonance component are found from the accelerations of the
high-rise building, which are following the mode shape of the building, where it is known the largest responses
most often occur at the top of the building.
The background component is found from the external load on the high-rise building, where it is evident the
turbulent flow is generating a larger turbulence at the bottom, i.e. a larger standard deviation of the bottom,
which is directly seen in the background components, as the background component has no substantial
contribution at outrigger 4.
Time domain
Outrigger 1
Outrigger 4
Figure 5.34:  Comparison of the mean, resonant and background part in the static equivalent loads for outrigger 1
and 4 in the x- and y-direction for the principal building in the twin arrangement, determined in the time domain
==PAGE:156==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 140 -
When comparing the separated static equivalent loads in Figure 5.34 to the case of the single high-rise building
in Figure 5.19, it is seen the background and mean component at outrigger 4 are almost identical, except for some
small variations at 10 and 350 degrees. This can be explained as mentioned before, due to the shielding effect not
occurring at the uppermost part of the high-rise building, why the case of the twin arrangement will look similar
to the case of the single high-rise building. Conversely, the resonant components at outrigger 4 are highly
different, as they are following the peak accelerations, where it was found that shielding effects at 315 – 45
degrees were beneficial for the principal building in the twin arrangement, resulting in lower resonant
components, and at 90 and 270 degrees the channelling effects are inducing higher resonant forces to the
structure.
At outrigger 1 the difference in the mean components is clearly seen, as the shielding effects occur at the
principal building in the twin arrangement, leaving almost no mean forces at 0 degrees, where the mean forces
have a great contribution for the case of the single high-rise building. The resonant components for both cases
are following their peak accelerations, which are smaller at outrigger 1 than at outrigger 4, resulting for both
cases in a low impact in the static equivalent loads.
The background component at outrigger 1 is contributing the most to the static equivalent loads and varying
significantly, when comparing the single high-rise building to the principal building in the twin arrangement. It
is seen the background component for the single high-rise building is only varying slightly around 4.5 MN,
where the shielding effects from the interference building are resulting in almost half the magnitude for the
background component at 30-60 and 300-330 degrees in the twin arrangement.
The same phenomena of contributions are seen at the torsional static equivalent loads in Figure 5.35, where the
resonant component is dominating at outrigger 4 and the background component is dominating at outrigger 1.
The explanation is similar to the x- and y-direction, as the resonant component is determined by the peak
acceleration, which obviously is larger at the top of the building than close to the bottom. It is seen the peak in
the resonant component at 180 degrees is sort of “cut off” due to the interference effect, which also was the case
for the torsional peak accelerations in Figure 5.32.
The most significant difference in the torsional forces, than for the x- and y-direction, are the peaks of the mean
components, which are occurring at an offset of +/- 10-20 degrees to 0, 90, 180 and 270 degrees. These peaks occur
in consequence of oblique wind flows, where the pressure distributions vary significantly from 0, 90, 180 and 270
degrees and induce large consistent torsional moments. Furthermore, it is seen the maximum peaks in the mean
component at outrigger 1 are occurring at 80 and 280 degrees, which is explained by the channelling effect and
contribution to the torsional moment, where the smallest peaks are seen at 110 and 250 degrees, also occurring
due to the channelling effect but easing the torsional moment instead. The channelling effects are strongest at
the bottom of the high-rise building, and are therefore the reason why the phenomena are not occurring at the
top of the building.
When comparing the torsional static equivalent loads to the single high-rise building shown in Figure 5.21, it is
seen the background component are reduced with almost 10 MNm due to the interference effect, which was
observed for the background component in the x- and y-direction as well. The resonant component is observed
to have higher magnitude than for the single high-rise building, due to higher peak accelerations, which also was
the case for the x- and y-direction. When comparing the mean component, it is seen that the reduced peaks at 110
and 250 degrees and increased peaks at 80 and 280 degrees, are not occurring for the single high-rise building,
where the mean component are more stable.
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings

The combined static equivalent loads in outrigger 1 and 4, determined in the time domain, for the principal
building in the twin arrangement are shown in Figure 5.36 for the x-, y- and ɔ–direction, where the static
equivalent loads determined from the full solution of the time series are compared. It is seen the forces
determined by the time series are containing negative values, since the peak forces are determined directly from
the time series for the 22% and 78%-fractile.
It is observed that the mean components in the x- and y-direction for outrigger 1 have great impact for the
direction of the forces, as the peaks are following the curve. Furthermore it is seen the shielding effects around 0
degrees result in remarkable lower force magnitudes, than for wind directions of 90-270 degrees.
It is clearly seen the resonant forces in the crosswind direction are dominating for outrigger 4 in the x- and y-
direction, where the motions are creating both positive and negative forces. In addition it is observed that the
mean component has a small impact of the resulting direction of the forces in outrigger 4, as the mean forces in
the x-direction are larger at 90 degrees than at 270 degrees, likewise for the y-direction, where the largest forces
are occurring at 0 degrees and the lowest at 180 degrees.
==PAGE:158==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 142 -
In the full solution from the time series, the direction of the torsional forces for both outrigger 1 and 4 are
evident, as the negative peaks are at its minimum at 80, 170 and 260 degrees, where the maximum peaks are
occurring at 100, 190 and 270 degrees.
When comparing the full solution from the time series to the case of the single high-rise building in Figure 5.22
and Figure 5.23, it is evident that the shielding effects entail the forces in the y-directions at outrigger 1 to be
remarkably reduced for the positive values, where the forces at outrigger 4 are increased due to the channelling
effects. In spite of these observations, the forces will at some wind directions reach approximately the same forces
in the x-direction with or without the interference building, where the forces in the y-direction is slightly
increased with the interference building.

The time series of a wind angle of attack of 180 degrees (run 56) are shown in Figure 5.37, where the 22%- and 78%-
fractile values determined by the full time series are seen, together with the peak static equivalent load found in
the frequency domain. The three time histories are divided into 16 subseries, where in each the 16 maximum and
minimum values are shown as the turquoise and red dots, and from which the 22%- and 78%-fractile value are
found. The three time series give a good understanding of the internal forces of the structure, where the
oscillations of the system are creating large responses. It is seen that only a few of the extreme peaks are higher
than the estimated maximum and minimum peak values.
From the three graphs the high variation of peak estimation by the frequency domain are seen, as the peak in
the x-direction leads to large overestimations of the time history peaks.
As observed in the circular plots of the peak forces, the fluctuations of the cross-wind is significantly larger than
the fluctuations in the along wind direction at 180 degrees. This is evident at the graphs as well, as it is seen the
mean value in the x-direction is close to zero, but the force is highly fluctuating, where the mean force in the y-
direction is different from zero, but are fluctuating a lot less than the x-direction.

5.5.2.3
Base forces
The base forces are an overall picture of the forces the high-rise building are affected off, why the base forces are
later on used to determine the critical wind angle of attack. The principle coordinate system of structural loads in
relation to the floor plan is illustrated in Figure 5.25.
The base forces and base moments of the principal building in the twin arrangement are shown in 360 degrees
plots in Figure 5.38, for the mean, maximum peak and minimum peak forces. It is clearly seen the mean base
forces in the x-direction, and the overturning moment around the y-axis are following the curve of the drag
force coefficient in the x-direction, seen in Figure 5.29, where the base force in the y-direction and the
overturning moment around the x-axis are following the drag force coefficient in the y-direction. Furthermore,
the critical wind directions are easily detected, as the peaks are clearly detected at 100 and 260 degrees in the y-
direction, and for 0 and 180 degrees in the x-direction. In addition the peaks of the torsional moment are
occurring at four wind directions in both the negative and positive direction, leaving eight critical angles of
attack in total.

Base forces
Torsional base momemt
Figure 5.38: Overturning base moments, base drag forces and torsional base moment for the principal building in the twin
arrangement
==PAGE:161==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data – Twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
- 145 -
When analysing the static equivalent loads in Section 5.5.2.2 it is evident, that in spite of shielding and
channelling effects from the interference building, the magnitudes of the forces will at some point in the changes
of the wind directions, reach the same magnitudes as for the case of the single high-rise building. The same is
valid for the peak forces of the base- forces and moments, where the two cases are similar in the x-direction, but
where an increment in the y-direction is detected.
5.5.2.4
Floor-by-floor loadings
For structural design it is convenient to transform the static equivalent loads, determined in the nine heights
shown in Figure 5.39, into floor-by-floor loadings as point loads in the 82 floors of the high-rise building. The
floor-by-floor loadings are determined as described in Section 5.3.6.1.
The floor-by-floor loadings in the three directions are determined from the maximum or minimum occurrences
at one direction, meaning the floor-by-floor loadings in the x-direction ܨ௫ are determined a wind angle of 180
degrees, in the y-direction ܨ௬ from an angle of 260 degrees, and in the ɔ-direction ܯ௭ from an angle of 90
degrees.
The floor-by-floor loading for the high-rise building in the twin arrangement are shown in Figure 5.39, where the
static equivalent loads (SEL) for the nine heights are shown with the solid line, together with the more smooth
load distribution to obtain the floor-by-floor loadings (FBFL) in the 82 floors, marked with dots.
Furthermore it is seen the lowest 40 m of the high-rise building is not provided with a static equivalent load, due
to no pressure points are available here, why an expected load distribution are added to include the lowest 40 m
in the resulting floor-by-floor loadings.
Figure 5.39: Plot of Static Equivalent Loads (SEL) divided by 9 heights, Effective Floor-by-floor Loadings (FBFL),
and location of floor heights for twin arrangement of high-rise buildings
When comparing the distribution of the floor-by-floor loadings in Figure 5.39 to the single high-rise case in
Figure 5.27, it is seen the two distributions are quite similar. The lowest 40 – 140 m in the x- and y-direction are
of lower magnitudes for the twin arrangement, where the uppermost part of the load distribution in the y-
direction is strongly increased in the twin arrangement. Since the forces in both the x- and y-direction are
decreased at the bottom, it is evident the interference effects between the two buildings are inducing clear
changes in the wind loads. Furthermore when looking at the ɔ-direction, it is evident that the two cases matches
almost flawlessly.
The floor-by-floor loadings for the worst case configurations are given in Table 5.13 for a return period of 50
years. It is clearly seen in the table, that the heights where the four outriggers are positioned are dominated by a
remarkable amplification, due to the massive mass inducing larger resonant forces.

Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 148 -
5.6 Concluding comparison of two test configurations
The drag force coefficients for the single high-rise and the twin arrangement in Figure 5.11 and Figure 5.29
respectively, both indicate a smooth curve, where the single high-rise case almost flawlessly fits the theory
represented in Chapter 2. The drag force coefficients for the twin arrangement match the single high-rise case
and the theory in the range of a wind direction of 90-270 degrees, where the two cases are almost identical, since
the principal high-rise building is fronting the windward direction. At a wind direction of 0 degrees it is evident
that almost no drag forces are occurring, due to the principal building is sheltered.
The same tendencies are observed for the mean average pressure coefficients of the four surfaces, which are seen
in Figure 5.12 for the single high-rise building and in Figure 5.30 for the twin arrangement. The single high-rise
building matches almost flawlessly with theory, where the twin arrangement is influenced by shielding and
channelling, which implies that surface of the principal building, which is adjacent to the interference building,
is affected of remarkable amplifications of suction.
It is visible in the pressure distribution of the surfaces that both cases are quite consistent with the theories of
single and twin cases. The single case displays similarities to the theories at the windward wall and the leeward
wall, where some deviations are observed at the sidewalls, as the two theories in Figure 2.9 and Table 2.3 nor are
completely identical. The twin arrangement of high-rise buildings and the interference factors are surprisingly
consistent with the theories in Chapter 2 as well, where an amplification of up to 60% is observed at the sidewall
due to the channelling effect.
In the analysis of the peak accelerations it is observed that the cross-wind direction prompts the dominating
motions, which are induced by vortex shedding, observable in the power density spectrums. Due to large
uncertainties when the performing the read-off of the spectrum ordinate in the frequency domain, the three
peak accelerations for each of the 36 directions resulted in large deviations, where the “peak of the peaks” is
chosen as the resulting peak to be on the safe side.
It is found that the peak accelerations in the frequency domain result in overestimations for both the single
high-rise building and the twin arrangement. In the comparison between the two configurations it is evident
that a shielding effect occurs at 0 degrees, where the interference building is in front, whereas the channelling
effect occurs with its maximum at 100 and 260 degrees, inducing a more pronounced suction at the adjacent
surface, than for the single high-rise case. This implies that the peak acceleration in the y-direction has its peaks
at 100 and 260 degrees, instead of 90 and 270 degrees as for the single high-rise case.
In the analysis of the single high-rise building three methods were applied to determine the static equivalent
loads. It was found that the frequency domain results in overestimations of the resonant response at outrigger 4,
where the background response results in underestimations at outrigger 1. Furthermore it is evident that the
torsional forces in the frequency domain consequently result in underestimations. Due to the uncertainties of the
frequency domain, it was concluded to use the static equivalent loads from the full solution in the time domain
to determine the floor-by-floor loadings. The estimated drag force and overturning moment determined by
Eurocode, results in an underestimation when the values are compared to the resulting base forces for the single
high-rise building.
Since it was concluded that the forces determined in the frequency domain results in a somewhat flimsy picture
of the resulting forces, the full solution in the time domain is applied to obtain the static equivalent loads of the
twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings. Again it was clearly observed, that the principal building is affected
of interference effects, as the forces in the y-direction are increased due to the channelling effect, and the forces in
the x-direction are decreased at a wind direction of 0 degrees, due to the shielding effect.
The resulting floor-by-floor loadings in Figure 5.27 for the single high-rise building revealed only small
deviations of the load distribution in the x- and y-direction, which is expected as the high-rise building is
completely identical in the two directions. The load distribution of the floor-by-floor loadings in Figure 5.39 for
the twin arrangement showed a very similar distribution to the configuration of the single high-rise. However,
==PAGE:165==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 149 -
due to channelling and shielding, the loads in the lowest region of the twin building are decreased in both the x-
and y-direction, whereas an amplification at the uppermost part of the building is seen in the y-direction. This
leaves a total drag force in the x-direction of lower value than present for the single case, and a larger total drag
force in the y-direction.
As it is important to design for the worst case test configuration, the peak occurrences of both the single high-rise
and twin arrangement need to be considered. To investigate if the resulting design loads of the high-rise building
should be used for the configuration of the single high-rise building or the twin arrangement, as given in Table
5.6 and Table 5.13 respectively, a comparison between the base forces and base moments are shown in Figure 5.41.
Here the single case is marked with blue lines and the twin case with black lines. As mentioned above, the
overturning base moments ܯ௬ are very similar, as the forces in the x-direction only is of slightly higher
magnitude for the single case compared to the twin case, whereas the overturning base moment ܯ௫ is strongly
increased for the twin arrangement. The torsional moments ܯ௭ are almost identical, but with a shift of 10
degrees for the wind angle, resulting in the peaks in the twin arrangement occurring where the moments in the
x- and y-direction have a greater impact than in the single high-rise configuration.
Base moments
Base forces
Torsional base momemt
Figure 5.41: Overturning base moments, base drag forces and torsional base moment for the two test configurations
==PAGE:166==
Chapter 5 – Analysis of wind tunnel data
- 150 -
As a conclusion of the results from the two test configurations, it is seen that the single high-rise configuration is
causing the largest forces in the x-direction, whereas the twin configuration is causing the largest forces in the y-
direction. The magnitude of the torsional moments are almost identical for both configurations, but should be
tested for the twin configuration, due to the interaction between ܨ௫ and ܨ௬in the direction where the torsional
peaks occur.
These observations results in that the floor-by-floor loadings should be implemented as a combination of the two
test configurations, if it is undefined if the construction of one or two high-rise buildings should be executed.
The combination of the floor-by-floor loadings should therefore be:

The floor-by-floor loadings for the single high-rise in Table 5.6
with load combination 1-8 in Table 5.7

The floor-by-floor loadings for the twin arrangement in Table 5.13
with load combination 9-16 in Table 5.14

The floor-by-floor loadings for the twin arrangement in Table 5.13
with load combination 17-24 in Table 5.14

Chapter 6
6 COMPUTATIONAL FLUID DYNAMICS
With the rapid expansion within computer modelling and hardware/software development, future prospects will
with most certainty show an abolition of the physical testing of a scaled model, as statements for the past decades
have been made that Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) would replace reduced scale wind tunnel testing and
become the new “numerical wind tunnel”. (Blocken, 2013) Therefor it is almost inevitable not to just briefly cover
the subject when dealing with wind tunnel testing.
The expansion within computational wind engineering has risen concurrently with the development of 3D-
modelling, as the compatibility with the CAD-programs makes it possible to implement the models in the CFD-
software, whereas the construction of scaled models is necessary for wind tunnel testing. The design of the 3D-
models also makes it much easier to adopt ongoing changes and modifications in building geometry or boundary
conditions in the computer software, which requires expensive re-runs and model-modification, when working
in a wind tunnel. Furthermore CFD can provide information on the flow variables in the whole calculation
domain, compared to a wind tunnel test, which only provide measurements-results where the instrumentation is
installed.
However, the numerical simulation is still not fully developed, why errors and uncertainties still occur,
especially when assessing the flow acting on a structure, why it is interesting to see the comparison of results
from a wind tunnel test in relation to the results from a “numerical wind tunnel”, which is substantiated by the
quote from Murakami, 1990:
“The results of numerical simulation cannot be free from various types of numerical errors… Therefore it is
indispensable that the accuracy of numerical simulation be examined by comparing the numerical results with
those from wind tunnel tests or field experiments… Therefore the two different methods of research should
proceed in concert and in cooperation with each other.” (Blocken, 2013)
To see the similarities between the wind tunnel test and a numerical simulation, a CFD model is constructed as
an exact replica to investigate if the pressure distribution on the high-rise can be matched with that from the
CFD-model. As mentioned, a CFD model can provide information on the flow variables in the whole calculation
domain, why streamlines on varies surfaces can be assessed. These streamlines can be compared to the oil
painting model tests performed in the wind tunnel as well, where the streamlines are created by shear forces
parallel to the surfaces.
==PAGE:168==
Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 152 -
6.1 CFD in general
As stated in the previous chapters, flow related phenomena can be described by a set of partial differential
equations, which can be solved numerically by a discretization method to obtain an approximated solution
where the differential equations are approximated by a set of algebraic equations. The approximations are
applied to small domains in both space and time, if transient cases, where the numerical solution provides results
at discrete locations. (Ferziger & Peric, 2002).
This is the theory behind every Computational Fluid Dynamics software, where the accuracy of the solution
depends on the approximate form of the governing equations, the turbulence model, the computational domain,
grid, convergence criteria, boundary conditions and so on.
The choice of turbulence model has however the greatest effect on the flow simulations concerning modelling
errors and uncertainties. The turbulence model is a choice of equations used to describe the physics of the flow.
Turbulent flow produces fluid interaction at a large range of length scales, and this can be taken into account by
modifying the non-linear, unsteady three-dimensional Navier-Stokes (N-S) equations, when calculation for a
turbulent flow regime. Therefore the different turbulence models use different approaches to solve these N-S
equations depending on the requirements for accuracy or computational availability.
The overall different turbulence models are the Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS), Large Eddy Simulation
(LES) or Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS).

The Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS) captures all of the relevant scales of turbulent motion and
numerically solves the N-S equations completely three dimensional and time dependent. However, as
the model represents the smallest scales of fluid motion, the grid cell size must not exceed the smallest
size of an eddy, which requires an exorbitant dense grid, which necessitates too much computational
demand and cost for practical use.

The Large Eddy Simulation (LES) solves the spatially averaged N-S equations, and is a technique, where
the smaller eddies are filtered and are modelled using a sub-grid scale model, while the larger energy
carrying eddies are simulated. The LES solution requires a more refined mesh than a RANS model but
not in the same density range as the DNS solution.

The Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) solves the flow fluctuations averaged over time, which
introduces new apparent stresses known as Reynolds Stresses. This approach does not form a closed set
of solutions, which requires the introduction of further approximation models, described in section
6.2.2.1. (Koss, CWE: Computational Wind Engineering, Lecture slides, 2014) & (Ferziger & Peric, 2002)
In general a turbulent flow is a wide range of turbulent eddy sizes. The large eddies derive energy from the mean
flow and the energy is transferred from larger eddies to smaller eddies. In the smallest eddies, the turbulent
energy is converted to internal energy by viscous dissipation, as can be seen on Figure 6.1. Furthermore the figure
shows how comprehensive the different turbulence models are, which can be derived from the size of the eddies
resolved. In general the first methods compute all turbulent motions, whereas more and more motions are
approximated by models when choosing the last method.
==PAGE:169==
Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 153 -
Figure 6.1: Turbulence models (Fan, Note 13: Turbulence modelling , 2014)
To generate a full turbulent flow in CFD for a scaled model as in the wind tunnel test, the most comprehensive
and exact way to solve the N-S equations is by the DNS approach, but as stated, this requires almost intractable
computational cost for a complex flow around a building. The LES approach requires considerable less
computational power, as the smallest scale of eddies are filtered and modelled instead of resolving them,
however, the approach still requires a sufficiently accurate and dense mesh to be able to capture the small eddies
at the near wall boundaries. The RANS-model is the most widely used method within turbulence modelling, as
the averaging of the N-S equation decreases the computational time substantially, however the use of a RANS
model is only beneficial when looking at averaged forces, as all information regarding instant fluctuations and
peak pressures are disregarded, as the RANS model only provides a mean value of the flow. Therefor a complete
analysis of the wind load on a structure is not possible to generate with the use of a RANS-model, as stated by
Tamura, 2008:
“It should be noted that, in order to use CFD for wind load estimation, an accurate time-dependent analysis, such
as LES, is definitely required, because it enables prediction of peak-type of quantities such as a peak pressure or
maximum response of a building and a structure. Furthermore, consistency of inflow turbulence characteristics
for various numerical models is very significant for appropriate wind load estimation.” (Blocken, 2013)
As substantiated by the quote, a LES approach is necessary to be able to account for a time-dependent analysis,
where the fluctuations in the wind are recorded, enabling the assessment of the peaks in the flow, which is
obtainable by a wind tunnel test. However, to get the same results as obtained by 50 seconds in the wind tunnel,
it would require an exorbitant level of computational power and time to simulate a transient case with full
resolution of the fluctuations, why it has not been possible to simulate a usable LES-model.
It should however be noted, that the intention at first was to try to simulate a full LES-model, but after
assessment of the results of a model run with a transient case for only a couple of seconds, it provided no usable
results. Therefore the LES approach was abolished due to limitation in computer resources and computational
time, and a RANS approach was used instead.
As mentioned the RANS approach averages the N-S equations over time, why only the mean values are available
for comparison to the wind tunnel. Even though the numeric simulation cannot provide knowledge about the
peak forces, which is necessary information for a structural engineer, it is still interesting to investigate if the
mean values comply with the mean values from the wind tunnel test, to see if the behaviour of the flow has the
same tendencies. To assess the mean coefficients, the mean pressure distribution on each surface from the wind
tunnel and CFD model is compared. Furthermore, the streamlines visible on the oil painting models will always
correspond to the mean values, why a RANS-model is appropriate for comparison of this cause.
Because of the complexity and size of the model, the CFD simulations require an excessive amount of
computational power, which standard computers cannot manage, therefore Rambøll has been very helpful in the
process and kind to put their computer resources at disposal to run the different simulations.
==PAGE:170==
Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 154 -
A wide range of different software packages are available within Computational Fluid Dynamics, where ANSYS
and OpenFOAM are the most commercially used within structural engineering. In the present simulation,
ANSYS has been chosen as the used software, using CFX as solver preference.
In the following an exposition of the generation of the wind tunnel model in ANSYS is made, after which an
explanation of the simulation process will follow, elaborating the choice of the turbulence modelling method.
6.2 The CFD model
6.2.1 Geometry and mesh generation
6.2.1.1
Geometry
To simulate the wind tunnel test, the geometry of the CFD model is created as an exact replica of the wind
tunnel, WT2 at FORCE Technology, as used in the test of the surface pressure model. The WT2 has a total length
of
20.4 m, where the flow-processing unit is 14.8 m and the test section is 5.6 m. The width of the wind tunnel is
ܮௐ்ଶ= 2600݉݉ and the height ܪௐ்ଶ= 1800݉݉. For picture documentation and a directory of the
dimensions of the roughness elements in the flow-processing unit, reference is made to Chapter 3, Figure 3.3 and
Table 3.1.
To be able to emulate the wind tunnel tests, a CFD model of both the single high-rise and twin high-rise
arrangement is investigated, creating two models for simulation. The high-rise models have the dimensions
equalling to a height of ܪ௠௢ௗ௘௟= 610݉݉ and width of ܮ௠௢ௗ௘௟= 102݉݉, as mentioned in Chapter 3.
When creating wind flow around buildings, the surroundings reflects the domain of the CFD-model, which
should be constructed sufficiently large to avoid any interference with the investigated model. To minimize an
undesired channel effect, a rule of thumb for the domain sizes are provided in Figure 6.2, where ܪ௠௔௫ refers to
the height of the high-rise model.
Figure 6.2: Required minimal distances between the buildings and the domain boundaries
 (Fan, Workbench tutorials 05, wind around buildings, 2014)

The blockage area ratio of the domain should be less than 3-5%

6.2.1.2
Specification of boundary layers
The influence of the external surroundings, which have been cut off by the computational domain, can be
represented by defining the boundary conditions of the domain. The boundary conditions express the properties
of the different surfaces and are essential in order to obtain the desired results.
In ANSYS, the boundary conditions are recognized by standard naming from where the CFX solver uses these
‘named selections’ to resolve the case with known boundary properties. The named selections include inlet,
outlet, symmetry and wall, where boundary conditions are applied to each surface.
The inlet and outlet surfaces define the airflow in and out of the domain, and are defined as front and back of the
domain, respectively. Normally when dealing with wind around buildings in a CFD simulation, it is necessary to
make a turbulent velocity profile with a ‘User Defined Function’ and interpret it in CFX. However, as the model
itself builds up a turbulent velocity profile above the roughness blocks, the inlet flow is uniform. This means
that the airflow firstly affects the spires and then the roughness blocks to create the turbulence, before affecting
the high-rise building. The inlet flow in a wind tunnel is determined with a velocity flow, where the magnitude
is set as predefined in the wind tunnel test to 15 m/s. The inlet turbulence boundary conditions are estimated
from which turbulent kinetic energy and turbulent dissipation rate will occur in a wind tunnel test. In reality it
is not possible to have no movement of air particles, and therefore the turbulence parameters can never be equal
to zero, even though that would be the optimum case.
The definition of the outlet has the boundary condition of a fully developed flow, which means that the
derivatives of all flow variables are forced to vanish at the end of the domain. The outlet boundary conditions are
set to the default properties, as the pressure through the model will secure that no backflow will happen.
Normally the approach flow direction is parallel to the lateral boundaries and top of the domain, why the
symmetry boundary conditions are usually used on these sides, but as the model should be compared to
measurements from a closed test section as the wind tunnel, the lateral and top boundaries are treated as solid
walls. The same is valid for the roughness blocks and high-rise buildings, as the roughness blocks have to create
turbulence and thereby act as “wind-blockage” in the domain, which means that fluid cannot penetrate. The
ground surface is treated as walls as well but divided in two, as different roughness are valid.
The wall boundary conditions are either treated as ‘smooth walls’ or specified by the roughness height of the
material. If the material is unknown the roughness height can be estimated from a number of equations, but
since it is known which materials the wind tunnel is made of, the parameters are estimated from known
magnitudes of the materials. The wind tunnel is constructed of smooth wooden plates and plexiglas to secure
that the walls and ceiling will affect the wind flow as little as possible, however as air particles always will stick
to a surface which is not completely smooth, the boundary conditions of the walls are treated as ‘non slip’.
In Table 6.1 the definitions of the different boundary conditions are listed with their appertaining boundary
properties.

Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 158 -
When creating a mesh, both efficiency and quality of the mesh have to be considered, as a high resolution mesh
requires much computer resource, and a bad quality mesh can lead to poor results, why balance between two
parameters has to be implemented. When evaluating the quality, the mesh is examined by assessing the
equiangle and equilateral volume skewness of all elements. The skewness describes the variety of the obscure
angles of the different mesh elements, the lower the skewness the less distortion of the elements and thereby
lower margin of errors. When evaluating the efficiency of the mesh, the general aim is to generate a sufficiently
dense and satisfactory mesh, which is able to resolve the small fluctuations engendered in the flow. This requires
the mesh to be created in such way that the flow aligns with the grid line near the boundaries. Furthermore the
resolution of the mesh should be higher in regions containing large gradients of pressure or velocity and just in
general in regions of interest.
The mesh of the CFD model is created in Mesher in the ANSYS software package, where different meshing
options with appertaining control systems are available. The overall meshing options available are the
tetrahedron, hexahedron and the mulitzone mesh, where the multizone mesh generates hexahedron mesh where
possible and tetrahedron mesh in the remaining parts. To be able to generate a denser mesh around the
interesting areas, the ‘advanced size function’ in Mesher is used, which means that the distribution of the
meshed cells can be controlled manually. To make the grid align with the flow direction near the boundaries, the
‘inflation’ function is used, as this will create layers of mesh parallel to the selected boundary wall.
As mentioned, the quality of the mesh has great impact on the simulation, why it is a very time consuming
process to generate a sufficient mesh. As the CFD model of the wind tunnel consists of a vast amount of elements
due to the boundary layer constructed of small roughness blocks, the mesh will with necessity consist of millions
of cells, which require a great amount of computational power.
At first lot of effort was made to generate a mesh consisting of hex-mesh where possible, why the domain was
decomposed into different bodies to be able to apply different meshing methods dependent on the configuration
of the geometry and the areas of interest. It ended up with a mesh consisting of around 16 million cells, which
were simulated on Rambølls computer resources. However, as the results file ended up with an unsatisfactory
outcome, the decomposed model was abolished, as the density of the mesh around the high-rise building and
roughness blocks was too poor to generate a sufficient resolution of the fluctuating flow. Therefor a pure
tetrahedron mesh was applied on the total domain, enabling a much denser mesh with a total number of
100.915.188 cells, where inflation was used on all boundaries to control the resolution of the flow. In Figure 6.4 the
tetrahedron mesh is illustrated, where it is seen that the mesh is much denser around the high-rise buildings and
how inflation creates prism along the boundaries of the high-rise. Due to similar configuration of the mesh
generated for the CFD model of the twin arrangement, the mesh does not contribute with further information,
why it is not depicted in the following, however it can be mentioned that the number of cells in Mesher has
slightly increased due to the implementing of the twin building, leaving a total number of 103.142.112 cells.

6.2.2 Simulation and post-processing
For simulation and post-processing of the results, the solver CFX is used from the ANSYS software package. The
routine consist of three steps:

CFX-pre, used for defining all setup regarding the simulation conditions

CFX-solver, used for the run of the simulation and assessing the convergence criteria

CFD-post, used for evaluating all result files and extracting information from the simulation
As the basic of the simulation and quality of the results mainly attribute to the choice of turbulence model and
the convergence of the simulation, these two aspects are elaborated in the following.
6.2.2.1
Turbulence models
The choice of a RANS-approach requires introduction for further approximation models, as the approach does
not form a closed set of solution to the N-S equations, as mentioned in Section 6.1. A wide range of approximation
turbulence models are available, where the choice depends on the level of accuracy needed, the computational
capacity and the configuration of the model properties, as some models are more suitable for assessing different
flow scenarios. In the case of the present wind tunnel CFD model, the Shear Stress Transport model is chosen.
Shear stress transport (SST)
An important factor within the modelling of turbulence, is the prediction of flow separation of a smooth surface,
where the standard two-equation models often misinterpret the onset and the flow separation under adverse
pressure. This is due to the fact that the onset might be predicted too late, and the amount of flow separation
under-predicted, which leads to an under-conservative flow prediction. This has given rise to the implementation
of new turbulence models, which include transport effects in the formulation of the eddy-viscosity, called ݇െ߱
models, where the Shear-Stress-Transport (SST) based model is recommended for high accuracy boundary layer
simulation. Therefor the SST model is chosen to give the most accurate prediction of the flow separation and
onset. Furthermore the ݇െ߱ models are capable of solving the steep near-wall profiles, which is important,
since the accuracy of near-wall modelling is an essential element to gain a successful prediction of frictional drag,
pressure drop and separation. The efficiency of the near wall modelling is dependent on a sufficiently fine mesh
near the wall regions, which can be assessed by the ݕା values. (Ansys help site: The k-omega and SST Models,
2015)
ݕା is the dimensionless distance from the wall, which is used to provide information on the near wall resolution,
and is given by the following equation. (Fan, Note 13: Turbulence modelling , 2014)
ݕା= ߩݑכݕ
ߤ
( 6.4 )
Where the density of the fluid is denoted ߩ. ݑఛ is the friction velocity, which is a variable that represents the
magnitude of the velocity fluctuations in the wall-bounded flow. ݕ is the distance from the wall, and ߤ the
friction constant. The lower the ݕାvalue, the better the resolution of the mesh.
Advisable values of ݕା are obtained from experience and expert knowledge from Rambøll. Generally a lower
value entails better results, as the eddies are more easily resolved at the near boundaries. Optimally a value below
1 is desired for a full resolution, but values up to around 100 are acceptable depending on the turbulence
intensity in the flow, where a higher ݕାvalue generally is more acceptable at higher turbulent flows.
==PAGE:177==
Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 161 -
6.2.2.2
Justification of solution convergence
An essential part of a simulation is to justify the quality of the solution, which is done by validating the
convergence of the simulation. The solver uses iterative methods to solve the algebraic system of equations, and
starting from an initial guess the flow variables are recalculated in each of the iterations until the equations are
solved up to the specified criterion. At the end of each solver iteration, the residual sum for each of the conserved
variables is computed and can be defined by the transport equation for a general variable ߮, shown below, where
ܽ is the influence coefficients and ܲ refers to the centre cell and ܾ݊ to the neighbouring cells (Ansys help site:
Monitoring Residuals, 2015).
ܽ௉߮௉= ෍ܽ௡௕߮௡௕+ ܾ
௡௕
( 6.5 )
At the start of each iteration the equality will not be valid, and the imbalance of the equality is called the
residuals. As the iterations proceed these residuals should tend towards zero or until the specified tolerance is
achieved, which will then indicate that a stable solution is obtained.
The solvers are controlled by the convergence criteria of the residuals, but to fully secure a converged model the
convergence control and conservation target needs to be fulfilled as well. The convergence control is the
maximum number of iterations needed to reach a stable flow variable, and the conservation target is the overall
mass balance, which is important as incongruence in in- and outflow would indicate that the model is not
actually converged even though indicated by the residuals. In Table 6.2 the solver controls of the convergence
limits are listed.
Table 6.2: Solver control of convergence
Convergence control 
Convergence criteria 
Conservation target
Minimum iterations: 1
Maximum iterations: 350
Residual type: RMS
Residual target: 10-6
0.01
To justify the solution convergence, a best fit solution for a combination of both convergence trough residuals
and convergence trough monitoring of flow variables are combined. The solution convergence is assessed in
Figure 6.5 and Figure 6.6.
Convergence of residuals: Momentum and mass
Convergence of residuals: Turbulence
Figure 6.5: a) Convergence criteria of residuals for momentum and mass, b) Convergence criteria of residuals for turbulence
==PAGE:178==
Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 162 -
It is seen, that the convergence of the residuals does not reach the criterion of six orders of magnitude within the
maximum allowed iterations, as prescribed in the solver settings. An increase in order of magnitude naturally
increase the accuracy of the solution, but since a turbulent flow will have difficulties obtaining convergence with
a strict criterion, industrial applications most often set a lower criterion, why the value reached on both Figure
6.5 a) and b) seems eligible, if convergence trough monitoring of flow variables and the conservation target is
satisfactory as well.
Convergence control of max pressure
Conservation target of mass imbalance
Figure 6.6: a) Convergence control of max pressures on the high-rise surfaces, b) Conservation target of mass imbalance
In Figure 6.6 a) the convergence of the maximum pressures on the high-rise surfaces is shown per iteration,
where it can be seen, that the pressure on each surface is stabilized at around 200 iterations, which is within the
limit. In Figure 6.6 b) the mass imbalance is shown per iteration, where the conservation target of 1% is reached at
more or less 150 iterations.
Evaluation of the convergence through both the residuals, the flow variables, and the conservation target,
quantifies that the solution can be considered sufficiently stable which validates the quality and accuracy of the
solution.
The convergence control is assessed in the exact same manner for the CFD model of the twin arrangement,
however, as the model is run in continuation of the model of the single high-rise, the convergence through both
the residuals and flow variables requires much less iterations to obtain a stable solution, where the maximum
iterations is 100.

Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 166 -
The mean pressures at the four surfaces and the top are seen in Table 6.4, where the measured pressure at the
surface pressure model in the wind tunnel is illustrated to the left, and the mean pressures extracted from the
CFD simulation are illustrated to the right.
In general when studying the pressures from the CFD simulation, it is observed that the mesh is extremely fine
around the high-rise buildings surfaces, resulting in endless small areas at the edges, where small points are
found to have larger pressures than the nearby areas. The contour plot pressures at the surfaces from the wind
tunnel are generated from 51 pressure taps at each surface, where the built-in Matlab function contour determine
the distribution of the contour lines. This can imply that the pressure distribution from the wind tunnel test is
not dense enough, as the small differences, detected at the surfaces in the CFD simulation, are not identified at
the wind tunnel model. Furthermore it is worth noticing the lowest 100 mm at the wind tunnel model are not
visible, as no pressure points are available here, thus no pressure distribution can be plotted in the surface
pressure distribution plots in this area.
In general when comparing the pressures from the two models, it is found the CFD simulation is showing
overestimations at the positive pressure areas and underestimations at the suction areas. This is consistent with
the fact that RANS models are not capable of generating vortex shedding at the wake, leaving turbulent kinetic
energy with underestimations and the suction areas with lower suction magnitudes. (Blocken, 2013)
It was found in Section 5.4.1.2 that the pressure distribution on the surfaces of the single high-rise building is
fairly consistent with the theory of the pressure distribution, represented by Holmes and ESDU in Chapter 2.
Furthermore it was found that the stagnation point is situated in a height of approximately 90%, corresponding
to 550 mm height, which is slightly higher than the theories of 70-80%.
It is a known fact that CFD simulations are generally good at estimating positive pressure areas, but lacks in
estimations of the suction areas. When looking at the windward wall, which is affected of large positive pressures,
it is seen that the pressure distribution from the wind tunnel and the CFD simulation are quite similar, only
differing in the shape of the contours, created by the downwash, which are shaped as ellipses in the CFD
simulation and almost as a triangular shape on the surface from the wind tunnel test model. This entails that the
pressure distribution from the CFD simulation is matching the theories from Chapter 2 in Figure 2.9 and Table
2.3 better than the wind tunnel. When comparing the magnitude of the pressures, it is seen that the CFD
simulation have slightly higher pressures, deviating around 6 Pa from the wind tunnel test. Furthermore it is
striking that a thin suction strip is occurring at the edges, which is a phenomenon difficult to recognize, as it
usually does not occur at the windward wall. Nevertheless, this can also be a result of higher ݕା values, as
discussed in Section 6.3.1.1.
When comparing the two sidewalls, a similarity is seen as a thin strip of suction close to the windward wall is
appearing at both models with similar magnitudes, which is also identified in the theory represented by Holmes
in Figure 2.9 in Section 2.1.2.1. At the surfaces from the wind tunnel the magnitudes are increased with areas of
negative pressures along the mid part of the sidewall, and decreased close to the leeward wall, where this
phenomenon is recognized as the reattachment zone, explained in Figure 2.7. This is not seen at the surfaces of
the CFD simulation, as the suction areas are decreased in magnitude along the sidewall instead. However, this
pressure distribution is more or less consistent with the pressure coefficients represented in Table 2.3, where the
thin strip is missing at the edge facing the windward wall. The pressure distribution close to the ground at the
sidewalls shows the same triangular shape with a lower suction area than the remaining surface. In general the
magnitude of the negative pressures at the CFD simulation model is smaller than the ones from the wind tunnel
model, where the positive pressures are slightly larger.

==PAGE:184==
Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 168 -
The leeward wall is indicating some similarities. At both models an extremely thin blue line of similar
magnitude is detected at the edges to the sidewalls and the top, indicating a large suction due to the sharp edges.
The largest suction area occurs at the centre near the ground for both models, where some deviations of the
distribution are seen, as a decrease in magnitude gets wider at the wind tunnel model and thinner at the CFD
model. The two green areas near the ground at the CFD model are presumably showing the phenomenon of
standing vortices at the wake near the bottom, as indicated in Figure 2.5 and 2.7. However, due to the lack of
information of the bottommost 100 mm at the wind tunnel model, it is difficult to verify if the phenomenon can
be recognised in both models.
The upper part of the leeward wall is showing a deviation, as the low suction area, marked with red, is seen as a
half circle area on the wind tunnel model, and only an ellipse at the CFD model. Furthermore the magnitudes of
the suction at the leeward wall on the CFD model are fairly underestimated, as a deviation of approximately 15
MPa is observed at the mid part of the wall.
The top of the high-rise models are quite consistent, as a strong suction at the edge facing the windward wall is
occurring at both models and then decreasing along the depth of the building. The suction strip top at the CFD
model is strongly increased in magnitude, which is impossible to detect with only 12 pressure taps at the surface
pressure model in the wind tunnel. However the remaining magnitudes of the CFD model are somewhat smaller
than the pressures from the wind tunnel model.
6.3.1.4
Comparison of streamlines and oil paint model
As mentioned, the streamlines of the oil paint model are created due to friction and shear forces at the surfaces,
where the magnitude of the forces are seen in the extent of driven oil paint. To be able to compare the oil paint
model to the CFD model, the shear stresses of the surfaces are shown as streamlines. In Table 2.3 the surfaces of
the oil paint model and CFD model is compared.
When looking at the top of the high-rise building, it is seen that the two models are showing the same
phenomenon, where a large suction is seen at the edge facing the windward wall, indicated with light blue at the
CFD model and a dark black area at the oil paint model. This area is also known as the separation zone.
Additionally it is seen that the streamlines are restricted at the large suction area and spread out again when
facing the edge to the leeward wall.
The consistence of the windward walls is very prominent. The stagnation point is clearly seen in both models,
and when comparing the positions, the stagnation point measured at the CFD model is apparent at a height of
approximately 550 mm, which is very consistent with the 560 mm measured for the oil paint model.
Furthermore the downstream of the surface is seen at both models, where streamlines are down warded, with a
separation line in the centre of the surface.
The sidewalls of the two models are quite comparable as well. At the oil paint model a strip at the edge facing the
windward wall is almost clear of oil paint, indicating the occurrence of large forces in the area. The same
phenomenon is seen at the CFD model, where the light blue colour indicates larger shear stresses. Next to the
clear strip at the oil model, a darker strip of oil paint is visible, indicating the separation zone. At both models the
streamlines are horizontally facing downwards, however a deviation between the streamlines are visible at the
top side of the oil paint model, as the arc-shaped streamlines indicate a vortex at the top of the model, which is
not as pronounced in the CDF model. Furthermore the vortices seen near the bottom at the CFD model are
likewise observed when looking closely at the oil paint model.
Lastly, the similarities of the leeward walls are observed, where the streamlines facing upwards are detected at
both models, where a clear centreline is seen, indicating the separation of the streamlines. Near the ground, a
darker area is seen at the oil paint model and yellow streamlines at the CFD model, representing the large forces
from the standing vortices at the wake.

6.3.1.5
Flow field around the high-rise
The flow separation effect caused by the high-rise building is clearly identified at the ground surface where the
streamlines forms a horseshoe vortex. The shape and size of the influence area are dependent on the height and
shape of the high-rise building. The horseshoe vortices visible from the oil paint test and the CFD simulation are
shown in Figure 6.10, where it should be noted that due to the perspective of the picture, the top of the high-rise
building in the oil paint appears larger than the one from the CFD simulation.
Oil paint test
CFD
Figure 6.10: Comparison of horseshoe vortex of the single high-rise model from streamlines of oil paint test and CFD simulation
From the theory represented by Beranek in Figure 2.6 a), the theoretical geometry of the horseshoe vortex is
calculated in Table 6.6, where the measured values are listed for both the oil paint and CFD as well. In Figure 6.10
the shape of the theoretical geometry is illustrated as the blue line and the measured geometry as the red line. It
is seen, that the two models are almost identical, as the measured shape of both horseshoe vortices are equal. The
radius of the horseshoe vortex is 30 mm smaller than the theoretical, where the theoretical arc can be fitted to
the outer streamlines of the horseshoe vortex at the oil paint model, but do not show any pronounced similarity
in the CFD model.
Table 6.6: Comparison of horseshoe vortex, found from theoretical, oil paint and CFD simulation

Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
