When studying the influenced area at the ground, the stagnation point in front of the building is clearly seen as
number 7 at both models. The horseshoe vortex in front of the building is indicated with number 6, where the
streamlines are showing a wind flow turning outwards from the building. The speed up around the corners are
seen as number 8, visible at the oil paint model as a dark area with surplus oil paint, and at the CFD model with
an increase in velocity magnitude. The board jet stream and outer bound of the influence area are marked as
number 9, where an increase in velocity is appearing. When looking closely, the small fast rotating vortices are
seen as number 16 at the oil paint model, where the streamlines are blurred, indicating the wind direction in the
area is constantly changing. This phenomenon is not visible in the CFD model, which is explicable as it is a
known fact that these small flow changes cannot be detected in a RANS model. As discussed in Section 6.3.1.4 the
stagnation points of the windward walls are consistent, and both marked as number 14 at the figures. From the
stagnation point the wind flows are separated horizontally, indicated as number 4, and vertically above the high-
rise building as number 3. The downwash of the building is seen as number 5, also discussed in Section 6.3.1.4.
At the wake of the building the horizontally standing vortices are clearly seen at number 13, visible at the oil
paint model as surplus oil paint, indicating an area where the streamlines ends with low velocities, which is also
seen at the CFD model. The stagnation point at the ground at the wake is marked with number 11, which is
clearly seen in both the CFD model, where the streamlines are separated, and at the oil paint model as a dark
area. The wind flow behind the stagnation point is seen as number 12, and the reversed wind flow turning
towards the building in between the two standing vortices (number 13) is seen as number 10. The upward
directional wind stream at the leeward wall is discussed in Section 6.3.1.4 and seen as number 15.
The wind flow around the high-rise building can be visualized with the CFD simulation, where a midplane is
created with wind flow in the direction of the high-rise building. Figure 6.13 shows the wind flow by means of
velocity streamlines, where the different wind phenomena are indicated with numbers, referring to Figure 2.5.
The stagnation point at the windward wall is again clearly seen at number 14, where the wind flow is separated
going upwards over the building (number 3), and downwards indicating the downwash on the wall (number 5),
and entering the horseshoe vortex, seen as number 6. The airflow above the high-rise building is seen as number
1, indicating the outer bound of the influence area. The stagnation point at the ground at the wake is clearly
detected at number 11, being the starting point of the streamlines. From the stagnation point the uprising wind
flow at the wake of the building is easily recognised, as the streamlines are reattached from the stagnation point
and entering leeward wall. Between the building and the stagnation point at the wake no streamlines are visible,
indicating that no significant wind flows are appearing, which is explicable, as the created mid plane is where the
two standing horizontal vortices at the wake meet. The striking spiral at the wake of the building is the standing
vertical vortex, seen as number 17, which collide with the upwind air flow at number 15.
Figure 6.13: Velocity streamlines of single high-rise CFD model in mid plane

Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics
- 175 -
Atmospheric boundary layer
The normalized mean wind velocity profile and turbulence intensity profile is obtained by the same manner as
for the single high-rise building simulation, as described in Section 6.3.1. The parameters are extracted from the
twin arrangement CFD simulation by a line located 0.4 m in front of the front building, corresponding to the
same position as for the single high-rise CFD simulation, see Figure 6.7. The wind profile and the turbulence
intensity are, likewise the single high-rise building, plotted together with the wind profile from the wind tunnel
and the theoretical profiles from the Power law, all seen in Figure 6.14.
Figure 6.14: Comparison of the wind profile in CFD simulation, the wind profile from wind
tunnel test and theoretical wind profile from Power Law terrain category III, for the twin arrangement
The reference wind speed at a height of 0.5 m, corresponding to the height of the cobra probe in the wind tunnel,
is found to 11.74 m/s, which are slightly higher than the reference wind speed from the single high-rise building
of 10.74 m/s. This indicates that even though a CFD simulation look identical, some changes can occur due to
changes in mesh and/or geometry. However, the resulting wind profile looks highly similar to the single case in
Figure 6.8, where the turbulence intensity is indicating changes in magnitude close to the ground
When comparing the profiles to the wind tunnel and the theory, it is seen the wind profile is crossing both the
theoretical and wind tunnel profile, which is identical to the single high-rise case. However, as discussed in
Section 6.3.1, the similarity to the single high-rise, indicates that simulation reflects occurrence happening in the
wind tunnel.
It is seen the turbulence intensity profile is parallel with the profile from the wind tunnel. As discussed in Section
6.3.1, none of the profiles from the wind tunnel or CFD simulation fits the theoretical curve. However, as it is of
most interest to check the validation of the CFD simulation to the wind tunnel test, it is evaluated the turbulence
intensity from the CFD simulation is reflecting the profile from wind tunnel profile.
6.3.2.2
Presentation of the two models
The simulation of the wind flow around and on the CFD model of the twin arrangement is compared to the oil
paint test conducted in the wind tunnel. The two models are pictured in Figure 6.15, showing the streamlines on
the windward walls and sidewalls with the prominent horseshoe vortices at the ground.
At first sight at the oil paint model, an oblique vortex at the windward wall of the rearmost high-rise model is
seen, indicating strong standing vortices between the two high-rise buildings. The same phenomenon is seen at
the CFD simulation model, but the vortex is conversely oblique in the opposite direction.

Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics

The sidewalls of the front building look similar to the single high-rise case, why references are made to Section
6.3.1.3. The magnitudes of the CFD model are slightly lower than for the wind tunnel model, which obviously
recur when comparing suction areas. The sidewalls of the rearmost building are showing the same phenomena at
both models, where suction is occurring at the main part of the sidewalls, with higher magnitudes at the edge,
facing the windward wall. Again, the CFD model indicates a slightly smaller magnitude than the wind tunnel
model.
The leeward wall of the front buildings are showing some similarities as well, as larger suction areas are
occurring at the uppermost part of the surfaces and decrease towards the ground. The area close to the ground at
the CFD model indicates the influence of the standing vortices between the two high-rise buildings, as two
increased suction areas are seen. This phenomenon is not clearly seen at the wind tunnel model, possibly due to
the missing 100 mm at the lowest part of the surface.
The leeward wall on the rearmost building is showing an almost constant suction area at the wind tunnel model,
which is similar observed at the CFD model, but again with lower magnitude. A strong increase of suction at the
edge facing the top of the building is occurring at the CFD model, which are observed at the wind tunnel model
as well, but lower in magnitude.
6.3.2.4
Comparison of streamlines and oil paint model
The streamlines on the oil paint model are indicating the mean wind and are created by friction forces on the
surface. To compare the streamlines to the CFD model, the shear stress on the surfaces of the high-rise models are
used. In Table 6.9 the two models are compared. It should be mentioned, that due to natural difficulties in taking
pictures of the leeward wall of the front building and the windward wall of the rearmost building, the pictures
are not perpendicular to the surfaces.
The top of the front building looks identical to the single high-rise case, why references are made to Section
6.3.1.4. The wind flow from the front building is affecting the rearmost building by the shielding effect, why no
separation zone is seen at the top of the rearmost building.
The front windward wall looks identical to the single high-rise case as well, where the stagnation point followed
by the downwash is clearly detected. Likewise, the position of the stagnation point is identical to the single high-
rise building.
The windward wall on the rearmost building is quite interesting to investigate, as the streamlines are occurring
in different directions, which are not clear or visible when assessing the pressure distribution of the surface. In
between the two high-rise buildings two standing vortices are occurring, why horizontal streamlines are visible
at both models with a remarkable separation zone in the middle. The separation line is oblique in opposite
directions in the CFD and wind tunnel model, which indicates that only small angles are deciding the mean
direction of the separation line. The vortices above the front high-rise building are affecting the uppermost part
of the rearmost windward wall, why vertically downward directed streamlines are occurring. The streamlines
from the top are met with the vortices from the ground, why they collide at the mid part of the surface creating
horizontal streamlines. The two models are surprisingly similar, in spite of the direction of the oblique separation
line.
The streamlines of the sidewalls on the front building are similar to the single-high-rise case, why references are
made to Section 6.3.1.4. The streamlines of the sidewalls on the rearmost building are different when studying the
oil paint model, as the left sidewall is almost only indicating a parallel wind flow where the right sidewall is
showing arc shaped separation lines similar to the streamlines of the CFD model. The arc shaped separation line
at the oil paint model is occurring due to the wind flows occurring at the rearmost windward wall, as the oblique
separation line meet the arc shaped separation line at the edge in the same height.
The leeward wall of the front building is affected of an upward directed wind flow, where the separation line is
twisted, due to the two standing vortices in between the two high-rise buildings. This phenomenon is seen at
both models. The windward wall of the rearmost building is likewise affected of an upward directed wind flow,
but here the wind separation line is straight and similar to the leeward wall of the single high-rise case.

The wind flow around and on a single high-rise building are known wind phenomena which can be explained by
theory, discussed in Section 2.1.2 and Figure 2.5. The wind flow around a twin arrangement is on the contrary
difficult to describe with theory, as the wind phenomena change with the influence of the distance between the
buildings and the shape of the buildings. However, a wide range of the wind phenomena apparent in Figure 2.5
are occurring at the twin arrangement of the high-rise buildings as well. The specific wind phenomena are
marked with numbers at the oil paint model in Figure 6.17 and at the CFD model showing the velocity
streamlines in Figure 6.18.
The stagnation point at the ground in the influenced area is seen as number 7 at both models. From the
stagnation point the streamlines are separated around the building, shown as number 2, and meet the horseshoe
vortex in the vicinity of the front building, indicated as number 6. The speed up around the corners is seen as
number 8, where an increase in the magnitude of the velocity is detected at the CFD model. The board jet stream
is shown as number 9, indicating the outer bound of the influence area of the high-rise buildings. The fast small
rotating vortices, marked with number 16, are likewise for the single high-rise case only observed at the oil paint
model.

The stagnation point of the windward wall is seen at the front building as number 14 and is discussed in Section
6.3.2.4. From the stagnation point the airstream above the front building is indicated with number 3, and the
horizontal air streams around the high-rise building are indicated with number 4. The upstream airflow at the
wake of the rearmost building is seen as number 15.
The horizontal standing vortices at the wake of the rearmost building are indicated with number 13. The
stagnation point at the wake of the building (number 11) is clearly seen at both models, as a dark area of oil paint
at the oil paint model and as an area where the streamlines are separating at the CFD model. The reversed air
stream at the wake is seen as number 10 in between the two standing vortices. The airstream behind the
stagnation point at the wake is seen as number 12.
The airstream in between the two buildings is discussed in Section 6.3.2.4, where it was found that the separation
line seen at the windward wall of the rearmost building is created due to standing vortices in between the two
buildings, which are consistent to the streamlines visible at the ground, where number 13 is indicating the
horizontal standing vortices. In between the two buildings, a vertical standing vortex will occur as well, creating
the vertical streamlines at both surfaces.
The airflow around the twin arrangement is seen in a mid-plane of the CFD simulation in Figure 6.19. The
stagnation point at the windward wall of the front building is clearly visible as number 14, from where the
airstream is directed downward as the downwash on the windward wall (number 5) and enters into the horseshoe
vortex, indicated with number 6. The upward directed air stream from the stagnation point is seen as number 3,
creating a speed up of the airflow above the front high-rise building. The airstream enters into the vertical vortex
in between the two high-rise buildings, indicated with number 17, which induces upward directed wind flow at
the leeward wall of the front building, marked with number 15. The vertical standing vortex behind the
rearmost building is indicated with number 17. The stagnation point visible at the ground at the wake of the
high-rise buildings is marked with number 11, where the reversed wind flow in between the two standing
horizontal vortices are seen as number 10. The airflow behind the stagnation point is indicated with number 12
and the upward directed wind flow at the leeward wall of the rearmost building is indicated with number 15,
which is occurring due to the reversed wind from the stagnation point (number 10).

Chapter 6 – Computational fluid dynamics

6.4 Validation of CFD simulations
When using CFD simulations for the determination of mean pressures and visualisation of streamlines, it is
necessary to consider if the simulation provides credible results. The validation of the CFD simulations can be
examined by comparing the results from the numerical simulation to those from wind tunnel simulation.
However, one can ponder which simulation to trust, when the comparison deviates, as both test methods each
show inexplicable phenomena. One consideration, when comparing the two simulations, is that the CFD model
has an infinite numbers of ‘measuring points’, whereas the pressure model only provides measurements at a
fairly modest number of pressure taps, why some information might get lost
Both CFD simulations of the two configurations are showing consistence in the underestimations of the suction
areas, which substantiate the fact, that RANS models are known to be poor at dissolving vortices at the wake,
resulting in underestimation of the negative pressure. Conversely, it is seen that almost all pressure areas are
overestimated, which are difficult to describe why.
In spite of the significant deviation of the wind pressure, the pressure distributions of the surfaces on the CFD
model are quite consistent with the wind tunnel test, as the changes in surface pressures are more similar to the
wind tunnel tests than to the theories.
The CFD simulations of the surfaces streamlines are showing surprisingly similar results to the oil painting
models, where only very small deviations are seen for both for the single high-rise and the twin arrangement.
The conclusion of the simulations are clear, as it is evident the simulations only can be used as guidelines for the
pressure magnitudes and the pressure distribution on the surfaces, based on the quite significant deviations from
the wind tunnel test, which is further elaborated in the discussion. However the airflow around and on the
surfaces are showing quite consistent estimations, why these can be used for evaluation of the surrounding
airstream phenomena.
==PAGE:203==
- 187 -
DISCUSSION
In the following section, the elements in the analysis that have given rise to ponder and reflections are discussed.
A discussion of the quality of the measured and corrected data is performed to evaluate if the data and correction
methods are leading to reliable results. Furthermore a discussion of the three methods used to determine the
static equivalent loads is included to argue the advantages and disadvantages of the three methods, followed by
considerations of an optimisation of the load combinations. Lastly, interesting subjects for further studies are
represented and discussed.
Validation of measured data
When a wind tunnel test is executed, it is of great importance to build up a proper boundary layer reflecting the
atmospheric boundary layer in nature. The measured wind profile and turbulence intensity profile are
investigated in Section 5.1.1.1, where it is observed that the slope of both profiles do not quite match any theories.
Due to the inconsistence of the atmospheric boundary layer profiles, it can be discussed if the flow-processing
unit in the wind tunnel is constructed properly to generate the desired atmospheric boundary layer, since the
build-up boundary layer flow is too turbulent with an almost constant shape. The discussion rises to the extent
of impact these profiles induce in the measured data. As seen in Figure 5.1 the lowest 170 mm of the wind profile
is affected by a stronger wind velocity than theory states, which implies larger magnitudes close to the ground.
However, as the lowest pressure point at the surface pressure model is situated in a height of 109 mm, the extent
of impact can be pondered. The far too turbulent flow might induce large fluctuations in the measured data at
areas where strong turbulence normally does not occur, which might give rise to phenomena reflecting other
terrain categories, thus create an inconsistent picture of the expected terrain category.
Some uncertainties of the surface pressure measurements causes considerations as well, as it is a known fact at
FORCE Technology that the surface pressure system is sensitive and occasionally uncomprehending, mostly due
to the quality of the scanners in the pressure system. In the time series of the measured data, it is observed that
some obscurely high peaks are occurring, compared to the expected peaks from ‘normal’ wind fluctuations. It can
be discussed if the peaks in the time series actually are occurring or if they are occurring due to the sensitiveness
of the scanners. In case of the latter, the peaks should be corrected and thereby reduced. A reduction factor in
both the time domain and frequency domain is available to reduce the obscure peaks, where a mean of the
nearby time steps are applied as substitution for the peak in the time domain, or where a cut-off in the power
spectrum can be applied to remove the high frequencies. However, it can be discussed if the correction of the
peaks results in a more correct time series, as it is somewhat to manipulate with the measured data. On the
contrary, if the peaks actually are occurring, but reduced due to correction-routines, it can result in substantial
underestimation of the 78%-fractile in the extreme value analysis.
When assessing the pressure coefficients it became evident that 18 channels at scanner 3 were damaged. Due to
the lack of 18 time series, it is necessary to deliberate different methods to conduct artificial time series as
substitutions. The most cumbersome method is to replace the damaged time series with a new surmised
stochastic process, whereas more straightforward methods are either to copy the time series from a neighbouring
pressure tap, or to create an artificial time series from the mean value of each time step between nearby pressure
taps. However, when using the last-mentioned method, a source of error will supervene since the artificial time
series is a mean value of two time series, thus the peaks of the artificial time series will never contain peaks in the
same magnitude as the nearby time series and thereby imply lower peak values. The choice of method is a
weighting of what is desired or most reasonable; copied pressure areas or pressure areas with lower peaks.
However, no matter what, all methods will be a result of ‘as good as it gets’, as the best improvement naturally
would be a conduction of the wind tunnel tests without scanner 3.
==PAGE:204==
Discussion
- 188 -
Modal analysis model
The choice of modal analysis model can have great impact on the final results of the responses. In general the
quality of the model is proportional with the refinement of the model. This implies the most reliable results of
the floor-by-floor loadings are obtained by a full 3D finite element model in a FEM programme, constructed as a
replica of the full scale elements in the structure, entailing almost an infinite number of degrees of freedom and
nodes. However, as this procedure is far too time consuming and not always necessary, realistic forces can be
obtained by simplified models as well. By choosing the analytical model or the stick FEM model, the final floor-
by-floor loadings would be more doubtful as the accuracy increases with the number of elements, as it reflects
reality more. The frame FEM model could be optimised as well, but a question of how much extra information it
would contribute should always be considered.
Results
Noticeable deviations of the peak accelerations in the frequency domain are observed, as the variance of the three
runs in each of the 36 directions often resulted in large differences in the read off from the spectrums to obtain
the spectral ordinate, why the choice of how to determine the resulting peak acceleration of the three runs can be
discussed. By choosing a mean value of the three runs, the resulting peak acceleration results in a lower
magnitude than the for the peak accelerations in the time domain, whereas choosing the maximum occurrence
of the peaks, the peak acceleration results in a higher magnitude than for the time domain. The most reliable
result would be obtained, if a larger sampling of runs were conducted, from where a statistical magnitude of the
peaks could be obtained.
The resulting static equivalent loads determined by the component-dividing-method in both domains results in
only positive values, as the direction of the loads is lost in the determination. The full solution in the time
domain has in this context an advantage, as the information about the magnitude of the forces in the different
directions is available as minimum and maximum values. It is evident that the full solution in the time domain
gives the most reliable results, however, it can be very time consuming to obtain the responses in the time
domain due to computational power, why the frequency method is most often preferred as the information of
the cumbersome and time consuming time series is neglected. However the choice of method is a weighting
between getting fast average results and optimisation of the resulting forces.
The load combinations are conducted by the full solution of the time series to optimise the magnitude of the
resulting floor-by-floor loadings. However an additional optimisation of the load combinations could be
conducted, where a resulting force time series from the x- y- and ߮-direction could be found to investigate where
and when the peaks are interacting. Though, this procedure would be extremely time consuming and result in a
large amount of load combinations.
The determination of the load combinations for the twin arrangement can be questioned. The load combinations
are performed in the same manner as for the single high-rise building, where the floor-by-floor loadings ܨ௫, ܨ௬
and ܯ௭, are determined for the worst case of wind direction, resulting in the structural elements being designed
for the worst case of the floor-by-floor loadings in both positive and negative direction. However, a question rises
if the load combinations can be optimised, as the wind direction from where the interference building is fronting
the windward direction implies that the principal building is sheltered. By implementing one or more load
combinations, taking the interference effects between the two buildings into account, the structural elements can
be designed for the actual loads that affect the building, thus lower magnitudes in the load combinations can be
obtained. However, this would enforce the structural designers to apply more than 24 load combinations, which
can be discussed if is of interest.
==PAGE:205==
Discussion
- 189 -
CFD simulations
Since CFD simulations are more widely used than ever before, it is relevant to compare the results from the wind
tunnel tests to CFD simulations. The CFD simulations showed obscurely suction areas at the edges of the
windward wall, which can be pondered if they actually occur. Due to the fact that the surface pressure model in
the wind tunnel only are provided with 51 pressure taps at each wall and the CFD simulation with an infinite
number of elements, it is difficult to evaluate which of the models are showing the correct pressures. The suction
areas are not shown in the theories of the pressure coefficients, which indicate that the peaks in the CFD
simulations can be processed as an error-source. But again, since the theories are evaluated from full scale or wind
tunnel testing techniques similar to the high-rise building, it can be questioned if the small areas even were
possible to detect with the provided equipment used for the experiments in the theories. However, the discussion
is a merely a matter of understanding the wind flow around a bluff body, since the small suction areas have no
effect on the overall picture of the pressures on the high-rise models.
It is a known fact that RANS solving models are not capable of dissolving the vortices at the wake, why most
suction areas are underestimated. Furthermore, a RANS model is not sufficient to produce design wind loads, as
this requires peak loads which are not obtainable by the averaged values provided in a RANS model, why a LES
or DNS model should be used for this purpose. At last it can be discussed if the pressures observed by a wind
tunnel tests are much more reliable than a LES or DNS simulation, as a wide range of uncertainties can be
mentioned for the wind tunnel tests as well, where the sensitiveness of scanners, uncomprehending zero
measurements, damaged channels and obscure peaks are among the relatively large uncertainties, which wind
engineers are dealing with when investigating wind loads in wind tunnels. At present, wind tunnel tests are
without doubt more reliable than CFD simulations when determining the aerodynamic structural wind-induced
loads, but it can be discussed if CFD simulations will become as reliable as wind tunnel tests and thereby replace
the physical scaled models in the nearest future.
Further studies
During the analysis of the high-rise buildings, it has been considered to implement further studies, but as the
principle to perform the investigations are similar to the present calculations and due to lack of time, they have
not been included. However, the results could give extra useful information for optimising the present analysis
of the high-rise building.
As mentioned in Section 1.3.2.2, it can be favourable to investigate the high-rise building for a set of different
damping ratios, as it is difficult to determine the structural damping before the final structure has been executed.
By analysing the high-rise building for more damping ratios, more scenarios are accounted for, which is
beneficial for a structural designer, as a higher damping ratio can lead to optimisation of the structural loads.
Furthermore, it could be interesting to investigate the high-rise building for a modal damping ratio, equal in all
modes, compared to Rayleigh damping, to see the impact of the two damping approaches.
Normally when a building is investigated for the purpose of structural design, a storm event with a return period
of 50 years is used in Europe. However, it can be interesting to investigate the high-rise building for different
velocity scenarios, where the wind load are scaled to return period of 5, 10 or 20 years to observe the changes of
the peak accelerations and static equivalent loads as different return periods might be useful in the
differentiating between safety and serviceability structural design.
Since no information about position and direction is available on the high-rise buildings, the determination of
the floor-by-floor loadings and thereby the load combinations are performed by the “Non-Directional Method”,
which is relatively conservative, as explained in Section 4.3. Other methods to determine the floor-by-floor
loadings are available, where an implementation of a directional factor and seasonal factor in the determination
of the mean reference wind speed is applied to obtain lower wind velocities and thereby lower resulting load
distributions from the different directions. This would also imply an optimisation of the floor-by-floor loadings
and resulting load combinations.
==PAGE:206==
==PAGE:207==
- 191 -
CONCLUSION
The scope of the present thesis was to increase the knowledge of how to investigate high-rise buildings exposed to
severe wind loads, and thereby to produce a set of floor-by-floor loadings with appertaining load combinations,
interpretable for a structural designer.
Throughout the present thesis different methods for wind load investigation have been applied to examine the
differences the methods imply. The dynamic properties of the structure were investigated for both a linear and
discrete system. The peak accelerations and static equivalent loads were determined by methods in both the
frequency domain and the time domain. Lastly, the wind loads and wind phenomena from the wind tunnel tests
were compared to a Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) model, to investigate the quality of the future
‘numerical wind tunnel’.
In Chapter 1 a brief description of how high-rise buildings can be designed was given, where it was clear that an
outrigger + core system with super columns as a structural design method is highly desirable, as the stiffness of
the structure becomes more than doubled with this design system.
Furthermore a description of the modal analysis of an analytical and discrete system was given, where the high-
rise building was analysed for both a uniformly distributed cantilever beam and for two finite element method
(FEM) models, where a ‘core + outrigger system’ FEM model was used for the transverse motions and a ‘stick’
FEM model for torsional motions.
It was found that the fundamental sway mode of the analytical and discrete model resulted in identical
eigenfrequencies, where the remaining modes for the discrete model clearly showed lower magnitudes than the
analytical model. Since lower frequencies give rise to higher responses and the resemblance to the actual
structure was more pronounced in the discrete models, the analytical model was neglected in the further
investigations.
In Chapter 2, a description of the calculation routines in Eurocode and Architectural Institute of Japan (AIJ) was
stated, together with the theoretical expressions represented by Holmes, to investigate the differences in the
shape of the mean wind velocity profile and the turbulence intensity profile. It was found that the four profiles
for a suburban terrain were quite consistent, where the Power law provided the highest magnitude of the mean
reference wind velocity. Furthermore, the resulting drag force and overturning moment were determined by
formulas from Eurocode, to investigate the coherence to the resulting forces determined from the wind tunnel
test. At last it was chosen to scale the wind tunnel test measurements with relaxed similarities, known as free
scaling, due to the latitude of choosing any velocity to run the wind tunnel tests at.
In Chapter 3 a description of the practical exposition of the wind tunnel tests was given, together with the
adjustment procedures of the recorded data. It is a known fact at FORCE Technology that the scanners in the
pressure system are extremely sensitive, which was substantiated in the assessment of the time series. It was
observed that obscure peaks occurred in the time series, thus it was concluded that the peaks existed only due to
uncertainties in the equipment. Furthermore, when the pressure distribution of the surfaces was investigated, it
was observed that 18 channels at one of the scanners were damaged, why artificial time series were created to
accommodate for the missing information at the damaged channels.
In Chapter 5 the full analyses of both the preliminary tests and the surface pressure model was conducted. It was
found that the measured atmospheric boundary layer profile in the wind tunnel did not match any theories
close to the ground and up to a range of 75 m. However from 75 m and up to the gradient height, the profile
matched almost flawlessly the Power law wind profile for a suburban terrain category, why the reference mean
==PAGE:208==
Conclusion
- 192 -
wind velocity was determined by the Power law to 42.53 m/s at the top of the building. Furthermore the
measured turbulence intensity profile in the wind tunnel was evaluated, where it correspondingly to the wind
profile became evident that the profile did not match any theories, since it was seen that the standard deviation
of the wind speed along the height did not deviate. Due to the result of the profiles, it was concluded that the
flow-processing unit in the wind tunnel does not build up a satisfactory boundary layer. In spite of the
inconsistences with theory, it was found the surface pressure measurements did not indicate any inexplicable
results.
The three investigations of the drag coefficients, the mean average pressure coefficients and the pressure
distribution of the surfaces, respectively, were conducted for both the single high-rise building and the twin
arrangement of high-rise buildings, and compared to the theories of ESDU, Holmes and Yu, Xie, Zhu and Gu.
Since the high-rise building is double symmetric, it was generally seen that the single high-rise building matched
the theories of the three investigations quite well, and reiterated for every 0-90 degrees of wind angle attack,
verifying the symmetry of the building. In the three assessments of the twin arrangement of high-rise buildings,
it was generally seen that the twin arrangement was affected of interference effects. At a wind direction of 0
degrees the interference building was fronting the wind direction, which resulted in shielding effects of the
principal building. At 90 and 270 degrees the two buildings were parallel, where the interference building
induced channelling effects.
The peak accelerations at the top of the high-rise building were conducted in both the frequency domain and the
time domain. It was observed the accelerations in the cross-wind direction were dominating, due to large
fluctuations induced by vortex shedding. Large uncertainties in the frequency domain were observed when the
spectral ordinates were read-off from the spectrums, evidently resulting in overestimations for both the single
high-rise building and the twin arrangement. It was observed that the peak accelerations of the single high-rise
building were repeated for every 0-90 degrees, and only for every 0-180 degrees for the twin arrangement.
Furthermore it was detected that the twin arrangement was affected of shielding effects at 0 degrees and
channelling effects with its peaks at 100 and 260 degrees, which resulted in a strong increment of the peak
accelerations in the cross-wind direction, when the magnitudes were compared to the single high-rise building.
The peak accelerations were obtained for a storm event with a return period of 50 years, where it is of great
importance to check the peak accelerations are not exceeding the threshold values for human comfort for more
than one return period. The peak accelerations were scaled to return periods of 5, 10 and 20 years, where it was
found that the frequency domain did not observe the threshold values for all return periods.
In Chapter 4 a theoretical description of the methods of determining the static equivalent loads was given, being
the component-dividing-method and a full solution in the time domain. The component-dividing-method can
be applied in both the time domain and the frequency domain, where the loads are given in terms of a mean-,
background- and resonant component. The resulting forces with this method imply only positive magnitudes, as
the directions of the forces are lost in the calculations. The full solution of the forces in the time domain requires
more computational power, but gives a more credible result.
The static equivalent loads of the single high-rise building were determined by all three methods, to investigate
the advantages and disadvantages. In general it was found that the three methods gave reasonable similar results,
but when the fluctuating part of the static equivalent loads was investigated, some deviations were observed. It
became evident that the frequency domain implied overestimations at the top of the building (outrigger 4) due
to overestimations of the resonant component, and underestimations at 60  m height (outrigger 1) due to
underestimations of the background component. Furthermore it became evident that the torsional forces in the
frequency domain consequently resulted in underestimations. Due to the uncertainties of the frequency domain,
it was chosen to determine the resulting static equivalent loads by the full solution in the time domain, where
both positive and negative magnitudes of the forces are available.
==PAGE:209==
Conclusion
- 193 -
The floor-by-floor loadings of the high-rise building were determined by the static equivalent loads, where the
peak loads for a specific wind direction for each x-, y- and ߮-direction, used as the design loads, was found from
the overturning base moments. It was found for the single high-rise building that the dominating force in the x-
direction occurred at a wind direction of 180 degrees, for the y-direction it occurred at 270 degrees and for the
torsional direction it occurred at 170 degrees. For the twin arrangement of high-rise buildings the dominating
force in the x-direction was found at a wind angle of attack at 180 degrees, for the y-direction it occurred at 260
degrees and the dominating torsional moment at 90 degrees.
The floor-by-floor loadings were presented for the two transverse directions ܨ௫and ܨ௬ and for the torsional
direction ܯ௭ in two tables with 82 point loads in the 82 floors of the high-rise building and the principal building
in the twin arrangement. It was found that the resulting base forces for x and y in the single high-rise building
were similar, due to the symmetry, where it was observed that the forces in the y-direction for the twin
arrangement were increased due to channelling, and the forces in the x-direction were slightly decreased. The
resulting base forces for the single high-rise were furthermore compared to the estimations by Eurocode, where it
became evident the base forces from Eurocode provided profound underestimations, which also was expected,
due to the fact that Eurocode comes short when determining wind loads at buildings with a height above 200 m.
The appertaining load combinations to the floor-by-floor loadings were conducted from the base moments,
where the dominating force in the above mentioned wind directions was applied as 100%, where the remaining
two forces were read-offs as the percentage of their directions maximum magnitudes. Due to peaks of each of the
three base forces, three overall load combination were obtained, all with a set of 8 combinations within each
maximum for ܨ௫, ܨ௬ and ܯ௭, leading to 24 load combinations in total for both the single high-rise building and
the twin arrangement. To account for the worst case scenario of the two test configurations of the single high-rise
building and the twin arrangement, the floor-by-floor loadings of the two cases should be combined if it is
undefined if the construction of one or two high-rise buildings should be executed. It was found that the single
high-rise case resulted in the largest floor-by-floor loadings in the x-direction, whereas the twin arrangement
resulted in the largest floor-by-floor loadings in both the y-direction and rotational direction.
In Chapter 6 the CFD simulations of the single high-rise building and the twin arrangement were assessed. The
CFD simulations were carried out with a Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) turbulence model, which is a
simulation that only produce mean values, thus the simulations were compared to the mean pressure coefficients
of the surfaces from the wind tunnel tests and the oil paint tests.
When the pressures between the CFD simulations and the wind tunnel models were compared, it became evident
the pressures from the CFD simulations resulted in overestimations of the positive pressures and
underestimations of the negative pressures. Though, in spite of a large differentiation of the pressure magnitudes,
the distributions of the CFD models complemented the distribution of the wind tunnel models quite well.
Furthermore the comparison of the streamlines of the oil paint models and the CFD simulations lead to some
surprisingly good and almost identical results, where only small deviations were observed. As a conclusion on the
CFD simulations, it became evident that a CFD simulation with a RANS solver only can be used as a guideline
for the pressure distributions on the surfaces, whereas the airflow around the high-rise buildings displayed
reliable results, why these can be used for estimations.
